Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent testifies before Senate panel on the U.S. economy — 2/5/2026

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·134:14 ·30.9K views · 2026-02-05
Scott Bessent

TL;DR Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent highlighted the Trump administration's focus on economic growth and affordability, asserting that FSOC has appropriately refocused on economic security, while facing strong challenges from Democratic senators over rising household costs,...

TL;DR

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent highlighted the Trump administration's focus on economic growth and affordability, asserting that FSOC has appropriately refocused on economic security, while facing strong challenges from Democratic senators over rising household costs, manufacturing job losses, and potential conflicts of interest.

Economic Performance and Affordability: Contrasting Views

  • Trump Administration Stance (Secretary Bessent & Allies):
      • Strong Growth & Wages: Cites "parallel prosperity" with strong GDP growth (4.1% average over past three quarters), real wage increases, and CPI falling to 2.1%.
      • Tax Relief: Mentions the "working families tax cut bill," which eliminated taxes on tips and overtime, and increased the child tax credit to $2,250.
      • Affordability Improvements: Notes declining mortgage rates (lowest in three years in January), falling gasoline and egg prices, and an overall decrease in trueflation (observed inflation) below 1%.
      • Blame on Previous Administration: Attributes high inflation and loss of purchasing power (21.5% cumulative CPI under Biden administration) to prior policies.
      • Permitting Reform: Emphasizes its importance as part of a deregulatory agenda to unlock growth and reduce inflation.
  • Democratic Opposition (Ranking Member Warren & Allies):
      • Rising Costs: Argues that Trump's policies have driven up prices for groceries, electricity, healthcare, and housing construction, with grocery prices 2.4% higher in 2025 according to BLS.
      • Manufacturing Decline: Cites a loss of 72,000 manufacturing jobs since "Liberation Day" (April 2025) and reports of U.S. manufacturing retreat due to tariffs.
      • Failing Families vs. Wall Street: Contends that the economic agenda is failing middle-class families while benefiting Wall Street and "billionaire buddies" through tax cuts and reduced bank supervision.
      • Affordability as a Crisis: Dismisses the President's characterization of affordability concerns as a "hoax," emphasizing that families are genuinely struggling.

FSOC's Mandate and Regulatory Focus

  • Refocusing FSOC (Secretary Bessent):
      • Core Mission: FSOC's 2025 annual report prioritizes long-term economic growth and economic security as essential for financial stability, moving away from a "climate agenda" and "regulation by reflex."
      • Household Resilience: Emphasizes strengthening household balance sheets, promoting savings, and access to affordable credit to improve system resilience.
      • Policy Areas: Focuses on:
          • Treasury Markets: Strengthening against future shocks.
          • Cybersecurity: Protecting the financial system from sophisticated attacks.
          • Regulatory Modernization: Addressing material risks and reducing unnecessary burdens, especially for community banks.
          • Artificial Intelligence (AI): Prioritizing responsible use and monitoring emerging risks, potentially through an AI sandbox.
      • Critique of Past Approach: Secretary Bessent stated previous FSOC approaches treated "nearly every single sector... as a potential financial stability vulnerability," leading to "regulatory myopia" and the second, third, and fourth largest bank failures under the Biden administration.
  • Concerns over Deregulation (Ranking Member Warren & Allies):
      • Weakened Safeguards: Accuses FSOC of "getting rid of guard rails" and making future taxpayer bailouts more likely by ignoring risks like the private credit market.
      • Housing Market: Expresses concern about housing-related risks, citing a 2024 FSOC report on non-bank mortgage servicers, rising foreclosures (up 21%), and four-year highs in mortgage delinquencies.
      • Delayed CDFI Funding: Criticizes the OMB for withholding congressionally appropriated funds for Community Development Financial Institutions (CDFIs) for 11 months, impacting underserved communities.

Key Policy Initiatives and Industry Impacts

  • Community Banks & Deposit Insurance:
      • Decline of Small Banks: Notes a 50% decline in community banks since 2010, arguing that overregulation has made them "too small to succeed."
      • Targeted Deposit Insurance: Supports bipartisan legislation to increase deposit insurance limits for non-interest-bearing transaction accounts (like payroll accounts) to prevent deposit flight during crises and support Main Street lending.
  • Digital Assets & Dollar Dominance:
      • Genius Act (Stablecoins): Highlights the Genius Act as a critical framework for expanding dollar dominance, increasing global demand for U.S. treasuries, and fostering a "safe, sound, and smart" U.S. private-sector digital asset market.
      • Clear Rules: Advocates for clear rules of the road and market structure legislation to embed the industry in the U.S. economy, contrasting with Central Bank Digital Currencies.
      • Taxation: Acknowledges the complexity of digital asset taxation, particularly regarding de minimis rules for capital gains on assets used as a means of exchange.
  • Credit Card Interest Rate Caps:
      • President's Proposal: Mentions President Trump's call for a one-year cap of 10% on credit card interest rates, which Secretary Bessent supports.
      • Potential Impacts: Discussed potential impacts on asset-backed securities and credit card issuers, with a focus on encouraging competition on rates rather than rewards.
  • "Trump Accounts":
      • Wealth Building: A new program created by the working families tax cuts, providing a $1,000 seed investment for newborns to expose 38% of American households to the U.S. equity market and foster financial literacy.

Transparency, Accountability, and Conflicts of Interest

  • IRS Enforcement:
      • Increased Activity: Secretary Bessent reports identifying $10 billion in tax crimes (up 15% from 2024) and $4.5 billion in tax fraud (up 110%).
      • Democratic Criticism: Accused of "gutting enforcement" and "dismantling the DOJ."
  • President Trump's Lawsuits Against Treasury/IRS:
      • Legal Scrutiny: Senators questioned the $10 billion lawsuit against Treasury/IRS and claims seeking $230 million from the Justice Department, raising concerns about potential conflicts of interest.
      • Secretary's Role: Secretary Bessent clarified that these are Justice Department matters, Treasury acts as "paymaster," and he has no independent decision-making authority in these cases. He noted the President's stated intention to donate proceeds to charity.
  • Other Concerns:
      • Personal Credibility: Secretary Bessent was pressed on past statements regarding a shooting incident and comments on inflation and tariffs.
      • Russia Sanctions & Jared Kushner: Questioned on the administration's Russia sanctions policy and the unofficial role of Jared Kushner in recent talks.
      • Binance Pardon: Secretary Bessent stated he was unfamiliar with a pardon for Binance founder Zhao or any related donations to Trump family businesses.

Notable Quotes

"Under your leadership, FSOC has rightly recognized that long-term economic growth and economic security are core components of financial stability. A system that slows growth, limits opportunity, or prices families out of basic financial services is not a stable system. It's a fragile one." — Chairman Tim Scott "President Trump's economic agenda is failing families, but boy, it is working great for Wall Street and Trump's billionaire buddies." — Ranking Member Elizabeth Warren "Promoting economic growth and economic security is essential to ensuring financial stability. Economic growth strengthens households, business, and financial institution balance sheets, creating capital buffers that reduce the risk of defaults and financial stress." — Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent "We are going to see a choice between American private sector assets with our best practices and regulation or between central bank digital assets whether in Europe whether in China in the rest of the world and I think the world is going to choose the US dollar and the private sector well-regulated choice that US stable coins will provide." — Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent
Good morning, and thank you, Secretary, for being with us today. Before I start my prepared comments, I will say that so often we have staff members who are able to move forward and move out, and so often they tell me that the best job they've ever had is working for my office, and then they leave. Shay Hawkins, one of my staffers that I just spoke to yesterday, has recently taken a job with Treasury, and he said he's so sad to go. I said, "Well, you can stay." He said, "No, I'm not that sad to stay." So, welcome new members of your staff from my staff; they're looking forward to working with you and working for you. As chairman of the banking committee, I'm committed to enacting pro-growth, pro-affordability policies. From the Genius Act to the Road to Housing, I am proud of the bipartisan work this committee has done to promote economic growth and affordability. As we build on that progress, it's important to remember that affordability is not an abstract concept. It's about whether families can afford a home, save for the future, and feel confident that hard work still pays off in America. Today, I want to focus on the gains we have made in affordability, growth, and how they fit together with financial stability. For many Americans, including families and small businesses at home in South Carolina—one of the fastest growing states, I might add, in the country—these issues are not hypothetical. They shape everyday decisions about work, housing, and opportunity. Secretary Bessant, as you pointed out in your FSOC 2025 report, economic growth and economic security are critical to financial stability. As a fellow Charlestonian, a fellow South Carolinian, you understand firsthand how economic growth, population growth, and opportunity intersect at the community level. FSOC was created to identify and respond to systemic risk. But for far too long, FSOC has focused on a climate agenda that damaged financial stability. And as you pointed out, Secretary Bessant, it treated nearly every single sector of the economy, financial market, and major financial institution as a potential financial stability vulnerability. Under your leadership, FSOC has rightly recognized that long-term economic growth and economic security are core components of financial stability. A system that slows growth, limits opportunity, or prices families out of basic financial services is not a stable system; it's a fragile one. Thank you for refocusing FSOC on household balance sheets and household financial resilience. When families have savings, access to affordable credit, and confidence in their financial future, they are better equipped to weather storms, weather economic shocks, and the system becomes more resilient. Strong households also strengthen local communities, and strong communities contribute to a more resilient national economy. That's why I'm interested in how FSOC balances systemic risk and its oversight with the need for economic growth, innovation, and affordability. Reassessing bank regulatory and supervisory frameworks to remove undue burdens isn't about weakening safeguards; it's about making sure the financial system works as intended for the people it serves. Coordination matters, too. Businesses should not be caught in the crossfire of conflicting rules, duplicative supervision, or regulatory whiplash. When regulators fail to coordinate, the consequences don't stay in Washington; they show up in higher costs, fewer choices, and less access at the kitchen table. Finally, I want to recognize Treasury's work implementing the Genius Act. This framework has the potential to expand dollar dominance, increase global demand for dollar-denominated assets like US treasuries, reduce systemic risk, and support economic growth and innovation. At the end of the day, affordability, growth, and financial stability must move forward together for families and small businesses across the country, including back home in South Carolina. That means an economy that is growing, a financial system that is resilient, and policies that keep opportunity within reach. I am confident that our economy will continue to grow, and our financial system will remain resilient. Thank you, Mr. Secretary, for being here, and I look forward to your testimony. Ranking Member Warren, you're now recognized for your opening statement. Thank you, Mr. Chairman. Secretary Bessant, as one of President Trump's top economic advisers, you have a lot to answer for. Donald Trump campaigned on lowering costs on day one. Those were his words over and over. But 381 days into his term, Trump's economic policies have driven prices up. Grocery prices are up, electricity prices are up, healthcare prices are up, the cost of building housing is up. President Trump's economic agenda is failing families, but it is working great for Wall Street and Trump's billionaire buddies. There have been massive tax cuts for billionaires. Big bank supervision has been cut to the bone and sidelining the cops who go after white-collar crime. Meanwhile, middle-class families get the short end of the stick. Unemployment is growing and prices are up. But American families can't even get the basic respect of a straight answer from Team Trump. The president lies about prices being lower when every family who's walked into a grocery store knows that prices are up. Secretary Bessant tells workers that the Trump administration has created a blue-collar boom, while Donald Trump's own Labor Department reports that manufacturing jobs are down by 71,000 since Trump took office. But American families aren't fooled when Secretary Bessant and President Trump try to tell them that the sky is not blue and that water is not wet. Hard-working people know in their pocketbooks just how big those lies are. They also know that Trump and his team don't have any plans to fix what is broken. Secretary Bessant is here today in his role as chair of the Financial Stability Oversight Council. Congress created FSOC after the 2008 financial crash to protect families from large financial institutions that load up on debt and then turn around and crash our economy. Secretary Bessant could have used FSOC over the last year to tighten regulations so that we don't face more bank failures like the Silicon Valley Bank or First Republic Bank that crashed less than three years ago. Instead, he has helped to get rid of the guard rails so that banks can load up on even more risk. He's boosted profitability for giant banks and pay for CEOs while making it more likely that all of this ends in yet another taxpayer bailout. And what has he done for consumers? Secretary Bessant tried to shut down the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau, the agency that has returned more than $21 billion directly to Americans cheated by big banks and giant corporations. He's effectively told the banks, "They can cheat as many people as they want, and he and Donald Trump will look the other way." But it's not just about rolling back safeguards. Secretary Bessant is ignoring emergency risks in the economy. The private credit market looks like a ticking time bomb. Late last year, Moody's found that banks had $300 billion of exposure, and that the risk posed by these shadowy holdings is growing. And as Jamie Dimon said, "When you see one cockroach, there are probably more." But Secretary Bessant and FSOC have their eyes shut tight and have decided they don't see any cockroaches at all. Here's all you need to know about what's happening under President Trump's and Secretary Bessant's watch. Middle-class families are paying higher prices for just about everything while it becomes harder and harder to get a raise or find a job. Meanwhile, Wall Street CEOs are getting the kid glove treatment as Trump policies may be edging the economy closer and closer to another disastrous financial crash. President Trump is so busy raking in cash from foreign governments and building his golden-encrusted ballroom that he's completely out of touch with how his economy is making life harder for millions of Americans. We are only one year into Donald Trump's second term. And if Trump and Bessant don't wake up and change course quickly, the economy may keep delivering for billionaires, but it's going to get even worse for American families. Thank you, Mr. Chairman. Secretary Bessant, good Chairman Scott, Ranking Member Warren, and members of the committee. Thank you for inviting me to discuss the Financial Stability Oversight Council's 2025 annual report. This report is the culmination of extensive collaboration among FSOC members. I'd like to thank them for their hard work and dedication in advancing the President's bold vision for a better America. Since day one, President Trump has focused on building parallel prosperity, an era of economic expansion where Wall Street and Main Street grow together. To that end, Treasury has tirelessly pursued pro-growth policies to unlock the potential available to all Americans when they are free to save, invest, build businesses, and drive their own economic destinies. The Financial Stability Oversight Council plays an important role in delivering on this agenda. Too often in the past, we have seen regulation by reflex. Rather than preempting crises, regulators have frequently reacted to them after the fact. They played the role of hazmat cleanup team instead of preventing dangerous spillovers in the first place. Regulation by reflex has led to a regulatory myopia that has undermined safety and soundness. Under President Biden, the bank regulators preoccupied themselves with reputation risk, now discredited climate-related financial risk, and other risks with no clear nexus to safety and soundness. At the same time, they centered supervision on management and other governance matters that distracted examiners and bank risk managers from the real risks to safety and soundness. The result, predictably, was the second, third, and fourth largest bank failures in US history under his administration. Besides undermining safety and soundness, regulation by reflex has driven excessive regulation that can lead to economic stagnation, and economic stagnation is itself a threat to financial stability. In calibrating regulations, federal agencies must avoid the temptation to create a zero-risk financial system which would result in what others have called the stability of the graveyard. FSOC should aim to identify vulnerabilities that could lead to systemic crises and encourage the private sector to mitigate those risks before recommending additional regulation. FSOC should also work with its members to support efforts to avoid or pare back existing regulation that stifles pro-growth lending, capital formation, and innovation. The best way to achieve these goals is by centering economic growth and economic security at the heart of FSOC's agenda. Promoting economic growth and economic security is essential to ensuring financial stability. Economic growth strengthens households, businesses, and financial institution balance sheets, creating capital buffers that reduce the risk of defaults and financial stress, and economic security reinforces domestic production capacity, raising living standards while reducing vulnerability to external shocks and supply chain disruptions. FSOC's annual report prioritizes economic growth and economic security accordingly, with a specific focus on our four policy areas: Treasury markets, cybersecurity, regulatory modernization, and AI. First, the council is ensuring that the US Treasury market remains the deepest and most liquid in the world. The council is supporting efforts by member agencies to strengthen this market against future shocks, including through the interagency working group on Treasury market surveillance and the market resilience working group. Ongoing monitoring and targeted reforms by individual agencies remain essential to financial stability. Second, the council is taking action to protect our financial system from increasingly sophisticated cyber attacks. Nation-state actors and criminal groups continue to target our financial institutions and critical infrastructure. To address this risk, the council is supporting expanded information sharing, joint monitoring, and scenario-based exercises, and it is emphasizing the need for regulated firms to manage cyber risk tied to third-party service providers. Third, the council is committed to supporting efforts to modernize supervisory and regulatory frameworks for banks and credit unions. Going forward, regulation and supervision should address material risk, enhance transparency, and reduce unnecessary burdens, particularly for community banks. Fourth, the council is prioritizing the responsible use of artificial intelligence to strengthen financial stability. The council is working with public and private sector partners, including international counterparts, to enhance system resilience while closely monitoring emerging risks. I will close by highlighting our progress and enhancing the utility of our annual report. In this year's report, FSOC shifted away from its past approach, where nearly every major market and financial sector was described as a financial stability vulnerability, by introducing a new structure centered on fostering economic growth and security. We are tuning out the white noise to concentrate on the issues that matter most for US financial stability. With this overview, I look forward to taking your questions. Thank you, Secretary. To all the members, we have essentially full attendance, and it looks like everyone wants to ask a question. So, I will be pretty consistent on calling us to five minutes. I just want to make sure that you have a little time to prepare for that. Okay. Got it. Just so long as it's everybody else. Of course. Yes. Yes. Yes. Yes. We'll work on that. Okay, Secretary, I'd love to give you a couple minutes to respond to some of the things the ranking member said, starting with the fact that two of the three largest bank failures in American history happened under President Biden, not President Trump. Mr. Chairman, during Joe Biden's tenure, CPI was 4.7% on an annualized basis. Cumulative CPI was 21.5%. There was a loss of real purchasing power for working families. And there is an index called Trueflation, which is observed inflation, not measured inflation which comes with a lag, that just fell below 1%. Wow. I only have a minute left. I'm holding everyone to the same standard, including myself. So here's my probably not my last question, but I'm getting close to the end. Number two, when President Trump has been able to keep inflation under 3%, still heading towards our goal of 2%, that is much, much better for our economy, and as a result, interest rates have been able to come down consistently. Yes, we've seen the interest rates come down this year, and the 10-year US Treasury had its best year since 2020 when the bond markets and our G7 colleagues actually had rate hikes. We had rate decreases. I only have 10 seconds. At the end of the day, home ownership and affordability is more likely now in the Trump economy than it ever would have been under the Biden economy. Thank you, Mr. Chairman. For a solid year while he ran for president, Donald Trump promised pretty much every day that he was going to lower costs on day one. On day one, if he was elected, those were his words, not mine. So, Secretary Bessant, you are one of the president's top economic advisers. We're going to move on to the next one. Senator Rounds, the floor is yours. Thank you, Mr. Chairman. Mr. Secretary, first of all, thanks for being here and for your service to our nation. I'm going to come back and talk a little bit on the technical side for just a minute. The FSOC 2025 annual report recommends that its member agencies use the council's Artificial Intelligence Working Group to identify regulatory impediments to the responsible adoption of AI by financial institutions. From your perspective, what are the biggest impediments today that prevent banks from adopting AI responsibly, especially for compliance, fraud detection, and risk management? Well, as you know, AI is an exciting and new technology. And there's a great amount of learning to do here. So FSOC, Treasury, the regulatory agencies are working with our private partners to implement in a gradual way robust usage of AI. AI can be a great tool, but we also have to think that AI can be a risk through state and non-state actors. So it is a public-private partnership, and we are pushing very hard across the agencies and at Treasury. FSOC's report also recognizes that overly complex and subjective supervision, like reliance on vague concepts such as reputational risk, as you mentioned in your opening statement, restricts valuable activities. Given FSOC's push for clearer standards and responsible AI adoption, would a time-limited AI sandbox for financial institutions with clear guard rails help firms test AI tools in a controlled setting while regulators evaluate risks and benefits? Senator, that is clearly one very interesting option, and we are considering that moving forward. We'd be happy to work with your staff on that. I feel very passionate about that part of it. I would look forward to working with your team as well, sir. I'm also just curious. We've talked now, and the discussion will go on and on, about how we build the economy and so forth, but I think just for the American public, since they probably don't get an opportunity to just have a back and forth with the Secretary of the Treasury very often, let's talk a little bit about how we really reduce the price of getting a home for an American family. I think a lot of it has to do with bringing down the interest rates that charge in a 30-year mortgage. Can you share a little bit about what it takes to bring down the price of treasuries with regard to the growth of GDP, and when treasury rates come down, what that does for an average American homebuyer who's looking at a 30-year mortgage? I'd be glad to, Senator, because President Trump is focused on Main Street and not Harvard Square. So, a mortgage rate is two components. We could say that it is derivative of the 10-year, but it's really a seven-and-a-half-year, and we have seen that decline. And then it is the spread between the 10-year bond and the actual mortgage security, and we have seen that is at multi-year lows. So what we saw in January was the lowest mortgage rates in almost three years. We've got $37 trillion in debt. A lot of the folks back out there are saying if you've got that much debt and you're paying interest on it, that competes with folks that want to loan you money on a mortgage. Can you talk about what it means to build GDP and how someone who is looking at laying out bonds and so forth as you build GDP, what that does and how that impacts a homeowner? Yes, because two things, sir. We brought down the fiscal deficit for the calendar year. So from 2024 to 2025. In 2024, the deficit to GDP, which is a very important number, was 6.97%, highest when we weren't at war or in a recession. We brought it down to 5.4%. That was a combination of holding spending and growing the economy. And as we continue to do that, interest rates will naturally decline, and it pushes out inflation. What we could see from an MIT study was that the Democrats' spending was responsible for almost half of the great inflation. The overspending, according to MIT, was responsible for that and penalized American homeowners. So by controlling the budget, growing in a responsible way, we will bring down mortgage rates, and I would say that building costs are now down in the year. Thank you. Thank you, Mr. Chairman. Yes, sir. Senator Reed. Thank you, Mr. Chairman. Mr. Secretary, the president announced on January 9th, in his words, "Effective January 20th, 2026, I, as president of the United States, am calling for a one-year cap on credit card interest rates of 10%." In remarks at the World Economic Forum in Davos, the president further said, "The profit margin for credit card companies now exceeds 50%." He also indicated that Americans are paying 28-30% interest rates. You were one of his top economic advisors, Mr. Secretary. Do you support interest rate caps to improve affordability on credit? I wholeheartedly support President Trump's policy, and I think that what you've just said would be in direct contrast to what Senator Warren said about this administration favoring the big banks, but those are facts. Well, I don't think my statement indicates at all what Senator Warren said or meant. I wanted an answer. You gave me one. But let's just look at the current situation. We already have legislation that protects service members by putting a 36% cap not just on credit cards but all credit relationships, and it protects them from being exploited. As in my youth, I was a company commander and saw it firsthand. Would it make sense as a first step to provide a 36% interest rate for all Americans, not just service members? 36% doesn't seem like much protection to me, Senator. That's why we are more focused, but we are very focused on the mortgage market through Fannie and Freddie and through the non-agency actors. So you're asking if Treasury's position is that this risk was overstated? If everything is a risk, nothing is a risk. I am specifically asking on the issue of housing. I believe part of housing is being able to have tight spreads in the mortgage market, and we are at multi-year lows in the spreads versus Treasuries, which tells me the mortgage market is very healthy. We also had the lowest mortgage rate in the past three years in January. So you're saying the mortgage market is healthy. Foreclosures are up 21%; mortgage delinquencies are at a four-year high. Separately, your 2025 annual report notes that FSOC created a household resilience working group to identify early warning signs of household financial stress. It's my understanding that this working group was created in December. Has it met yet? What is it expected to produce? It's exactly as it seems. It will be meeting monthly. It did not meet last month, and we want to look at the household balance sheets and understand whether it's sentiment or reality, and where the pressure points are, because we want to help working Americans and spot problems early. Do you know what the outcomes are, what it's expected to produce, or could you share that? As it hasn't started yet, Senator, I would look forward to sharing the work product with you as we move through the year. I would hope there is a plan, because I know usually if you set up a group, there's a mission for the working group, a plan, a set of expectations. So I look forward to hearing from you on that. You mentioned in your testimony "parallel prosperity" and you mentioned Main Street and Wall Street. If you were to rate how Wall Street is doing on a scale of 1 to 10, with 10 being it's doing well, and Main Street on a scale of 1 to 10, what would you rate them? I'm not going to speculate, but I will tell you the Wall Street Journal, not the Main Street Journal, criticized me for being a populist on my advocacy of Main Street. Main Street got killed during the Biden administration; Wall Street did not. In my 10 seconds, I want to say two things. Number one, this is now your watch. Number two, families are truly hurting in this very present moment. So I look forward to working with you and finding out about housing particularly, because it's one of the number one issues in this country. Thank you, and I yield back. Thank you, Mr. Secretary, and thank you for being so accessible. Your predecessor was almost invisible; you go out and you're on TV, you're on the media to make certain that the American people are communicated with on a regular basis. That's a refreshing change. I have a question on your many TV appearances: I've noticed that you said if the Democrats win, they'll do it again. Can you clarify what that means? Yes. We saw a spending surge during the Biden administration, blowout spending, and the deficit-to-GDP numbers blowing out, which, according to MIT studies, was responsible for the inflation surge. At the same time, we saw mass unfettered immigration, 10 to 20 million illegal aliens coming into the country and depressing wages. This took low-end housing, and a study from Wharton showed that much of the low-end housing inflation, especially in rents, came from the 20 million new arrivals. The law of supply and demand applies both for housing and for suppressing wages. So I would say when I talk about that, there will be blowout spending, which will lead to inflation, and then the border will be reopened. Clearly, this deportation effort, especially self-deportations, is going to lead to lower housing prices, and we've already seen it. In rental properties ranked A through D, we are seeing a substantial decrease in D and C properties. What we're also seeing is that because of the supply coming on, we are seeing a decrease in rents nationwide. As I said earlier, True Inflation, an agency that uses a basket of gathered real-time inflation measures, is now below 1%. Speaking of that, let's talk about it quickly because there are things that federal policy obviously impacts. An underlying cost to a lot of these key baskets of goods is the price of gasoline. What has happened in the last 12 months? The price of gasoline is down substantially nationwide, and we expect it to continue going down. Also, diesel is an input for farmers and truckers, so energy feeds into everything, whether it is food cost or manufacturing. More supply and being on the side of the energy industry has resulted in lower prices. Eggs are an underlying ingredient in a lot of foods. What has happened to the price of eggs in the last 12 months? The price of eggs is down substantially. The president had to wear that for a few months, and then the price of eggs collapsed thanks to a lot of measures taken under his direction. Of course, we don't hear about egg prices anymore. I will say, in my hometown, Senator Scott's hometown, I've noticed that the egg surcharge has gone off the menu in the diner we both like. But there are some things that the federal government can't control, and we had a conversation about electricity prices. What state would you surmise has the highest cost of kilowatt-hour? California has the highest cost in everything: the highest taxes, the highest homelessness. Believe it or not, Massachusetts wins this one at 31 cents per kilowatt-hour. Ohio's at 17. Wouldn't you agree, Mr. Secretary, that a lot of that is due to state policies, the policies initiated by those states? A huge amount is state policy. Whether it's Kathy Hochul, Governor of New York, blocking a pipeline, or the decision in New York, sitting next to the Marcellus Shale, not to allow gas in and pipelines to New England or down from Canada. New England has made the decision to de-industrialize; even when I was a college student, Connecticut was a hotbed of small manufacturing. From an Ohio perspective, we're happy to provide low-cost energy to New England. Would you be surprised to know that Massachusetts also has twice the core inflation as a national average? I wouldn't, and seeing the statistics for how many people are leaving the state, I understand why. In conclusion, you'd like to see states do more of the things that states like Ohio do and less like the things that states like Massachusetts do. The last thing we want to do is take those policies and make them national. Would you agree? I agree, sir. First, I want to thank Chair Scott and Ranking Member Warren for holding today's hearing. Secretary Besson, thank you for being here today to discuss our economy, which is very much on the minds of Marylanders. Before I get to my questions, I want to make a quick comment on market structure legislation. I speak for many of my colleagues when I say that we really want to get to a good bipartisan bill. We want to get it done, and I've appreciated working with your team both during the GENIUS Act and market structure process. I really appreciate their attention to the issue of stablecoin paying yield and interest. I am confident and feel really good that we are going to get a bipartisan compromise that protects innovation and our community banks, and I really encourage your continued work on that issue. The 2025 FSOC report confirms that 60% of small business loans and 80% of agricultural loans come from community and regional banks. I am very proud to be partnering with Senator Hagerty; he and I have a piece of legislation to increase stability for businesses and their customers by expanding the deposit insurance available for business checking accounts. I joined many Maryland business owners, credit unions, and small banks who believe a targeted expansion of deposit insurance could strengthen confidence in our financial system. The first question everyone wants answered about changes to deposit insurance, and it's a valid one, is who pays for it? I'm committed to working across the aisle to make sure that we get this right. I asked your colleague, FDIC Chairman Travis Hill, about costs and benefits in October. Chairman Hill testified that a targeted expansion of deposit insurance would likely not increase deposit insurance premiums on small banks, and that any increased premiums on large banks would be small or immaterial. Do you agree with Chairman Hill? More broadly, will you explain to the committee why a targeted expansion of deposit insurance can strengthen our banking system and boost business and consumer confidence? Senator, I want to thank you for your partnership on GENIUS and clarity. I am hopeful that we can get that across the line this spring. I've been an outspoken advocate for small banks because they are Main Street. We've seen 50% of our small banks disappear since the Great Financial Crisis, and I believe that is one reason Main Street has lagged. A large part of that is overregulation, but another part of this is moral hazard. During times of financial stress, like in March 2023, there's deposit volatility. Deposits flee banks because people believe that whether it's the three largest or the seven largest banks, there is no risk of loss. But if they stay with community banks—and I've met with personally more than 200 community bankers, spoken to a thousand—the 200 I've met with tell me they could have clients for 50 years, their families had them for a hundred years, and the money goes to Wells Fargo and doesn't come back. This is exactly what you said: because there wasn't one business checking account, normally a payroll account, they say, "I just can't lose this money." Once Wells Fargo gets it, they don't want to give it back; they offer. As you get a decrease in deposits through the fractional banking system, then there is a reverse multiplier for lending, so banks have lower deposits and less lending available for Main Street. I think that you and Senator Hagerty—Senator Hagerty and I co-authored a piece in the Wall Street Journal—this is an extremely important piece of legislation. I would encourage your colleagues to pass it, and anything I can do to advocate for it, please let me know. Thank you. One other question. Mr. Secretary, I want to thank you for your public support for Community Development Financial Institutions. I also echo Senator Warner's concerns about the congressionally appropriated funds being released. Maryland CDFIs include banks and credit unions that provide crucial funding for businesses, housing, and access to capital in underserved communities. I want to specifically mention two CDFIs serving in Maryland that regularly participate in the Banking Enterprise Award program, which allows them to provide credit and financial services in some of the most distressed neighborhoods in Maryland. The White House Office of Management and Budget has withheld the Notice of Funding Availability for the BEA program since March of 2025, which is 11 months. If the notice is not published, CDFI banks cannot apply for funding. In March, you said CDFIs are a key component in supporting Main Street America. Do you still stand by this statement? I am eagerly awaiting it to be released from OMB so that we can move it forward. As soon as it is released, we will follow our statutory duty and release it to CDFIs. I'm also very happy to see CDFIs getting back to a core mission of prosperity and resilience for their community. Secretary Besson, thank you for your leadership and your service to our country. You've talked many times about the ongoing burdens and effects of illegal immigration on our country as a result of the open border policies of the Biden administration. Last year, you said, "If you're here illegally, there's no place for you in our financial system." You also announced that the Treasury Department would propose regulations to cut off federal benefits and tax credits to illegal aliens. I wondered if you could give us a status update on those rules. How can we protect hardworking Americans from illegals who exploit our U.S. financial and credit system, and what are we doing about it? Yes sir. We have moved forward on that. Through the regulators, we've had them double down on "Know Your Client" (KYC) to make sure that everyone within the regulated banking system is legal. The other thing we have found out, and we saw it in Minneapolis as a hotbed of waste, fraud, and abuse, is outside of the banking system in Money Service Businesses. A lot of money is being wired out of the country, and we don't know where. So, this is a vulnerability we've spotted in our system, and we are cracking down on that. We've also cracked down at the southern border because we've found that lots of cartels are using or money laundering there, and we've done that on a targeted basis rather than increasing costs to all banks. Is it your sense that it's working? Yes, sir. And what else can we do? We are considering a re-underwriting of the clients. Many people are familiar with what is called a Suspicious Activity Report (SAR), but we find that many of those come in after the horse has left the barn. So we are encouraging banks to be more proactive, know your client, and re-underwrite. Can you give us an education about why all this matters? How does it affect my dad, a retired factory worker in Fort Wayne, Indiana, regarding the cost of illegal immigrants in our financial and credit system? Give us a lesson on why it matters. Again, it creates instability, and it also creates—as I cited earlier today—a Wharton study shows that it increases rents for working Americans. It takes jobs. So if we look at household resilience for working Americans, they got clobbered because the law of supply and demand dictates that for low-wage earners, as you increase the workforce by 10, 15, or 20 million in a very compressed period, you're going to see wage decreases. Secretary, I wish that my parents would have had access to Trump accounts when I was born and set something like that up for me and my brothers. What a game-changer that can be to help babies and young kids today achieve the American dream who grow up on the other side of the tracks and have an option like a Trump account. I praise you and President Trump in this administration for what you've done to work with Congress to create Trump accounts. One gentleman, Brad Gersonner, a native "Hoosier" like me, grew up from a very humble background and made it big; now he's giving back and funding Trump accounts for Hoosier kids who are born today. I know it's going to make a big difference for the next generation of kids. One thing I worry about, though, are politicians down the road who want to regulate those accounts or put restrictions or rules that prevent those accounts from growing. What can we do to protect this great vision today to make sure that politicians later don't ruin it? I think it's two things: mission creep, that we are committed to the lowest cost index funds, that it does not become something for social engineering, that it is return-based, low-cost, broadly diversified so that everyone owns a share of the U.S. economy. Senator, I think the more that people become invested in these, the smaller the chance for meddling is because I think they will be adamant. Thank you for your leadership. I yield back. For senators who wish to submit questions for the hearing record, those questions are due one week from today, Thursday, February 12th. CEO Fireside Chat With Jensen Huang and Pascal Daloz at Dassault Systèmes 3DEXPERIENCE World

NVIDIA(2.1M) ·37:04 ·38.4K views · 2026-02-05
Jensen Huang Pascal Daloz
NVIDIA and Dassault Systèmes announced a significant long-term strategic partnership, integrating Dassault Systèmes’ Virtual Twin technologies with NVIDIA’s AI and Omniverse platforms to create science-validated "World Models" that will redefine indu...
TL;DR NVIDIA and Dassault Systèmes announced a significant long-term strategic partnership, integrating Dassault Systèmes’ Virtual Twin technologies with NVIDIA’s AI and Omniverse platforms to create science-validated "World Models" that will redefine industrial design,...

TL;DR

NVIDIA and Dassault Systèmes announced a significant long-term strategic partnership, integrating Dassault Systèmes’ Virtual Twin technologies with NVIDIA’s AI and Omniverse platforms to create science-validated "World Models" that will redefine industrial design, manufacturing, and operations through generative AI and real-time simulation.

The Evolving Partnership & Generative AI

  • A Quarter-Century Collaboration: NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang and Dassault Systèmes CEO Pascal Daloz highlighted their long-standing partnership, which began over 25 years ago with OpenGL (precursor to today's RTX) and CGFX (precursor to CUDA), revolutionizing workstation computing.
  • New Computing Platform: The current collaboration marks a "reinvention of the computing platform," scaling Dassault Systèmes' 40-year vision of virtual twins to represent the physical world at a much larger scale through AI.
  • Generative Economy: Industry is shifting from structured design representations (specifying every geometry) to "generative computing models" where AI is foundational.
  • AI as Infrastructure: Jensen Huang emphasized that AI is becoming a fundamental infrastructure, akin to water, electricity, or the internet, powering every industry and country globally.
  • 100% Digital Future: The future of design, simulation, validation, and software integration is projected to be entirely digital, with Dassault Systèmes' VAs (CATIA, SOLIDWORKS, SIMULIA, BIOVIA, DELMIA, ENOVIA) leveraging NVIDIA's technologies.
  • Massive Integration: This is the largest collaboration between the two companies, involving the fusion of NVIDIA CUDA-X acceleration libraries, NVIDIA AI (for physical and agentic AI), and NVIDIA Omniverse (NVIDIA's digital twin platform) into Dassault Systèmes’ offerings.
  • Unprecedented Scale: This integration promises to accelerate computing by 100x, 1000x, and soon up to 1,000,000x, enabling real-time operations for tasks like wind tunnel simulations and robot orchestration.

Revolutionizing Design & Engineering with World Models

  • Inventing the Future: The partnership empowers Dassault Systèmes' community of 45 million users, 400,000 customers, and 15+ million engineers to "invent the future," moving beyond mere automation of the past.
  • The World Model Foundation: A core concept is the "World Model," grounded in biology, physics, and material sciences, which is crucial for computing complex systems, including life itself.
      • Computing Life's Language: This involves learning the language of DNA, proteins, and cells to understand interactions and properties.
      • Generative Biology & Materials: The ability to translate between human and biological languages, generating new proteins for drugs, chemicals, or materials with enhanced properties (e.g., stronger, lighter, more heat-resistant).
      • Case Study (Bell Group): The Bell Group uses virtual twins powered by a biological world model to automatically generate proteins for healthier, more sustainable non-dairy foods, leading to faster innovation and certified decisions.
  • Infinite Possibilities & Physics AI: The virtual twin can explore an "infinite of possibilities" by fusing simulation with AI, using "surrogate and emulation models."
      • NVIDIA Physics Nemo: This physics-aware AI model simulation system works alongside or is trained by principled simulators, predicting physical behavior 10,000 times faster while remaining grounded in the laws of physics.
      • Case Study (Lucid Motors): Lucid Motors embeds crash behavior, aerodynamics, and vehicle performance early in development, allowing engineers to design vehicle *behavior* (not just shape) and certify it.

Software-Defined Factories & Industrialization

  • Virtual Twin Factories: Factories are evolving into hybrid physical-virtual assets, designed, simulated, and operated entirely within virtual twins. This allows for complex simulations of millions of objects, optimizing layouts, sequences, and robot operations.
      • Case Study (Omron): Omron engineers "software-defined factories" where autonomous elements are integrated from day one, resulting in more flexible, resilient, and adaptive production systems.
  • The AI Factory Revolution: AI is driving a new industrial revolution, requiring the rapid scaling of three interconnected industries:
      • Chip Factories: Increasing production of semiconductor and packaging facilities.
      • Computer Factories: Manufacturing supercomputers from these chips.
      • AI Factories: Housing supercomputers to "manufacture" AIs themselves.
  • Largest Industrial Buildout: A gigawatt AI factory can cost approximately $50 billion, contributing to tens of gigawatts being built worldwide – representing the largest industrial infrastructure buildout in human history, potentially worth trillions over the next decade.
  • MBSE for AI Factories: NVIDIA uses Dassault Systèmes' Model-Based Systems Engineering (MBSE) to design, plan, and simulate AI factories (including data centers, supercomputers, networks, energy, cooling) within a virtual twin *before* construction, saving significant time and money.
  • "Looks Real, Is Real": The virtual twins offer precise digital representations, integrating industrial bills of material (BOMs) and allowing for advance validation, preventing costly mistakes in complex builds like a $50 billion AI factory.

The Augmented Engineer & Knowledge Protection

  • Virtual Companions: AI agents will serve as "companions" to engineers, augmenting the design process rather than replacing them. Every designer will have a team of AI companions trained to perform specific tasks.
      • Structured & Unstructured Data: AI facilitates the conversion of unstructured information (photos, videos) into precise, controllable 3D structured data for enhanced design.
      • Revolutionized Workflow: Workflows will be transformed, enabling rapid progression from a 2D sketch to a parametric 3D model with automatic simulation in seconds.
  • Engineers as Architects: Engineers will manage and direct their AI companions, who can explore design options, optimize parameters, and present multiple solutions for human fine-tuning. This will significantly increase the number of users of Dassault Systèmes' tools, encompassing both human and AI agents.
  • Compliance by Design:
      • Case Study (NAR): The National Institute for Aviation Research (NAR) can use virtual companions to automatically ingest and constantly verify compliance with the 10,000+ requirements for airplane certification, shifting compliance upstream to become a competitive edge.
  • From Language Model to World Model:
      • Language Models: Understand syntax, vocabulary, and "taste" in language.
      • World Models: Go further by obeying the laws of physics, understanding causality (inertia, friction, gravity), and learning through simulation and examples.
      • "Shift Left" for Design: This allows for manufacturability, regulatory compliance (integrating human values), and component approval to be integrated upstream into the design process, ensuring "good to go" designs.
  • Protecting Knowledge: AI companions will codify individual designers' skills, preferences, habits, and domain expertise, creating a personalized, private repository of knowledge that enhances individual productivity without being public or open-sourced.

Notable Quotes

"Everything that we do is in the digital world. 40 years ago, Dassault Systèmes revolutionized the idea of virtual twins... now we're going to represent the physical world at a much, much larger scale in a completely revolutionized computer, an AI computer." — Jensen Huang, NVIDIA CEO "They don't want to automate the past. They want to invent the future." — Pascal Daloz, Dassault Systèmes CEO "AI is foundational to every single industry, it is going to become an infrastructure. Just as water was infrastructure, electricity is infrastructure, internet was infrastructure, now artificial intelligence will be infrastructure." — Jensen Huang, NVIDIA CEO "It is very likely and I'm certain this is going to happen that every designer... will have a team of companions and you've trained these companions... they're all going to be using Dassault Systèmes tools." — Jensen Huang, NVIDIA CEO
Hey everybody. Are you called solid workers? Hard workers. Hard workers. So, welcome on stage, Jason. Thank you. It's always a pleasure to have you. I don't know if people realize, but we have a long-standing relationship, right? I think we almost started the collaboration 30 years ago. Yeah, over a quarter century ago. Do you remember how it started? Well, we started during the last computing platform revolution, in fact, the personal computer revolution. What used to be Unix workstations was migrating to Windows-based workstations. The technology that made it possible for us to collaborate was based on OpenGL. We invented a technology called CGFX, which is the precursor of CUDA. OpenGL became RTX today, fully path-traced and physically based, and CGFX, of course, became CUDA. Here we are working together again as we reinvent the computing platform. Everything that we do is in the digital world. Forty years ago, Dassault revolutionized the idea of virtual twins. The idea of a virtual twin, of course, is to represent the physical world in a computer. And so now we're going to represent the physical world at a much, much larger scale in a completely revolutionized computer, an AI computer. This is a really, really fantastic time. You're right. It's an enormous journey. And as you say, now we are entering into a new chapter. We are now in what we call the generative economy, where we are powering the virtual twin with accelerated artificial intelligence. From your perspective, Jensen, what is happening in the global industry right now? Well, we're reinventing the computing stack altogether. In the last generation, the representation of the designs were structured representations, meaning we specified every geometry, every material, literally everything. Now it's going to be a generative computing model. In the world of generative computing models, the entire computing stack is being reinvented. Because AI is foundational to every single industry, it is going to become an infrastructure. Just as water was infrastructure, electricity is infrastructure, internet was infrastructure, now artificial intelligence will be infrastructure. We're growing so fast because every single industry needs to build it. Every single country will be powered by it, and literally every society will have it. This is the beginning of a new industrialization, which is fantastic for you because Dassault is the engine of the representation of everything that you want to build. In the past, I would say that we spent a third of our time in design and digital, maybe two-thirds of the time in physical. It is very likely in the future we're going to spend 100% of the time in digital. Even after we're done designing it, simulating it, validating it, we have to integrate it with software. Everything that's inside the Dassault systems, whether it's Katia, Simulia, or Biovia, or what are the other VAs? We have Delmia, we have ENOVIA. All of those VAs are going to be built on top of NVIDIA. Did we know that a quarter century ago? Anyhow, the design, the representation, all the simulation, and even the operations of it will be software-defined in the future. Everything from a pair of tennis shoes will be software-defined. Cars are software-defined. The robots that build the cars are software-defined. The factories where the robots are orchestrated to build the cars are software-defined, and the cars themselves are software-defined. Everything will be software-defined. Everything will be represented inside Dassault, and so we'll be designing everything, operating everything really as a virtual twin and realizing your vision for the first time. Before we go further, if you look around the crowds, it's a kind of a ruckus crowd. At Dassault Systèmes, we work with 45 million people around the world, 400,000 customers, more than 15 million engineers, researchers. I think you have here probably one of the, if not the, largest engineering community in the world. They do more than half of the products surrounding us every day: robots, drones, planes, cars, medical devices, drugs, home, city, factories. This is an amazing community. Don't you think so? I know they think so. Absolutely. Yeah, we're all engineers. You are an engineer? Yeah, sure, I'm still an engineer. So you belong to this community. I am. If I was to start all over again, I'd choose to use SolidWorks. That's why the world model matters, in fact, because for this community, success is not about automation. They don't want to automate the past; they want to invent the future. This is the reason why we are announcing this new chapter in our partnership, because together we are bringing the virtual twin factory with the AI factory. This opportunity is really enormous for you guys and for us. To prove the power of this, we have some concrete examples. I think Jensen and I have selected some use cases we want to share with you. Let's start with research and engineering first. I remember that almost everything that we do together starts with the computing platform. When PCs went into the cloud, Dassault reinvented itself again. Now, as we're extending from cloud to AI, we're reinventing again. Today, we're announcing a massive partnership. This is the largest collaboration our two companies have ever had in over a quarter century. Dassault is going to integrate NVIDIA CUDA X acceleration libraries, NVIDIA AI for physical AI and for agentic AI, and NVIDIA Omniverse, our version of digital twin technologies. All of these libraries represent our body of work over the quarter of a century. Now we're going to fuse these technologies into Dassault so that all of you will benefit from accelerated computing, artificial intelligence, and be able to work at a scale that's a hundred times, a thousand times, and very soon a million times greater than what you were able to do before. What used to be pre-rendered or offline simulations will now be literally the virtual twin vision that you've always had. Everything will be done literally in real time. We'll design products and simulate them in a wind tunnel in real time. We'll interconnect these robots and let them operate in a factory in real time, and they'll be building your products literally in real time. All of this is going to be happening, and in the next 5-10 years, this is going to be extraordinary. Speaking about this, let's start with life. I think life is the most complex system ever created, right? When you think about it, how much knowledge is encoded in the living world? With our virtual twins, we are learning from life. We are also understanding it in order to replicate and to scale it. This is possible. This is NVIDIA AI integrating with Biovia. Yes, Biovia. Right. And we will come back to this. Yes. But this is possible because I think we have this foundation. We call it the world model. The world model where it's grounded in biology, physics, and material sciences. The key question I have for you is, what does it take to compute a world model for life? The most important thing, the first thing that we have to do, is understand the language of life. Of course, in the world of physical design, the design started with your imagination, and you represented that physical object using structured information, geometries, and textures that were designed by you. However, life is different. Life existed before us. So we have to go learn the language of DNA, learn the language of proteins, and learn the language of cells and understand how they interact and its properties. That first stage of learning the meaning of life is what we are in the process of tackling. The second part, of course, is generative. Once you can learn something, learn the meaning of something, we can translate it between languages. We can translate between human language and the language of biology, and interpret it so that we can understand it in human language. Beyond that, you can now translate and generate new proteins that could be used for a drug, or generate new chemicals that could be used for a drug, and then, of course, generate new materials that could be stronger, more heat resistant, lighter, easier to manufacture, last longer. All of those properties are now within our grasp, and this is one of the reasons why this is likely going to be one of the most impactful areas of engineering in the next decade. Exactly. And it is already happening. In fact, we have a case with The Bel Group. You know them; they make the famous Babybel. Their mission is very simple: they want to produce healthier foods for millions of consumers, but at the same time, they want to consume less water, and they want to progressively change or at least complement dairy protein with non-dairy proteins. That's the reason why they are inventing what we call food science. Before, there were hundreds of physical tests for one single product. Now they generate automatically, and this is what you can see on the screen. They generate automatically the protein from the virtual twins because it's powered by the biological world model. The result is not only faster innovations, it's also certified decisions because you cannot play when you have the life of the people in your hands. That's what we do. Now let's move to something else, and you started to speak about it. You have seen on screen this is changing the daily life of the engineers in Dassault Systèmes. Now you define the specs, you run your simulations, and automatically the generative experience is producing and exploring the space of possibilities and finding the optimum solutions for you. Actually, the virtual twin is exploring an infinite of possibilities. So the question I have for you, could we compute infinity? We can't compute infinity, but we can imagine infinity, which is the reason why these surrogate and emulation models—the fusion of simulation and artificial intelligence—are so powerful. Eight years ago, I introduced the idea to scientific computing and simulations: the idea that in the future, not only will we use principled simulations where the equations, the laws of physics, are well understood and well represented, however, the simulation time takes way too long. Why don't we augment that with generative methods of predicting the future using artificial intelligence? It's a little bit like the analogy I would give. It's a little bit like dogs able to catch a ball out of the air, and yet they're not doing physics simulations of balls bouncing or the elastic nature of the ball. They're just literally watching us and predicting where it's going to go, and they snatch it out of the air. So the idea that an AI could learn how to predict physics and learn how to predict very accurately how materials would crumble, what happens to a crash—those capabilities are within grasp. We have a technology called Physics NeMo. Physics NeMo is essentially a physics-aware AI model simulation system and AI framework that allows us to create these AI models that are either trained by principled simulators or work alongside principled simulators. So it's grounded in the laws of physics but able to predict 10,000 times faster. Now if everything is already running in real-time, then you can predict it 10,000 times greater scale. That's just where we are right now. Imagine where we're going to be in the future. The idea of simulation and emulation coming together to help you design is going to be really revolutionary. Operating a factory of these gigantic scales inside a virtual twin is extraordinarily complex. A factory is not just one object; it's millions of objects. We want to simulate or emulate how these factories will operate in the real world so that we could arrange the manufacturing lines properly, arrange it in the right sequence, space it properly, organize the robots within it, run the robot AIs so that these AI robots could be operating inside the factory, manipulating things, assembling things, moving things, keeping things safe. All of this is going to happen inside a virtual twin. The products that Dassault is going to help people build and design are going to become gigantic in the future. Again, this is exactly what we do with Omron. You've seen on the screen, they don't use a virtual twin only to visualize the factories; in fact, they do much more. They engineer what we call a software-defined factory. That's right. The difference is coming from the fact that they are designing the autonomous part day one. It's not something they infuse when the production system is already up and running. As a result, those factories become much more flexible, resilient, and adaptive. It has to understand causality: if you tip over a domino, all of the dominoes that are connected to it or nearby it will be tipped over. It has to understand what comes after and what comes before. That's not necessarily captured in language, and all the language in the world won't capture that. FULL INTERVIEW: Sam Altman Responds to Anthropic’s Attack Ads, Live on TBPN

TBPN(48.4K) ·36:42 · 2026-02-05
Sam Altman John Coogan Jordi Hays
OpenAI CEO Sam Altman discussed the launch of GPT-5.3-Codex, highlighting its role in enabling a future where users manage AI agents at higher levels of abstraction for complex tasks.
TL;DR OpenAI CEO Sam Altman discussed the launch of GPT-5.3-Codex, highlighting its role in enabling a future where users manage AI agents at higher levels of abstraction for complex tasks. He emphasized the growing importance of compute power as the "new oil" and the...

TL;DR

OpenAI CEO Sam Altman discussed the launch of GPT-5.3-Codex, highlighting its role in enabling a future where users manage AI agents at higher levels of abstraction for complex tasks. He emphasized the growing importance of compute power as the "new oil" and the fundamental transformation of software, while also outlining OpenAI's advertising strategy focused on reducing AI's "capability overhang."

New Models & Agent-Driven Workflows

  • GPT-5.3-Codex Launch: OpenAI has launched GPT-5.3-Codex, described as the world's best and fastest coding model with improved personality and computer usage capabilities.
      • Mid-Turn Interaction: A key feature is the ability to interact with the model mid-turn, enabling users to steer and correct agents during long, multi-hour tasks for superior outcomes.
  • Agent Orchestration: Future workflows will involve users managing "teams of agents" and operating at increasingly higher levels of abstraction as agent capabilities advance.
      • Capability Overhang: There is a significant "intelligence overhang," making the development of better management tools for agents more critical than raw intelligence increases for the immediate future.
      • Codex Desktop: The Codex desktop app has seen surprising user adoption, demonstrating how small improvements in user experience ("10% of polish") can dramatically increase the utility of powerful models.
          • General-Purpose Agent: The vision extends to a general knowledge work agent that can control computers and handle complex tasks for non-technical users (e.g., an auto-completing to-do list).
      • Mobile Integration: Future plans include mobile capabilities, allowing users to initiate tasks and interact with their unified AI backend across various devices.
      • OpenClaw Spirit: Altman praised the "OpenClaw spirit" of individual open-source innovation, which can often develop features faster than large companies constrained by legal and privacy considerations.

Strategic Resources & Industry Shifts

  • Compute as the New Oil: While "data is the new oil" was an overstatement a decade ago, "compute power is the new oil" is now the closest accurate description, reflecting the exponential increase in resources driving AI model improvements.
      • Bottlenecks: Currently, chips are the primary bottleneck, and society needs to aggressively increase global wafer capacity.
  • Revival of Research Labs: Altman noted a positive trend in the resurgence of industry-backed research labs, a goal OpenAI initially aimed to inspire.
      • Acquisition Strategy: Future acquisitions will likely be hybrid entities that blend extraordinary product work with a strong research component.
  • Software Evolution: Software is not dead but undergoing a significant transformation, with expected volatility in the SaaS market.
      • API-First World: "Every company is an API company now whether they want to be or not," as agents will increasingly interact directly with various services.
      • Consumer-Centric Integration: Companies like Uber are recognizing the necessity of integrating with preferred AI agents, prioritizing consumer experience over platform lock-in.
  • Forward-Deployed Engineers: These engineers are crucial for integrating AI into non-AI-native companies, addressing challenges such as system integration, fine-tuning, orchestration, and critical data security.

AI's Impact & Future Outlook

  • Shifting Benchmarks: Traditional AI benchmarks are rapidly becoming obsolete; "GDP impact" may soon be the most relevant metric, with "happiness" potentially following.
  • Human-AI Relationships: Altman's 2016 prediction of emotional attachment to chatbots is now a tangible reality and a significant concern.
      • Deflationary Impact: AI could lead to scenarios where the quality of life dramatically improves, even if traditional GDP measures decline, posing a new challenge for societal measurement.
  • Model IQ & Scale: Altman anticipates models will become vastly more intelligent, capable of processing information for durations equivalent to "10,000 human years," though the societal impact might feel "very weird" rather than "impossibly weird" due to a human-centric focus.
  • Social AI: While current AI bots on existing social platforms are problematic, Altman is interested in designing new social experiences where agents interact on behalf of people, focusing on "assertion of humanity" over AI detection.
  • Sora & Creative Use Cases: Sora's video generation is primarily used for personal, niche content like memes and customized character images (e.g., caricatures or enhanced self-portraits), rather than mass consumption of AI-generated videos.
      • Tool Use: Models will become highly capable at utilizing motion graphics and editing tools like After Effects.
  • Internal Focus Amidst Scrutiny: Despite intense media scrutiny and public anxieties, Altman describes OpenAI's internal environment as "less chaotic" and highly focused on building new models and addressing challenges like the compute crunch.

Advertising & Competitive Landscape

  • OpenAI's Advertising Strategy: OpenAI's first Super Bowl ad was not a direct-response campaign but aimed at researchers and those driving the AI revolution.
      • New Ad Goal: Future advertising, including an upcoming ad for Codex, will focus on teaching people "what they can go do with AI" to reduce the general "capability overhang."
      • Ad Rollout: OpenAI has yet to begin testing ads and expects an iterative process to determine the most effective ad units and approach, acknowledging its rapid transition from a research lab to a large company.
  • Anthropic's "Attack Ads": Altman characterized Anthropic's Super Bowl ads as "wrong" and "deceptive," stating that OpenAI respects its users and would not integrate ads into the LLM stream in a "dystopic" manner.
      • "Playing Dirty": He found the ads ironic, using deception to criticize deceptive ads, and called the competitive exchange a "sideshow" compared to the models' actual capabilities.

Notable Quotes

"I think we will be heading towards a workflow where a lot of people just feel like they're managing a team of agents and they'll keep as the agents get better, they'll keep operating at a higher and higher level of abstraction." — Sam Altman, CEO, OpenAI "On the whole, compute power is the new oil is the statement that feels closest to true to me, but there will be other parts too." — Sam Altman, CEO, OpenAI "The statement that someone said to me that has stuck in my mind most these last couple of weeks is that every company is an API company now whether they want to be or not." — Sam Altman, CEO, OpenAI "I think AI is now unbelievably capable in most of the world. It's still like asking it basic questions on Chad GBT... Everyone can go build amazing things now. Everyone can go do all kinds of work." — Sam Altman, CEO, OpenAI
Welcome to the show, Sam. How are you doing? Good. Thank you for having me back. Big day. Kick us off with where should we start? Should we start with the model or Frontier? Can we start with the model just because I'm having fun with it? Absolutely. Break it down. What did you launch? We launched 5.3 Codex. It is, I think, the best coding model in the world. Eventually, the models will get so good that they'll help companies deploy themselves, and the forward-deployed engineers will again get to work at a higher level of abstraction. So the forward engineers take this incredible new technology and a platform like Frontier and say, "We will connect your company to an AI platform so that you can use all these agents and workflows and everything else you want." How important are these metaphors, or how temporary are they? I was very interested in reading about Gas Town, and you have these pole cats, and it's this whole Mad Max world. That feels like maybe just a temporary aberration where you're setting up agents for specific tasks, but also that could be incredibly valuable in explaining to a large corporation how they're going to integrate AI across the whole organization. I suspect, like everything else that's happening when an industry is moving so fast, all of this is somewhat temporary on a long enough time scale. As these models become more capable and these agents are operating on very long time horizons with the ability to just figure it out, and our trust in their robustness keeps going up, then maybe you don't need a lot of the abstractions we need today. Maybe you just have a single AI bot that runs at your company, and you can say, "Hey, I want to launch this new product," and it does everything an ambitious person would do. You're at the top of the charts. There's some subdivision that happens within a reasoning model, but it doesn't truly parallelize, at least that I'm aware of. What does it look like in a world where you go to a model, but now it's spinning up a whole bunch of different models underneath? There are two key insights of the whole field in this question. Number one, the implication that no chart in AI lasts more than a few years is right. We'll see how much longer this one is useful. The second is that a lot of people thought that we were just going to need a super long task and a super long task horizon, so we needed a super long context. What people have already seen with coding agents is that by agents breaking up work, orchestrating it well, farming it off to sub-agents, even with the current limitations of the technology, we can do something which should not be surprising because it's similar to how people do things and get amazing amounts of work done. That's been cool to watch, and I think that will keep going. A joke that some people at OpenAI make is that soon the chart that matters is just going to be GDP impact.
