So maybe the shape of things to come is that truly extraordinary product work will have more and more of a research component, and it'll be more of a hybrid thing. Is data the new oil? Is there value? We were joking a couple of days ago: "They have a bunch of data, but they don't understand AI. They don't know how to monetize." That phrase was effectively wasted a decade ago, and to say it now sounds really silly, but it feels like it could be more true now than ever. Certainly. They really did waste it a decade ago. I was just thinking of the kind of people that used to say that. They're nice people over at TED. The magic relationship of this last eight years, whatever you want to call it, has been that we can put in more and more resources—compute, data, new ideas, whatever—into creating an artifact, and it gets better. That's why we have this huge exponential increase in resources, but we keep getting better and better models. For all the concern people have about it topping out or slowing down, no one's been right about that. Sometimes it looked like they were for a couple of months as we digested a new model or came to a new form factor, but it has been an incredibly smooth last six or eight years in terms of what those resources are. There can be some trade-off; sometimes it's better to spend your money on better data, sometimes on more compute, sometimes something else. On the whole, "compute power is the new oil" is the statement that feels closest to true to me, but there will be other parts too. Is software dead? It's different. It's definitely not dead. But how you create software, how you're going to use it, how much you're going to have written for you each time you need it versus how much you'll want a consistent UX—that's all going to change. There have been a number of these big sell-offs of SAS stocks over the last few years as these models have rolled out. I expect there will continue to be more. I expect there will be big booms in software. I think it's just going to be volatile for a while as people figure out what this looks like. The statement that someone said to me that has stuck in my mind most these last couple of weeks is that every company is an API company now, whether they want to be or not, because agents are just going to be able to write a CS. We had D from Uber on yesterday, and he had a pretty refreshing approach. We were asking about integrating agents with Uber, and he recognized that the ad business could potentially be threatened if you can order an Uber in ChatGPT, but he basically said you have to think of the consumer. The consumer wants to order an Uber via their preferred agent. You should let them, otherwise you're going to have other problems. That is the right take for sure, and I think so at least. We've been through platform shifts like this before. Uber wouldn't have existed without one. It wasn't until the iPhone that it could make sense to order an Uber right where you were as you're out in the world. I think there will be totally new things that happen and other things you'll use in new ways. But definitely, as I've started using Codex, my excitement about having agents go off and do things for me and still use other services, pay other services, is high. I'm sure we'll need to figure out new business models and how revenue gets shared around, but that will happen. Talk more about desktop. One more question on SAS. Have any public market SAS companies tried to get a soft landing with OpenAI? Do you think there's any value there? No public SAS companies that I'm aware of have tried that with OpenAI. Some of them will certainly be durable and are on sale right now, and potentially just need new energy and an entirely new approach, and maybe OpenAI could provide that. I think some will be incredibly valuable. Some do feel like a thinner layer now. I don't know; I was talking recently to a bunch of SAS companies and they don't feel unexcited. They believe they're going through a big transformation. They say, "Sure, other people can instantly write software now, but so can we, and we have a great system of record." It seems reasonable. Some won't make it, of course. Talk more about the Codex desktop rollout. It feels like there's been a successful amount of downloads, but also a key shift for people who are maybe lightly technical but don't have time to set up an IDE and configure an environment to actually start writing software. I want to know about plans to integrate to the phone. That was a big moment, I think, for a lot of people with the OpenClaw thing; it was, "Oh, I can text something and it will go and write code," and that's valuable, and that unlocks a new agentic experience. Where do you see the Codex desktop ecosystem going? Codex Desktop has been somewhat of a surprise to me in terms of how much people love it, including how much I love it myself. I think it's a great example of how 10% of polish on the experience of using these models, especially when there's so much capability overhang, goes an extremely long way to what you can build and how you interact with this stuff. Of course, we should have an ability to kick off new tasks from mobile, and we'll do that. Really, what you want is your single AI working for you on a unified backend, with access to all of your data, your ideas, your stuff, your memory, all the context, and the ability to work across a lot of surfaces. Often you'll be at your desktop, often you'll be on your phone, and you just want to add something in. But it is a pretty profound shift in my own workflow, not just for coding tasks, but more general-purpose tasks. It's still hard to use if you're not at least reasonably technical. But obviously, we'll find a version of this product that can do other knowledge work tasks and control your computer, where you don't have to be technical. It will bring the magic of building stuff to a lot of people because even if you never look at code, you'll be able to build something reasonably sophisticated. One of the things I built when I was playing around with the new Codex app is this thing I had always wanted: a magic autocompleting to-do list. I really work with to-do lists, and this idea that I could just put tasks in and it would try to do them. If it could complete them, it would complete them. If it needed questions, it would ask me questions. If I had to do something, I could still do it the old-fashioned way. An interface like that, where all the stuff you want to do, you explain to a computer or your AI, and it tries to go off and do them—and sure, if you're on your phone, you're going to just add a task on your phone, or if you want to easily import something from email, you're going to do that—feels really good. So, I'm excited about all the ways that this will just become a general knowledge work agent. Were you unsurprised to see a product like OpenClaw come from open source? That kind of user experience is something that you knew would be a thing, and yet I think part of the magic of OpenClaw is that it would be very, very difficult for a large tech company. Peter didn't make many phone calls to hyperscalers to say, "Hey, I'm going to be integrating your API." It just went. When I think back on the Sky acquisition, this kind of experience was probably very top of mind and things that you're working towards internally. I love the spirit of everything about OpenClaw. You are totally right that it's much easier to imagine a one-person open source project doing something like that than a company that is going to be afraid of lawsuits and data privacy and everything else. I think this is how innovation works. Something like that starts, is clearly amazing. There will be a way to make a mass market version of that product, but letting the builders build, letting the equivalent of the Homebrew Computer Club spirit go here, is so important. Can we switch to social? If I Google "Sam Altman social," I get pure AI and Sora, and then also predictions about a human social network. Where do you see social going broadly? How do you want to integrate with it and power it in the future? The "mold book thing" was a very interesting social experiment to watch, and I think it points to agents interacting in social spaces, hopefully on behalf of people to some degree, which could be quite interesting. I don't think we know what to do there yet, but it feels like social is going to change a lot. I am interested in the space of what a social experience can look like when your agent is talking to my agent and coming up with new stuff. Clearly, putting a lot of AI bots on the existing social platforms is just making everyone crazy and not that fun. So that's not the right answer. I think we can design something new for what this technology is capable of that will feel good and useful. Is there a solution to the bot problem that's just all the labs integrating with all the other platforms? Even if you can't detect it's AI generated, you can literally say, "We just generated those tokens; those exact tokens are in our database." You can't do that because open source models are good enough to write at this point. I am excited about the assertion of humanity instead of the detection of AI as a thing here. I don't know if the social platforms are in their interest to solve this. It is, because in the short term it creates a lot of engagement and increased usage. I believe they could solve it if they wanted to, but I'm not sure it's in their interest. I don't like it, but some people do seem to enjoy it. Can you talk a little bit more about where Sora, as a video generation model, is going? It feels like tool use is maybe under-discussed. Adding reasoning. It's not just the diffusion model. It's giving these models the ability to make linear cuts and overlay motion graphics. When I scroll through Instagram reels, I see vibe reels with cutouts, and they flip negative, and it's all color-graded. It's stuff that, yes, you could probably diffuse it all, but it's pretty cool to teach a model to also use After Effects or whatever video motion graphics suite you want to use. Is that an interesting unlock? What do you see going on? All of that stuff will happen, and I agree with you, the models will get really great at doing that. People love generating videos. I would say we have not yet found a way that people really love watching other people's videos. This is true for a lot of other AI. People love talking to ChatGPT, but it's not that compelling for most people to read other people's generation. But isn't that for all writing and all video? It seems stronger to me in this case than the general case. But maybe you're right. If somebody says, "Hey, I generated a 15-minute video. I'm really excited for you to watch it," and you watch the first 10 seconds and you're not that captured by it, I don't care that it was human-made. You may be right, and this is not a special case. Do you see that in the data with Sora downloads? I've noticed that I'll generate stuff on Sora, download it, and share it to a group chat, and then it's this little inside joke that me and five other people get. We see this in family group chats: "Oh, it's our dog and our kids." But there's not really a broader business application. Does everyone like this? Absolutely. The most common use case is something like that. Memes on group chats are a real killer use case for Sora. How is the Disney rollout going? I was super excited about it. Jordy was extremely bullish on it from a business strategy perspective. When you look at how image models have grown, and various LLMs historically, and now you're going to have an image and video model that can do something that no other LLM can do, at least legally. Is Bob Iger joining OpenAI? I would love it if Bob Iger joined OpenAI. He's looking for a job and free a year. He's a free agent, pick him up. Do some recruiting. That would be great. I think that generating characters in images and videos is going to be very important to people, and they really like that. As we were saying, otherwise, I don't think many people want to watch me and some Star Wars character doing something together, but I might think it's cool. There's a real trend going on right now with ChatGPT where it's, "Make a caricature of me and my job based on everything you know about me." Those kinds of things people actually do like looking at other people's media. It's almost like a face filter or something. There's enough of the human still in there that it's personalized, and it says something about you. A lot of what's gone viral before with image generation is if you can make people look a little bit more attractive or cool than they are in real life without having to ask for that. How are you thinking about the actual rollout? We were debating between opening the floodgates so you can generate any Disney property versus having, for example, "Spider-Man week" where everyone posts Spider-Man, and then "Mickey Mouse week" for another viral moment. I'm not sure what the team is planning there. I know Disney has had some different opinions about how they want to do things, and we'll try to be a good partner there. But I'd be excited to open the floodgates personally. That would be fun. Talk about your first Super Bowl ad. It felt like it was not AI-generated, with lots of motion graphics, black dots coming together. What was the goal with that ad? Who were you trying to speak to? It didn't feel like a direct response, QR code, "download the app" kind of ad. What was the mission? I love that ad. I think that was such a cool one. It was clearly not meant to be a mass market or direct response ad. But speaking to the people at the center of this revolution, trying to celebrate everything that has come before and everything that will come after. We didn't hear a lot about it from average users of ChatGPT, but we heard a lot about it from researchers in the field, and there was a lot of resonance there. It was definitely not generated; it was done the old-fashioned way. A lot of people loved it, a lot of people hated it, and many people in the middle didn't get it, and I felt okay about that. It's a great example. I like our ad for Sunday. It's about Codex, no surprise. Talk about the evolution of the advertising to be more clear about the actual use case, the value. What are you trying to say with your advertising strategy now as it relates to video? The thing I would most like us to say, and I think this is a new challenge given where the models are, is to teach people what they can do with AI. I think AI is now unbelievably capable. Most of the world is still just asking it basic questions on ChatGPT. Everyone can build amazing things now. Everyone can do all kinds of work. Scientists are going to make new discoveries. To the degree that advertising we do can teach people how to use this, I think that would be awesome. So, the KPI is to reduce the capability overhang broadly? I think that should be a general KPI for us, not just for our ads. The products that we build, how we teach people to use those products—that feels very important. Anthropic also has a bunch of ads in the Super Bowl. They seem to run a ton. What do you think they're getting wrong about their characterization of how ads will roll out in chat apps? It's just wrong. The main thing is, we are not stupid. We respect our users. We understand that if we did something like what those ads depict, people would rightfully stop using the product. Our first principle with ads is that we're not going to put stuff into the LLM stream. That would feel crazy dystopian, like a bad sci-fi movie. So the main thing that's wrong with the ads is that using a deceptive ad to criticize deceptive ads doesn't sit right with me. I asked Claude what the definition of playing dirty was, and it said, "Misleading others about your intentions, hiding information, or creating false impressions." I thought it was a little dirty. It was well played for sure, and it was a funny ad. They nailed the stuff about the ChatGPT personality that most annoys me and which we'll fix very soon. So that part was funny. I think it's great for them not to do ads. We have a different shaped business. I did notice that they said they may later revise this decision and will explain why. The blog post did a good job of disarming the pro-ad people and gave themselves an out in the future. Do you think they care? It doesn't matter. I think it's a sideshow. People are excited for a food fight between companies, but the amazing capabilities of these models, the product, the groundswell of excitement around Codex—that feels way more important to me. How do you stop the pausing that happens in voice mode? Do you need new hardware for that or is it a model capability thing? We need a new model. We may need some new hardware too, but mostly we just need a new model. I think we will have a great voice mode by the end of this year. What's the bigger bottleneck? Energy or chips? It goes back and forth. Right now, it's chips again. It will be different at different times. Is there anything society, America, should be doing more aggressively to increase the supply of fabs? It may get solved on its own; normal capitalism may solve it. But I think somehow deciding as a society that we are going to increase the wafer capacity of the world, and we're going to fund that, and we're going to get the whole supply chain and the talented people we need to make that happen would be a very good thing to do. Do you think there's an upper bound on model IQ? The race right now is, you're smart, but you're not smart for days, you're smart for hours. Can you go much farther and get much smarter? It seems certain. Upper bound? I don't know how to think about that question yet. I can't even reason about what 2,000 IQ looks like. I don't know what that means. It's funny you say. I can't reason about what it means to think about a problem for 10,000 human years. That's crazy. But maybe IQ is going to feel even weirder. I don't know. I somehow feel like this isn't going to feel as strange as it sounds, for a bunch of reasons. We're so focused on other people. We're so focused on our own lives. We have such a human-centric nature that, okay, this thing is really smart. It's inventing new science for us. It's running companies for us. It's doing all this stuff. That sounds like it should be impossibly weird, and I think it will just be very weird. Do you think space data centers will provide a meaningful amount of compute for OpenAI in the next two to three years, five years? No. 10 years? I wish Ethan luck. The funny thing about the back and forth about ads is that in our world, the criticism is that you didn't launch ads early enough. Is there a world where you wish you launched earlier? How is the actual rollout going? Are advertisers happy? Do you have a really long roadmap, or do you think you'll be faster at catching up to what's frontier in ads? We haven't even started the test yet. We start the test soon. It's going to take us some number of iterations to figure out the right ad unit, the right way this all works. Do I wish we had started earlier? We have gone from not being a company three years and three months ago. We were a research lab, and now we are a pretty big company with a lot of products. So there are many things I wish we had done faster. I think we were correct on the trade-off here of how we balance things that we need to do. I wish we had launched this very cool enterprise platform this morning. I wish we had done that earlier too, but we had to deal with the monstrous growth of ChatGPT and Codex and all other stuff. Good problems to have. Last question for me: what happened to that internal writing model that you used to write the essay? That feels like something that was really cool, but we never really saw the light of day. We're going to get a lot of that spirit into a future model. There's so much stuff happening. We have to make these hard prioritization decisions. I would love a cool writing model, not as much as I would love a cool coding model. What is possible now for coding, for science—that's the thing I'm most excited about. For accelerating all kinds of research, AI and otherwise, for really accelerating the economy. I think that's the right thing for us to most prioritize in terms of new capabilities. You want a model that can write beautifully if it can also think very clearly and express that very clearly. That's just useful in normal work. That makes a ton of sense. Last question for me. How have conversations been with the broad OpenAI leadership team? You guys are in a position where any single word or sentence you say in any situation can be spun into a headline immediately, and then you have to go on damage control, correcting the narrative. But of course, the original news is often seen more broadly than the correction, and it seems like an interesting challenge. It is a strange way to live. I don't know of any private company that has ever been so in the news and under a microscope. At some level it's frustrating, and we're so squarely in the sights of everybody's anxieties and every competitor trying to take us down. Everyone is wondering what will happen with AI to their business or their own lives, so there's a lot of plasma looking for an instability to collapse on. In some other sense, though, the subjective experience of it is that we are so busy with exciting stuff that it often feels like there's a crazy hurricane turning around us, but when we sit, it's fairly calm. The media or Twitter goes insane about something one day; they're talking about a crazy meltdown. We think, "That is insane!" People talk about it all day and then later find out it's wrong, which seems like a lot of wasted energy. But we're just focused: we have this great new model coming, people are building incredible stuff, companies are transforming, and we're trying to figure out how to get more compute and deal with this compute crunch. We just keep going and we're busy. Then, if we open Twitter, pop up our heads, and look at the news, it's like, "Wow, that is an insane, crazy thing happening," completely divorced from reality or 99% divorced from reality. Someone will correct it, but then we get back to work and people flip out again. It is weird to watch when we look outside, but it is less chaotic internally than you would imagine from reading the media reports. That makes sense. Well, thank you so much for taking the time to come chat with us. Congrats on the— I'm excited to see the Codex ad. Please try it. The app and 5.3 have been, I think, the coolest thing we've done in a while. Yes. With one prompt, I rebuilt the tbpn.com homepage to look exactly like Berkshire Hathaway. It was immediate, very fun. Interesting choice. Plain text. It was very easy. I immediately one-shotted it. I didn't really push it to its limits, but I'm having fun. So, thank you so much for coming on the show. We'll talk to you soon. Thank you. Great to catch up. Goodbye.

President Trump talks Minneapolis, Joe Rogan, the Fed, AI and 2028 in a wide-ranging interview

NBC News(11.7M) ·12:26 · 2026-02-05
Donald Trump Tom Llamas
President Trump discussed evolving law enforcement strategies in Minneapolis, emphasized national election integrity with mandatory voter identification, speculated on a strong 2028 ticket featuring JD Vance and Marco Rubio, and reiterated a firm stance against Iran's nuclear ambitions.
TL;DR President Trump discussed evolving law enforcement strategies in Minneapolis, emphasized national election integrity with mandatory voter identification, speculated on a strong 2028 ticket featuring JD Vance and Marco Rubio, and reiterated a firm stance against Iran's...

TL;DR

President Trump discussed evolving law enforcement strategies in Minneapolis, emphasized national election integrity with mandatory voter identification, speculated on a strong 2028 ticket featuring JD Vance and Marco Rubio, and reiterated a firm stance against Iran's nuclear ambitions.

Domestic Security and Immigration Enforcement

  • Minneapolis Response: President Trump indicated that lessons learned from Minneapolis include the potential for a "softer touch" alongside necessary toughness when dealing with "hard criminals."
      • He claimed responsibility for a reported decrease in crime in Minneapolis and Chicago, attributing it to his administration's efforts despite dealing with "Democrats that don't know anything about crime prevention."
      • The recent drawdown of 700 officers from Minneapolis, which he confirmed was initiated by him, is linked to a demand for the city to release prisoners and "murderers" they are holding.
  • Immigration Policy & Border Security:
      • Asserted that "25 million people" were allowed into the U.S. under the Biden administration via an "open border policy," including "bad people," drug dealers, and those emptied from foreign jails (e.e.g., Venezuela, Congo, Africa).
      • Maintained that his administration is "getting them out" and has achieved "record low crime in the United States," claiming it hasn't been said for 125 years.
  • ICE Incidents:
      • Expressed unhappiness with the incidents involving Renee Good and Alex Pretti, stating "it should have not happened" and calling them "very sad incidents," despite suggesting neither individual was "an angel."
      • Stressed the importance of backing law enforcement, including ICE and police, as a necessity for maintaining the country.
      • Clarified that a shift in immigration enforcement after Minneapolis would involve cities like Minneapolis "asking" and saying "please" for federal intervention rather than it being forced.

Election Integrity and Reform

  • Nationalizing Elections: President Trump clarified his comments on 'nationalizing elections,' stating he meant addressing "extremely corrupt" areas like Detroit, Philadelphia, and Atlanta, which he says are characterized by "corrupt elections."
  • Voter ID: Strongly advocated for mandatory voter identification, suggesting that Democrats oppose it because "they want to cheat on elections."
      • Stated that if states "don't straighten it out," federal controls might be necessary, viewing states as "agents accumulating votes" for election winners.
  • Midterm Trust & Fulton County:
      • Confirmed he would trust the results of the midterms "if the elections are honest."
      • Reiterated his belief in widespread cheating in his past election, stating, "I believe there was cheating."
      • Defended the FBI raids on a Fulton County election center, stating it was under "review and investigation for four years" and that a "respected, but liberal judge" signed a court order to collect ballots for inspection to "find out the true winner of that state."
  • Foreign Influence: Believes China attempts to influence U.S. elections and dismissed "Russia" collusion claims as a "hoax."

Political Outlook and Alliances

  • 2028 Presidential Primary: Indicated he would "probably" endorse in the 2028 presidential primary, noting a "great bench" of potential candidates.
  • Potential 2028 Ticket: Suggested a Vice President JD Vance and Secretary of State Marco Rubio ticket would be "very hard to be beaten," highlighting both as "fantastic" and "very capable."
      • Distinguished them by saying one is "more diplomatic than the other," both possessing "high intelligence."
  • 2029 Presidential Scenario: Responded to a question about still being President in 2029 with "I don't know," but implied his continued involvement is "making America great again."
  • Public Relations: Believed his administration did a "phenomenal job" but was "not good at public relations," failing to adequately "sell the great job we're doing."
  • Joe Rogan Interview: Recalled a "great interview" with Joe Rogan, calling it "his biggest ever" with over 300 million views.
  • Bill Clinton: Expressed surprising positive sentiment towards former President Bill Clinton, stating "I still like Bill Clinton" and "I liked his behavior toward me. I thought he got me, he understood me."
      • Cited Clinton's advice against running against Trump in 2016 to Hillary Clinton.

Foreign Policy: Iran

  • Threat to Iran's Leadership: Stated that the Supreme Leader of Iran "should be very worried" due to U.S. actions.
  • Impact of U.S. Actions: Attributed the current "mess" in Iran to U.S. efforts to "wiped out their nuclear" program, which he claims brought "peace in the Middle East" as Arab countries are "not afraid of Iran anymore."
  • Nuclear Program Restart: Warned that if Iran attempts to restart its nuclear program, "we'll send them back to do the job again."

Notable Quotes

"I learned that maybe we can use a little bit of a softer touch. But you still have to be tough." — President Trump "If Democrats don't want voter I.D., that means they want to cheat." — President Trump "I think we do a phenomenal job but I don't think we're good at public relations... We don't sell the great job we're doing." — President Trump "I still like Bill Clinton... I liked his behavior toward me. I thought he got me, he understood me." — President Trump
He thinks Vice President Vance and Secretary of State Marco Rubio should be on the same ticket, and who should be on the top. We begin with the drawdown in Minneapolis. Let's drill into the news today. There's a big change on immigration, with 700 officers leaving Minneapolis. Did that come from you? Yes, it did, but it didn't come from me just because I wanted to do it. We are waiting for them to release prisoners, give us the murderers they're holding, and all of the bad people, drug dealers, all of the bad people. We allowed, in our country, I'd say 25 million people with an open border policy for four years under Biden. That group, I call them the autopen group, we allowed to come into our country, people the likes of which no country would accept. We're getting them out. We've gotten a lot of them out. Crime in Minnesota, in Minneapolis, is down. Crime in all cities is down. Speaking of that, do you know why it's down? It's down because of us. In Chicago, it's down 25%, despite always dealing with these people—they happen to be Democrats—who don't know anything about crime prevention. Speaking of Minneapolis, what did you learn? I learned that maybe we can use a little bit of a softer touch. But you still have to be tough. We're dealing with really hard criminals. But look, I've called the people, the governor, the mayor, spoke to them, had great conversations with them. Then I see them ranting and raving out there, literally as though a call wasn't made. We've done a great job everywhere. After the shooting of Renee Good, you said ICE made mistakes. What were they? I'm not happy with the two incidents, both of them, not one or the other. He was not an angel; she was not an angel. Look at some tapes, but still, I'm not happy with what happened there. Nobody could be happy. ICE wasn't happy either. But I'm going to be always with our great people of law enforcement, ICE, police. We have to back them. If we don't back them, we don't have a country. You mentioned Renee Good and Alex Pretty not being angels. Do you think that justified what happened to them, though? No, it should not have happened. It was, to me, a very sad incident, two incidents. They mention the one now, not the other, but I think both were sad. You know who feels worse about it than anybody? The people of ICE. They're strong, tough people, and they have to be tough; we're dealing with hardened criminals. Right. Jails have been emptied into our country from all over the world, from Venezuela, where we've had great success, you'll admit. Uh-huh. But from Venezuela, the Congo, Africa, all over the world, the jail population was emptied into our country. If we don't have strong people, you are not getting them out. We're getting them out. I say it again, we have record low crime in the United States. Nobody's been able to say that for 125 years. I want to be clear because it sounds like there's a shift in immigration enforcement here, a shift after Minneapolis. What should Americans expect going forward? One thing I say to my people, we do a good job, we don't get credit for it. I say they have to ask and say please, when a city is... Who asks? Who says please? The mayor or governor. I don't want to force ourselves into a city, even if their numbers are terrible. Joe Rogan was one of your biggest endorsers in 2024, and he said he understands the President's plan to deport people here illegally, but also understands why critics compare ICE officers to the Gestapo, asking people for their papers. What is your response to that? We had a great interview. It was his biggest ever. Over 300 million people. It was very good. She didn't want to do it. She decided she's not going to do it because she's not capable, but I spoke to him three days ago, had a great conversation. All I'm doing... Did he mention this? Did you talk about ICE enforcement? Just had a great conversation. I think he's a great guy and I think he likes me, too. Liking me is not important. What happens is, I think we do a phenomenal job, but I don't think we're good at public relations. What does that mean? You've said it a couple of times now. What do you mean by that? We don't sell the great job we're doing. Let's talk about the midterms. You recently suggested nationalizing elections. What did you mean by that? I didn't say... I said there are some areas in the country that are extremely corrupt, corrupt elections. Take a look at Detroit, Philadelphia, Atlanta, areas that are unbelievably corrupt. I could give you plenty more, too. I say we cannot have corrupt elections. If we have to, if they don't straighten it out—as an example, voter ID—why don't the Democrats want voter ID? You know who does want voter ID? Democrat voters want it, but Democrat politicians don't, because they want to cheat on elections. Can you imagine, if I were a politician and they asked me, "Do you want voter identification?" and I said no, there's no way to win that debate. If Democrats don't want voter ID, that means they want to cheat. We can't allow cheating in elections. If we needed to put in federal controls as opposed to state controls—remember this, they're an agent, accumulating votes for whoever wins the election. If they can't do it honestly, and it can't be done properly and timely, something else has to happen. Will you trust the results of the midterms if Republicans lose control of Congress? You suggested that Vice President JD Vance and Secretary of State Marco Rubio would be a tough ticket to beat. Who should be top of the ticket? I don't want to get into it. We have three years to go. I have two people doing a great job. I don't want an argument. I don't want to use the word "fight," it wouldn't be a fight. But JD is fantastic and Marco is fantastic. Extended interview: Tom Llamas exclusive with President Trump

NBC News(11.7M) ·50:17 · 2026-02-05
Tom Llamas Donald Trump
President Trump discussed his administration's strong stance on immigration enforcement, claiming success in lowering crime rates nationwide and on the border, while also outlining his economic policies including tariffs and potential rebate checks, ...
TL;DR President Trump discussed his administration's strong stance on immigration enforcement, claiming success in lowering crime rates nationwide and on the border, while also outlining his economic policies including tariffs and potential rebate checks, and his views on AI's...

TL;DR

President Trump discussed his administration's strong stance on immigration enforcement, claiming success in lowering crime rates nationwide and on the border, while also outlining his economic policies including tariffs and potential rebate checks, and his views on AI's impact, election integrity, and global relations.

Immigration, Crime, and Law Enforcement Strategy

  • Minneapolis ICE Officer Reduction:
      • Action: 700 federal officers were pulled from Minneapolis by Tom Homan, a decision attributed to President Trump.
      • Reasoning: Stated that the withdrawal was due to local authorities not releasing "murderers" and "bad people" that his administration aimed to deport.
      • Goal: To push cities to cooperate in handing over dangerous criminals.
  • National Crime and Enforcement Claims:
      • Crime Reduction: Claims crime is down in Minneapolis, Chicago (by 25%), and other major cities due to his administration's efforts.
      • Law Enforcement Backing: Emphasizes unwavering support for law enforcement (ICE, police), stating, "If we don't back them, we don't have a country."
      • Targeted Deportations: Current mission focuses on deporting "criminals, really bad criminals," including 11,888 alleged murderers, drug dealers, and mentally ill individuals "emptied into our country" from places like Venezuela and Congo.
      • Local Cooperation: Prefers to be "invited" by cities (e.g., Chicago, Philadelphia, San Francisco, Louisiana) to intervene in crime rather than forcing federal intervention.
      • Data Claim: Asserts the U.S. has "record low crime... for 125 years" and the "safest country since 1900."
      • Officer Conduct: Acknowledged "mistakes" in the Renee Good and Alex Pretti incidents in Minnesota, calling both "very sad," but defended ICE officers as "strong, tough people" dealing with hardened criminals.
  • Border Security:
      • Success Metrics: Credits policies for "historic lows" in illegal border crossings and a "beautifully closed" border.
      • Caravans: Stated that countries sent "people that they didn't want" in caravans, including criminals and those unwilling to work.
      • Kristi Noem: Defends her role, attributing the closed border to her efforts, but noted "public relations" challenges in getting credit.
  • Public Perception:
      • Poll Discrepancy: Dismisses polls showing low approval for his immigration handling, stating they are "almost dishonest" and depend on how questions are asked.
      • "Public Relations" Issue: Believes his administration does a "phenomenal job" but is "not good at public relations," failing to "sell the great job that we're doing."

Economic Outlook and Policy

  • Investment and Growth:
      • Investment Claim: States "$18 trillion being invested into the country," contrasting it with "less than a trillion" under the previous administration.
      • Outlook: Expresses confidence in economic growth, stating that growth "will soon make the debt look very small."
  • Inflation Control:
      • Inherited Situation: Claims to have "inherited the worst inflation in the history of our country" (48 years).
      • Current Status: Asserts "almost no inflation" now, citing 1.2% for the last three months.
      • Gasoline Prices: Points to recent gasoline prices at $1.99 a gallon, down from $4.50-$5.
  • Tariffs and Rebates:
      • Tariff Revenue: States that China is paying "hundreds of billions of dollars in tariffs," with "trillions" generated by the threat of tariffs, making the U.S. rich.
      • Farmer Support: Used tariff revenue to provide $12 billion to farmers.
      • Rebate Checks: Seriously considering $2,000 rebate checks for Americans from tariff revenue, confirming he "can do that" and may make the commitment.
      • Military Payment: Mentioned giving $1,776 to the military as part of his budget.
  • Federal Reserve and Interest Rates:
      • Future Rates: Confident that interest rates "are going to be lowered" and "should" be, stating current rates are "way high."
      • Fed Chair Selection: Implied his new Fed pick understands the desire for lower rates, noting that a nominee wanting to raise rates "would not have gotten the job."
      • Independence vs. Influence: While acknowledging the Fed is "in theory... an independent body," he states he knows "the economy better than almost everybody" and implies his influence is expected.
  • Government Spending and Employment:
      • Federal Workers: Claims to have "gotten rid of hundreds of thousands of federal workers," contrasting this with Democrats who "hire 2 million people."
      • Employment Claim: States "more jobs right now occupied... than at any time during its existence, 250 years."

Domestic Politics and Election Integrity

  • Election Corruption Allegations:
      • Local Corruption: Did not use "nationalized," but asserts "extremely corrupt" elections in specific areas like Detroit, Philadelphia, and Atlanta.
      • Voter ID: Argues Democrats oppose voter ID because "they want to cheat on elections," despite claiming "Democrat voters want it."
      • Federal Controls: Advocates for federal controls over elections if states fail to ensure honesty, proper, and timely processes.
  • Midterm Elections:
      • Trust in Results: Will trust midterm results "if the elections are honest."
      • Past Election Claims: Reiterates belief of "cheating" in past elections, despite claiming to have won "all seven swing states" and 84% of counties.
  • Fulton County FBI Investigation:
      • Warrant: Notes an FBI investigation in Fulton County, Georgia, regarding election ballots, operating under a court order signed by a "liberal judge."
      • Purpose: Believes the investigation will "find out the true winner of that state" and expose past "cheating."
  • Foreign Election Interference:
      • China: Believes China attempts to influence U.S. elections.
      • Russia: Dismisses claims of Russian interference as a "hoax," attributing it to Hunter Biden.
  • Legal Battles and Political Targeting:
      • IRS Lawsuit: Suing the IRS for $10.5 billion over leaked tax documents, pledging to donate any winnings to charity.
      • Mar-a-Lago Raid: Claims to have won a lawsuit regarding the "illegal" FBI raid on Mar-a-Lago, calling it a break-in by "very smart, radical left people."
      • Impeachments and Indictments: Highlights having been impeached twice ("fake impeachments") and indicted "87 different times," equating to "287 years" in prison if he had lost.
      • DOJ's Role: While he is the "Chief Law Enforcement Officer," he states he doesn't "choose to" be involved in prosecuting political enemies, but suggests that the intense scrutiny he faced justifies such actions by others.
      • Bill Clinton & Epstein: Expresses a surprising positive sentiment towards Bill Clinton and called the attempts to subpoena him regarding Epstein a "shame," noting Democrats might retaliate by targeting him if Clinton is pursued.

AI and Foreign Policy Engagements

  • Artificial Intelligence (AI):
      • Impact: Considers AI "a big deal," potentially "bigger than the Internet," with vast implications.
      • U.S. Leadership: Claims the U.S. is "leading China by a lot" and "leading everybody by a lot" in AI development.
      • Economic Opportunity: Predicts AI will be the "greatest jobs producer," "greatest military producer," and "greatest medical producer," dismissing concerns about job losses.
      • Energy Infrastructure: Proposes that new AI and auto plants will build their "own electric plant" to meet demand, as the country will need "twice the electric."
  • China Relations:
      • Positive Relationship: Describes a "very good relationship" with President Xi, with plans for reciprocal visits (Trump to China in April, Xi to White House late year).
      • Discussions: Confirms discussions on the economy and tariffs.
  • Iran Policy:
      • Threat Assessment: Warns Iran's Supreme Leader "should be very worried" due to U.S. actions.
      • Nuclear Program: Claims his administration "wiped out their nuclear" program, leading to "peace in the Middle East."
      • Military Action: States B-2 bombers "obliterated" targets, preventing a nuclear weapon within a month. Threatens renewed force if Iran attempts to restart its nuclear program at new sites.
  • Cuba Situation:
      • Crisis: States Cuba is "in big trouble" and needs "sanitarian aid," citing the cessation of financial support from Venezuela.
      • Engagement: Confirms the U.S. is "talking to Cuba" to explore options for Cuban-Americans to visit their loved ones.
      • Political Support: Notes strong support from Cuban-American voters (94%).

Presidential Legacy and Personal Reflections

  • White House Projects:
      • Oval Office Decor: Enhanced Oval Office with historical presidential portraits and the Declaration of Independence.
      • Building for Legacy: Overseeing construction of a new White House Ballroom, a "Triumphal Arc" taller than Paris' Arc de Triomphe, and a "Garden of Heroes."
      • Purpose: Aims to bring "glamor, prestige, beauty, and strength" back to the country and cement his legacy.
      • Security Features: The new ballroom will feature "drone-proof ceilings" (drones bounce off) and "bulletproof windows" reflecting modern security concerns.
  • Personal Safety and Health:
      • Safety Concerns: Expresses worry about his personal safety as President, calling it "the most dangerous job there is," statistically more hazardous than race car driving or bronco riding.
      • Mental and Physical Health: States he feels "great, physically and mentally," like he did "50 years ago."
      • Cognitive Testing: Regularly takes and "aced" cognitive physicals, noting no other President has agreed to do them, and criticizes former President Biden's perceived cognitive decline.
      • Medication: Continues to take Aspirin as he has for 30 years, preferring to keep his blood "nice and thin."
      • Self-Awareness: Believes he would know if his age began to impact his ability to perform his job.

Notable Quotes

  • "We have record low crime in the United States. Nobody has been able to say that for 125 years." – *President Trump*
  • "I don't believe the polls... The polls are almost dishonest, almost as dishonest as some of the reporters themselves." – *President Trump*
  • "AI is a big deal. AI is going to be maybe the biggest thing. Bigger than the Internet, bigger than anything else." – *President Trump*
  • "Being President is the most dangerous job there is." – *President Trump*

Episode 48: Dmitry Balyasny - Managing Partner and CIO of Balyasny Asset Management

Generating Alpha Podcast(1.1K) ·52:31 ·18 views · 2026-02-05
Dmitry Balyasny
> "You really need a particular approach like this is the type of trade that I look for, right? And this is the type of risk that I take.
TL;DR Dmitry Balyasny, Founder and CIO of Balyasny Asset Management (BAM), discusses his journey from an immigrant to building a leading $30B multi-strategy hedge fund, emphasizing a disciplined business model, partnership culture, continuous talent development, and adaptable...

TL;DR Dmitry Balyasny, Founder and CIO of Balyasny Asset Management (BAM), discusses his journey from an immigrant to building a leading $30B multi-strategy hedge fund, emphasizing a disciplined business model, partnership culture, continuous talent development, and adaptable edge to generate consistent returns across market cycles.

Dmitry Balyasny's Formative Journey

  • Early Life & Immigration: Dmitry Balyasny immigrated from Kiev, Ukraine to Chicago at age 7, speaking no English. His parents, a professor and engineer, bravely restarted their lives in menial jobs, instilling resilience.
      • His personal experience involved moving from 3rd to 2nd grade due to language barriers, highlighting initial challenges.
  • Entrepreneurial Spirit: From junior high, he pursued sales jobs for commission, quickly recognizing it as a path to earning money as a kid.
  • Early Foray into Finance: Became a stockbroker in college to fund personal trading, where he initially lost all earnings and more, leveraging with credit cards due to lack of mentorship and understanding of proper trading.
      • Persistence: Despite significant early losses, his passion for markets and a belief that consistent effort with reasonable instruction could lead to competence kept him going.
  • Impact of "Atlas Shrugged": Reading Ayn Rand's "Atlas Shrugged" between high school and college deeply resonated with his views on freedom, independence, self-determination, and the moral benefit of individual self-interest and earned rewards for society.
  • Start at Schonfeld Securities (1994): Joined after persevering through numerous rejections, attracted by their willingness to teach new traders without preconceived notions. Started with no salary and minimal capital, learning disciplined trading and risk management.
      • Career Progression: Steadily improved P&L, eventually hiring and leading a team of traders and later, fundamental analysts and sector portfolio managers, before spinning off his group.

The Genesis and Evolution of Balyasny Asset Management

  • Lessons from Schonfeld:
      • Disciplined Approach: Emphasized the need for a specific, well-thought-out trading approach with defined limits, risk profiles, and continuous iteration for improvement.
      • Fund-of-Funds Experience: Allocating firm capital revealed a critical distinction: "boutique" structures centered on individual risk-takers had high turnover, while "business" models (like multi-strategy funds) offered stability, consistency, and the ability to reinvent and scale. This reinforced his vision to build a sustainable business.
  • Founding BAM (2001): Balyasny co-founded BAM with a clear conviction to build a diversified, scalable multi-strategy business, rather than a single-manager model.
      • Initial Challenges: This model was difficult to raise capital for due to its complexity compared to simpler single-manager structures, leading to significant AUM volatility in early years.
  • Key Decisions Shaping BAM:
      • Firm Architecture: Maintained unwavering conviction in the multi-strategy, multi-risk taker model, developing fund, risk, and recruiting structures around this foundation.
      • Partnership Culture: Established a strong partnership culture early on with key hires (Scott and Taylor), which now permeates the firm with 20 partners across business and investment roles. This fosters a team environment where individuals are motivated for both personal success and the firm's overall well-being.
  • Resilience Through Adversity: BAM successfully navigated challenging periods like the 2008 financial crisis (making a small profit but facing 50% redemptions) and a down year in 2018, demonstrating the strength of its culture in retaining talent and rebuilding.

Cultivating and Developing Top Investment Talent

  • Analyst vs. PM Qualities:
      • Commonality: Both require the ability to think differently, generate unique variant ideas, and balance creativity with investment process discipline.
      • Key Differences for PMs: Portfolio Managers need higher risk tolerance (innate but trainable), the ability to juggle numerous issues daily, and to balance deep dives into specific ideas with a zoomed-out view of the overall portfolio.
  • Attracting Talent: Beyond competitive compensation, BAM differentiates by offering:
      • Centralized Resources: Strategy, policy, economics research, quantitative analysis, and collaboration across diverse asset classes (equities, credit, macro, commodities, quant).
      • Supportive Coaching & Partnership: A mentality of joint venture, providing resources, coaching, and team development to ensure PM success, rather than a "good luck" approach.
  • Talent Development Programs:
      • Campus Recruiting: Extensive program for summer interns (100+ annually), with over 50% hired into various firm roles.
      • Analyst Training: Primarily a "Jedi relationship" with their PM, supplemented by central off-sites, panels, and recorded sessions sharing diverse experiences and techniques.
      • PM Training: For high-performing analysts/traders, allocating capital and teaching team/risk management aspects they may lack.
  • Uncoachable Aspects: Innate risk personality and passion for the business are difficult to fundamentally change; screening for these is crucial.
  • Culture & Returns: A strong culture is vital for both defense and offense.
      • Defensively: Enables the firm to weather difficult periods without significant talent loss or permanent impairment.
      • Offensively: Amplifies returns through seamless collaboration, sharing insights, and fostering a "flywheel" effect across teams and strategies.
  • Communication in a Specialized Model: BAM's specialized structure, where teams have distinct mandates with minimal overlap, allows for open communication without excessive unintended correlation or groupthink. Diverse perspectives from PMs often lead to different conclusions, and shared convictions are seen as strong signals.

The Evolving Edge in Hedge Funds and Leadership at BAM

  • Raising Quality Capital: Dmitry highlights the rigorous and lengthy due diligence processes of large, high-quality capital allocators (e.g., a 17-year process for a sovereign wealth fund).
      • Challenge of Maintaining Capital: It's relatively easy to raise *some* capital, but maintaining and growing it through market ups and downs requires a steadfast edge and strong culture.
  • Defining "Edge": Edge depends on an individual's skill set, which could be:
      • A great manager, recruiter, risk manager, and capital allocator who attracts talent.
      • A great trader/PM with acumen in a specific area, supported by a strong team and infrastructure.
      • Problems arise when a firm's structure doesn't match its core edge or the evolving skill set of its leadership.
  • Dmitry Balyasny's Day-to-Day as CEO/CIO:
      • Market & Risk Focus: Daily engagement with market volatility, risk management, and capital allocation, especially during busy periods (e.g., earnings, commodity volatility).
      • Strategy Development: Extensive time dedicated to planning for the firm's future across different strategy verticals, including competition analysis, infrastructure needs, and scaling.
      • Infrastructure: Involved in technology and trading systems improvements to enable strategy execution.
      • Recruiting: Consistently conducts an average of "a couple interviews every day" for PMs, business managers, and senior department roles, crucial for growth.
  • Advice to a 16-Year-Old (Carpe Diem): "Seize the day." Balance what you want to do with what you have to do, creating space for passionate pursuits and setting yourself up for a future free of regret.

Notable Quotes

"You really need a particular approach like this is the type of trade that I look for, right? And this is the type of risk that I take. Here's my limits... and have like prescribed method to the madness." — Dmitry Balyasny "If the main thing that you're offering is a, you know, sign on or a guarantee uh check uh like that's not like really compelling because it is fairly commoditized... you really have to differentiate in my opinion, you know, beyond that." — Dmitry Balyasny "If you don't really love what you're doing, you're not excited, you know, you're you're going to kind of like either actually give up or psychologically kind of kind of go through the motions but not really, you know, do everything that you can." — Dmitry Balyasny "A lot of it is uh you know market oriented, risk oriented, capital allocation oriented... The other big part is um strategy development." — Dmitry Balyasny
This week on Generating Alpha, I sat down with Dmitri Balazni, founder, managing partner, and chief investment officer of Balazni Asset Management, one of the most respected multi-strategy hedge funds in the world. Dmitri immigrated to the United States from Kiev, Ukraine at age seven, speaking no English. He worked as a door-to-door salesman at 12, became a stockbroker in college, and started his trading career at Schonfeld Securities in 1994. In 2001, he co-founded Balazni Asset Management, which has grown to over $30 billion in assets under management with 2,300 employees across 23 global offices. Beyond building one of the industry's leading multi-manager platforms, Dmitri is the only founder among the big four multi-strat funds who still actively manages a book. He is also deeply committed to expanding opportunity, founding Atlas Fellows in 2021 to provide under-resourced young people with pathways into finance and investing. In our conversation, we talked about immigrating to America and learning to trade, the formative impact of *Atlas Shrugged*, losing everything his first year as a broker and why he persisted, what he learned at Schonfeld about building great hedge funds, the critical decisions that shaped BAM's evolution, what separates great analysts from great PMs, how culture translates into returns, the changing nature of edge, and much more. If you enjoy this episode, please follow the podcast and rate it five stars on Spotify. Subscribe on YouTube and share it with anyone who you think might find it valuable. I really enjoyed recording this with Dmitri, and I hope you enjoyed listening. Thank you. Thanks, Dmitri, for joining me. It has been a long time in the making. I really appreciate it. "Thanks, Mir. Nice to be here." Well, I want to start where I always do: at the beginning. You immigrated from the United States to Kiev when you were seven, I believe. "Other way around." Oh, Kiev to the United States when you were seven. "You do not want to immigrate the other way." Speaking no English, tell me a little bit about your childhood, how you think that experience shaped you, and what it was like growing up. "It was tough for me, but it was really tough for my parents, and they are the ones that certainly get all the credit for having the courage to make the move. To give some perspective for folks who might not know what was involved at the time, when you submitted your papers to leave the country and immigrate in the former Soviet Union, you were immediately ostracized and became persona non grata in the country. So my parents immediately lost their jobs and had to live off of meager savings and gradually selling off personal possessions as you tried to sort out everything you needed to make the move. Today, we have such a preponderance of information about everything with the internet and AI. At the time, it was just a void. You had really no idea, besides hearsay, of what was outside of the Soviet borders. So they really took a leap of faith, and fortunately it worked out. We immigrated to Chicago and I grew up there. My parents had never driven a car. They had to figure out how to get a driver's license, learn English, and start over in very menial jobs, going from working as a professor and an engineer to cleaning motel rooms until they got their feet under them. We slowly made our way out of the city into the suburbs and so on. For me personally, it was strange. On the one hand, it was amazingly exciting because you saw all these things that you had never seen before, whether it was flying on an airplane—which my kids cannot believe I had never flown on an airplane before that—or really driving in cars, which did not happen very often, to going to a grocery store and actually having it filled with stuff that you could just buy. These were all very novel experiences. But from a personal standpoint, on the other side, starting over with no English—I came when I was about seven, going to be eight soon. I was put into third grade, but I could not speak any English, and after six months in third grade, they moved me down to second grade. Then, the following year, I got the hang of English a little bit and got caught up, and then I went to fourth grade. So I still never really finished third grade. If I say anything really dumb during the podcast, that is the reason." I think you missed a little bit of division there. I also think a moment that seems very formative for you is when you read *Atlas Shrugged* for the first time in college, if I am correct, and it seems to have been very formative. Tell me about that time in college and then reading that book. What did that bring to light? "Yes, I think it was right between my senior year of high school and freshman year of college. So it also lined up, from a formative standpoint, with going to college; you are looking for new ideas, open to different perspectives. At least for me, I was hungry for knowledge, not factual knowledge, but broader wisdom and knowledge. I was always interested in philosophy and reading different philosophical type books. *Atlas Shrugged* had sat on my bookshelf for a couple of years prior to that, and I was intimidated by the length and did not really know what it was about. So it took me a while to motivate myself to start. But once I started reading it, I could not put it down. I think the best way I could put it is that Ayn Rand put into very eloquent writing a lot of moral and philosophical thoughts that I had bouncing around in my head, where I could not articulate them the same way; I could not put them together into a coherent philosophy. But it immediately resonated with me as far as freedom, independence, self-determination, working for your rewards, and all of that being not just a cost of doing business to keep the world moving, but actually the right moral way to do things that was beneficial for society as a whole as well as the individual working in their own self-interest. So, it really resonated with me, and I would definitely recommend it for folks to check out." I have been intimidated by it for a few years now. I think I will pick it up after this podcast. During your time in college, if I am correct, you became a broker because ultimately you wanted to become a trader, but the broker was the first step to doing that. You made some money from commissions being a broker, and if I am correct, you did not make any money in the end because you traded them and lost a lot of the money. "Yes, I was definitely not up. The career trajectory was, I did some sales starting in junior high and then throughout high school, different types of sales jobs, because I pretty quickly figured out that was the only way you could get paid any money as a kid; you had to earn commissions. Podcasting had not been invented yet. I was not smart enough to start a VC fund. So I did different types of sales. As I was looking to get into finance and trading, it was natural that I could get a foot in the door through sales, which is what being a stockbroker was at the time. You got a stack of leads to call and people you were trying to get to open accounts and do some trades or investments with the firm. I was pretty good at that, and I pretty quickly had a number of other young people working under my umbrella. We were doing very well for our age in terms of generating revenues, but I was really interested in trading, so I was taking all the commission earnings that I was making and using that to fund my personal trading account. There I had no idea what I was doing, so I was losing that and more because I was young and silly and leveraged that via credit cards. So that was my early version of prime brokerage, which was not the way to do it. I did not know what I was doing, and so I was losing more than I was making and got to a spot where I really figured out that I needed to get some formal mentorship to really figure out how to trade properly." And then you saw the only ad that Schonfeld has ever run in a newspaper, if I am correct. I am also just interested, first of all, tell me about your start at Schonfeld and your trajectory there before spinning out to BAM. I would love to hear that. But even before that, most people who lost a lot of money trading early on might have been, "Okay, this is not for me." Why did you keep on going? "Well, one, I really loved it. I was doing it not just to make money. I was doing it because I was really passionate about trading and investing and markets and companies, and I was always very interested in it. So, that was one. Two, I had never really been naturally good at anything. So I have had to pretty much work at anything that I become competent at. I usually have to spend a lot of effort to get there." I can relate. "Right. And so, I figured because I had that backbone from working my way up in other things, whether it was sales or sports or whatever, I figured if I really loved it, it is not rocket science. If I worked at it hard enough and got some reasonable instruction, I could eventually figure it out step by step. So that is where that confidence came from." And tell me about that. How did you get started at Schonfeld, and you had a very interesting career trajectory there, all the way from starting just yourself, then you ended up running a team and even on the fund-of-funds side? How did those steps lead up to spinning out of that and starting BAM? "Yes, I guess first off, just the perseverance in getting the job, I think, is important. At the time, there were not many different hedge funds to talk to, certainly not in Chicago, and I did not really have any experience that was useful for them to be interested. So I cold-called and sent resumes and worked with as many as I could find. I had a very large stack of rejection letters. But I would go through the paper every week, looking for ads for trading positions. Back in the day, that is amazingly what people did. If I had not been doing that, I would not have actually seen the one ad that ran at the time. So that was helpful. They were looking for people that did not have a lot of trading experience but had a little bit of market knowledge. They wanted to teach them the approach that they thought worked and not have people come in with preconceived notions. So I was very clear that I had no idea what worked, and I wanted to learn. I was going to work hard. And so I was hired into the training program. We had no compensation, no salary. Today there are lots of signing bonuses and multi-year guarantees and all this and that. We got free lunch, and that was it. Then you got a percentage of the profits that you generated from your trading account, but you had a very small pool of capital to start because the firm was trading its own capital, and it made sense to start people with a small amount of capital when they did not know what they were doing. So the first year I did not make anything there, and there were some people there that did not make anything for years and eventually dropped out. But I persevered and kept asking questions and kept slowly improving and learning different disciplines and types of trading and types of risk management that worked, initially in equities and then broadening it out to macro, et cetera. After the first year, I steadily started improving and consistently putting up solid P&L. As that grew, I started hiring people to work under my profit and loss center. Initially, I hired traders who I basically taught what I knew and gave them some responsibility to take a portion of my risk in different sectors. Then I started hiring analysts, five or six years into it. We were the first group in the firm to hire fundamental analysts, which was an unusual thing to do in the nineties because it was such a momentum market. But I thought that was not going to last forever, and if I wanted to keep scaling and growing, we needed to do fundamental work and hold positions longer. So we started doing that, and then that morphed into hiring sector portfolio managers where we gave them more capital to manage under my umbrella. Then that became the genesis of our trading group, and as we grew and expanded, we wound up spinning it off into a division and then into a separate fund entity that we capitalized." And I am interested in what you learned at Schonfeld, both from building a team over the years, but also, if I am correct, you were on the fund-of-funds side for a bit and got to interact with some of the top funds at the time. What did you learn there about what makes a great hedge fund, and how did you apply those principles to the beginnings of BAM? "Yes, that is a good question. On the trading side, I would say having a disciplined, specific approach. The markets have lots of things going on all the time. There are a million different types of trading and investing that you could do in different asset classes, different holding periods: long, short, neutral, investing, trading. So there are a million different things you could do. Generally, people do not really have a particular, well-thought-out approach. They do something, it works, they do a little more of it, it does not work, they do something else, they try this, they try that. That is what I was doing before. You really need a particular approach: this is the type of trade that I look for, and this is the type of risk that I take. Here are my limits. Here is what I am trying to make, here is what I am limiting myself to lose, and have a prescribed method to the madness. You start with a fairly small box. When we start new portfolio managers who had not run money before, we try to make the box fairly tight. When I started, it was really tight because we really did not have much experience. Today, the people we have starting off in a PM training program already have a lot of experience, so they have a much wider box. But you need that box so that it says, 'Here is the stuff that I think I have an edge in this particular portion of the market. Here is how long I typically hold positions. Here is the factor risk I want to take or not take. Here is the concentration, the liquidity, how much I am trying to make, how much I am willing to lose as a drawdown.' So you define all this stuff, and then as you get competent, the more success you have, you gradually widen out that box. Sometimes you grow it, and then you have a drawdown, something changes in the market, and you have to shrink it again and go back to your basics a little bit. But over time, that is how you develop, and then you are constantly looking for improvements in your approach, not just what happened with this particular trade, but how can I use what I learned so that my next thousand trades are better? So, you are constantly iterating a little bit. From the fund-of-funds side, I was allocating a firm account that Steve Schonfeld capitalized, and I co-invested with a couple of other guys. I was the fund-of-fund analyst. I went around and met a bunch of different funds to help pick where we should be invested. That was a really interesting experience. I would say the main thing that was really reinforced to me that was helpful was that there is a huge difference between two types of funds. One is a boutique structure that is structured around an individual risk-taker in a particular strategy." Yes. "And that is the vast majority of hedge funds. And then there is a business. It could be a proprietary trading business, like a Jane Street or HRT, or it could be a hedge fund with a lot of different risk-takers. That is a completely different DNA from the individual-centered model. What I saw, as we were allocating the portfolio, was split between the two models. We had a lot of different funds. The single manager model had a tremendous amount of turnover. Some did great, then they did not do great, and the manager would want to do something else or lose motivation or had some business issues. But it was very rare that we were invested with the same guys for 10 or 20 years. In that part of the book, they just turned over a lot. Versus the diversified funds, a lot of them ended up being the same ones for decades, or they would spin off, and one would become two, but it was the same basic DNA. The reason for that is that type of firm can reinvent itself. You can expand different strategies, some work, some do not, you change the mix of strategies, you can allocate capital to the areas of the market where you think there are better opportunities, you can refresh your investing teams as people develop and get better or not over time. So it is just a much more stable and consistent business model. So that reinforced that that was the way that I wanted to go in really building out our business." Yes, you wanted to build a business and a legacy rather than something more fleeting. "Yes, I just wanted to build a real business. So whether I had a good year trading or not, I wanted the firm to do well. I wanted our investors to do well, and I wanted to do well in all market environments. Some are great for my style of trading, some are not. I wanted it to be a consistent, scalable business." Yes. I would love to hear a little bit about, before we touch on talent and different topics, I would love to hear a bit about, from a high-level perspective, how BAM has evolved over the past 25 years. I would love to hear what you think were the most important decisions that led to it becoming what it is today. "Well, I think the first one is just what I was talking about: what is the architecture? What are you trying to build? A lot of times when people start funds, they start from the standpoint of how much money can I raise? What will people invest in? You obviously have to think about that, but that should not be the genesis of what you are trying to build, because investor appetites change, and they certainly will follow returns. You need something that you really believe in over good periods, bad periods, that you have a particular edge in and you know how to do, and architect the firm towards that, regardless if it is easy or harder to raise money for it to start. The reason I say that is this model was really hard to raise money for. We had a lot of ups and downs over the years in AUM before we got to the real critical mass and a really solid investor base. That took a long time, and the reason for it is it is a complicated model for an investor to understand. If you compare it to an individual single-manager PM coming in and saying, 'Here is my track record. You are investing with me. I am great at XYZ. I have this team. We have five guys. We worked together for 10 years. Simple fee structure, simple risk. Here it is.' Versus we come in and we are like, 'Well, the PMs change. We have a hundred different people; today we have 200 different PMs. There are a couple thousand people, all these different strategies; they are going to evolve over time. Everything is complicated.' So it takes investors a long time to get comfortable with that. But really having conviction that that is the model that I wanted to pursue was super important because that conviction gets tested. A lot of people want to do a particular model, and then when times get tough, they are like, 'Maybe I will do something else.' We really architected that we wanted to build a really diverse, scalable business, a real multi-risk or multi-strategy, and we stuck with it. We created a fund structure, risk structure, recruiting structure, everything built around that foundation. So, I would say that is the first critical thing because it is very hard to veer from a single manager to then say, 'Okay, I am going to have lots of different risk-takers, and now we are going to do multi-manager.' That is very, very hard. So I think that has been critical to success, just that steadfastness. Then a partnership culture is really important. I was fortunate enough to hire Scott and Taylor as two partners very early on, and we started the business together. So we have been together for 25 years, and that partnership culture permeates the firm. Now we have 20 partners, both strategy heads on the business side, department heads, and portfolio managers as well. So that really feeds the team-type environment where people want the firm to do well and are rooting for the overall firm, in addition to obviously being very motivated and the economics that they get from generating P&L on their own portion of capital that they are managing. So I think combining those two things is very hard to do, and most firms are kind of one or the other, and we have always believed you could be both. You could have individual incentives, and you could have firm incentives and firm partnership, and those things really reinforce each other. So I think those are the main ones."
