Thank you. And back to the episode. Yes. And I would love to touch on the topic of people. I think obviously what makes these firms enduring is a consistent flow of great talent. So I am interested, just to start off, what makes a great analyst and what makes a great PM, and how do those differ? "Yes, good question. What makes a great analyst, and I think this is similar to a great PM, is you have to be able to think differently. You have to come up with unique, variant ideas, so differing views on things that lots of other people are constantly looking at. You might not have a differing view necessarily for the next five years, but you should be able to come up with a consistent flow of differing views for the next few months, maybe the next year, maybe the next couple of years in some cases. So that takes a particular type of wiring where you are constantly looking at the other side of the coin and what might cause people to come around to your view and then digging on that. So the balance of creativity and investment process discipline is common for both analysts, and you need that for PMs as well. A lot of times people that are really creative and come up with brilliant ideas do not have process discipline. They might fall in love with one particular idea. They have a hard time running models on a hundred different companies. You need both things. So that is common. I think the thing that is different is at a PM level, you certainly need more risk tolerance, where you are comfortable with volatility, and some of that is innate and some of it can be learned. You need the ability to juggle lots of different balls. As an analyst, the great thing about being an analyst is you can go super deep on a particular thing and not worry about anything else for some period of time. If it is a big enough idea, you can raise your hand, talk to your PM, and say, 'Listen, I think we should spend the next week, I am just going to work on this.' And if you agree that this is a big potential thing, 'I am going to spend 90% of my time digging on this until we either prove or disprove this thesis because I think this could be a really big bet.' As a PM, you do not have that luxury. Every day there is something blowing up in your book. Hopefully, there is something well going on. There is some macro problem, somebody tweeted something. There are constantly 50 different things that you are juggling. So the ability to do deep work and dig in on something but also to zoom out and look at the overall portfolio and constantly balance those things, that is a unique PM skill." And on the topic of talent, in recent years, a lot of people have seen these big headlines of hedge funds paying up for big talent. But I do think that at a point, the marginal compensation does not really matter as much as the type of firm you will be going to. An extra million dollars on a $100 million contract is not going to matter as much as the firm. So my question to you regarding that is how do you uniquely position yourselves to attract the best talent as a firm? "Yes. So I think once you are at the level economically where you can compete, and realistically 99% of funds do not really compete in this pool for actual portfolio managers running meaningful books and getting paid on their performance because they do not have the economic ability and scale and structure to compete for that. So once you are past that and you are competing with other large firms in that space, if the main thing that you are offering is a sign-on or a guarantee check, that is not really compelling because it is fairly commoditized. If you are a strong PM, you have a good track record, you are a good team manager, you are a good risk manager, maybe somebody needs that particular coverage area more than somebody else, but in general, you are going to get paid very well if you go to any of the top firms. So you really have to differentiate, in my opinion, beyond that. You see this all the time in sports, and I do think it is somewhat similar. Athletes certainly want security and to get paid for their talent, rightly so. But you also frequently see them taking a smaller check or certainly a smaller guarantee to go on a winning team, or to go play with guys where they want to be on that team or play for a particular coach, and have the ability to bet on themselves and bet on that team, and then if it works out, they will hopefully end up doing even better financially. You see that all the time. So I do not think it is that different. So we try, beyond the basic economics of being one of the larger places that can offer that, beyond that, can you offer the central resources that they might not have somewhere? The strategy research, policy research, economics research, collaboration across equities, credit, macro, commodities, quant—all these areas where guys are constantly talking to each other and comparing notes of what is going on in the macro, what is going on in the market, what is working. Having great QR functions that work with the teams to optimize their risk-taking, to give them suggestions statistically on what works, what does not work for them, for others. And then just having a very supportive coaching mentality and a partnership mentality where we are going into this venture together. We are both making a bet, just like venture capital, we are both making a bet, and we are going to do everything possible to make the team successful. Not to say that if somebody loses money for years, we are not going to give up and make a change; we have to be disciplined on that, we will. But before that, we are going to do everything possible to make the team successful in terms of resources, coaching, team development, anything that we can do to help them get to where they want to go, as opposed to, 'Here is your capital, here is your risk, good luck. If it works out, great. If not, we will get somebody else.'" And on the topic of playing for a good coach, there is this idea of coaching and developing talent. Over the past year, I have gotten to know you guys and your campus recruiting program quite well, but there is obviously the campus recruiting program and then all the way up to the PM coaching program. From a high level, how do you internally develop talent from analysts to PMs? And what cannot be coached is also a question I have. "I think risk is tough to coach. I think people have an innate risk personality, and I think you can influence it, but it is hard to move it a lot. So you just have to go into it with eyes wide open that somebody that is super conservative, you are never going to get them to really be aggressive. And somebody that is super aggressive, you are never going to get them to be conservative. So wherever they are, you can help modulate them a little bit, and hopefully you can help the offensive person play a little better defense and the defensive person play a little bit more aggressively when opportunities are there. You are not going to hugely move them. So that is an innate psychology thing that you have to be comfortable with in the screening process. We look for passion a lot: is the person super excited to be in this business, in this firm, in this role, because these are hard? There are huge ups and downs. There are miserable periods when nothing is working, you feel like the biggest idiot in the world, you cannot put two cents together. Everyone goes through those periods. And if you do not really love what you are doing, you are not excited, you are going to either actually give up or psychologically go through the motions but not really do everything that you can. So those are a couple of things that we do. The different programs: we have everything starting from a college recruiting program where we have kids come in from all kinds of top schools and investment clubs and math programs, and they will go through a summer internship that is structured, and we will typically hire or make offers to about 65% of those kids. We end up hiring over 50% of the interns every year. I think this last year we had over 100 interns. I think this next year is probably going to be 130, 140 globally. So that is fun, exciting, and they take jobs throughout the firm: some in technology, some in risk, some in investing, some in trading. Then you have analyst training programs. As an analyst, the majority of your training should come from your portfolio manager. It is a bit of a Jedi relationship that you are signing up for. So you have to like and respect your Jedi and think that that is somebody you could learn a lot from, and vice versa. And that is the primary one, but in addition to that, we try to do a lot of stuff centrally because we have a lot of Jedi. So you can pick up different things from different people, different strengths, different techniques, different strategies. And so we will do central things, whether it is an off-site where different PMs will be on panels talking to the analysts, dinners, in-office gatherings, all sorts of stuff where some formal, informal, where somebody will talk about their experience, examples, and you go through this formal knowledge transfer, not just from the direct person you are working for, but throughout the firm. Then we try to put all these in a recorded format so people can get the benefit of all the sessions that have been done prior, and so you have this amazing encyclopedia that you could go through and hopefully learn from other people's mistakes and avoid having to make all of them yourself. Then you have the PM training program where somebody who is typically a great analyst or a great trader already, and now wants to move up to the PM seat, and there we will allocate capital to them, help them build out the team, and work to teach them the portions of being a PM that they might not know. So they might be great at picking stocks, but maybe they have not managed a team before, maybe they have not managed risk before. So there is a lot of both formal and informal training around those." And I picked up on something you said, which is when you were describing the departments, you said there is investing and there is trading. A lot of people see the overlap of investing and trading as the same thing. What is the difference between investing and trading to you? "Well, it is a little bit technical, and it depends a bit on the strategy. In macro, for example, when you say somebody is a good trader, it is typically the same thing as a good portfolio manager; they are running. Portfolio tends to be a very trading-oriented strategy. When you look at equities, they are different titles. So somebody today who is a trader in equities, they are typically on the trading desk, working orders, helping optimize execution, helping the portfolio manager trade the portfolio. And the portfolio manager is typically a little bit more fundamental, usually started off as an analyst, although we have some that started off as traders, and they are digging through the research with the analyst trying to pick the best investment ideas. So, it depends a little bit on the strategy." And you cannot talk about people without talking about culture. So, how does culture translate all the way from the culture in the building to returns for your investors? "I think it is all important. So many examples. One is, when you go through a really difficult period, if you do not have a strong culture internally, often businesses fold, or if they do not fold, they get pretty permanently impaired. You see that a lot, whether it is public companies, and you trade their stock, and you look at how companies recover from difficult periods or do not, and the list of top companies is hugely different every decade. Hedge funds, if you look at the top funds 10 years ago, 20 years ago, there is not a lot of overlap. There is some, typically like the multi-strategy firms, a few others, but there is huge change. A big reason for that is there is not enough that people are buying into beyond their current job for most funds. If you are tied a little bit more to the business and you really want the firm to do well, in addition to obviously wanting to progress in your own job and do great, when you want the person next to you to do well and the firm to do well, the whole thing is just much more solid, a lot more cohesive, and it is a lot stronger and able to get through all the ups and downs. So, whether you look at a period like 2008, where we ended up making a little bit of money, but we had 50% redemptions because people needed cash from other places, and we paid it out. We had a big headcount and had to cough up the difference from the management company. Oftentimes when people want to start funds, they are always talking about how much they are going to make on management fees. 90% of the time we lose money on management fees, and a couple of those years we had to put up a lot of money on the management fee side to get the business through it. But because of the culture, we did not lose many people. Same thing, we had one down year in 2018 out of 25 years. Again, we lost a lot of AUM, had to do a lot of restructuring. We lost a few people, but not many. I think most firms, if you do not have a strong culture, it is very hard to get through that. Another side of the coin is the offensive side. When things are going well, I think culture also really helps you amplify the returns, because if you do not really care how the person next to you does or how the overall firm does, you are not really going to collaborate with them to make them better, whether it is an investing insight that you could share that does not cost you anything but will help them. Or it is a process insight, your analyst is unhappy, 'I heard him talking by the water cooler. Maybe you should take him out to dinner and see if you guys get on the same page.' It could be a process insight on, 'Here is this new trick I learned in AI that is super useful for my investment process, maybe you should try this.' There are a million things like that every day, but you need a culture for that to permeate the firm, and when it does, and you can permeate that across teams, across asset classes, across strategies, everyone just becomes much better faster. You get the flywheel going. So I think it is super important, both defensively and offensively." And I am interested in this aspect of communication because there are some multi-managers that infamously are known to be very not communicative. So you seem to have a very communicative culture. Why did you choose to have that, and what do you think your upside to that is? "So there is no perfect system. There is the right thing for different firms of what you are trying to accomplish, but it has to fit with the architecture of the firm. So, to me, if you have a generalist mandate type of place where people can trade a lot of different things and there is a lot of overlap between what different people are doing, in that case, you really have to be very careful about the communication because you could end up with a lot of unintended correlation. So that is one model. We have always driven towards a specialized model. So we do not have very many pockets where there is a lot of overlap. You will have people that have a particular equity sector or geography that they trade, or a particular macro mandate that they trade, a particular commodity product that they trade. And we do not hire many, many people to trade the same types of things. We do a lot of analysis of what is the alpha pool in this particular strategy, how many teams do we need to cover that, what is the rough optimum number? And then we adjust that over time. So you want to get to a spot where there is enough coverage to pick up a lot of the alpha at scale, but not so much that you are creating a lot of friction and preventing people from collaborating. If you have that, and you do not have any particular team managing too much of the overall risk and having too much overlap with anybody else, then I think the risks of them talking to each other are much lower than the benefits you get from that. There is a preconception that if three PMs talk about an idea, they are all going to agree and have groupthink and go do the same thing. Most of the time, what actually happens is they will end up arguing for an hour and have three completely different views, and if they could have three different positions, they will have three different positions. And if they all actually do agree on something and come at it from different research and different styles and come to the same conclusion, very often that is a pretty good signal that you have a good idea. So I think the benefits of it really outweigh the downside with the type of firm architecture that we have, and it certainly has helped our returns over time." And I am very interested in this idea of, if I wanted to raise a hedge fund and I went out today and I pitched a sovereign wealth fund or I pitched a pension, one of the questions they would probably ask me is, "What is your edge?" How have you seen the nature of edge within the hedge fund industry change over the last two decades as a whole? What has it become? "Well, first of all, I think there is different quality, I would say, of capital that you can raise. When you say, 'I want to go pitch a sovereign wealth fund,' to give you a sense, we had a sovereign wealth fund invest fairly recently, and the first time I met with them was 17 years ago. So that was the due diligence period. Now, somebody else might be a lot smarter than me, maybe it will not take them 17 years, but the high-quality, large pools of capital are pretty rigorous on what they want to see and how long they want to see it for before they start writing meaningful checks most of the time. So that is one of the things that makes starting hedge funds very difficult: the quality of the capital that you typically get starting off, and certainly a year, two years, three years into it, it is very volatile. So if you go through a difficult period, it comes back to that culture and edge. Can you maintain it? It is much less about, 'Can you raise the capital?' It is not that hard to raise some capital, but maintaining it and growing it through the ups and downs, that is what is hard. In terms of what edge to demonstrate, there are different approaches, and it really depends on the person's skill set. So somebody could be a great manager; they are a great recruiter, great manager, great risk manager, capital allocator, and they have an ability to attract talent to them in the space that they really know well. So that is one model. Another model is somebody is a great trader PM in a particular area, and people are going to invest with them for their acumen in that particular thing, and they surround themselves with a strong team and strong infrastructure for that particular thing and get the right amount of capital for the capacity that they can generate good returns on and try to maintain and slowly grow from that. I think people run into problems when they veer from that core edge, and the structure of the firm does not match with their skill set as much. So if somebody is a really good risk-taker in a particular thing, that does not necessarily make them a great talent manager, recruiter, business builder. They might be, but most of the time they are not. So you have to match that up. I think that is really important, especially as the firm evolves, and if that person does not have it, then bringing in people that really have a different skill set, and that is one of the areas that I was fortunate in, and we have always invested a lot in bringing in top management and continuing to really do that, get things going, and then bring in folks to take it to the next level." And I think a lot of people have this question in their minds: people know what analysts do, people do not know what PMs do, but people do not really know what the heads of these large multi-manager hedge funds do. So my question to you, before I ask the final question I ask every guest, is, as the CEO and CIO of a $30 billion hedge fund, what does a day in the life of Dmitri look like? "Well, today was a very busy earnings day. So we are dealing with all sorts of earnings volatility and trading pretty actively, talking to the teams about different ideas. The last few weeks there has been crazy volatility in the commodities market. So I spent a lot of time talking to the management team, the risk team, the PMs about what is going on in their space, how can we manage through it well, and hopefully add or at least not subtract value through that process, figure out where you need to protect capital and where you need to lean in on stuff. So a lot of it is market-oriented, risk-oriented, capital allocation-oriented. So that is every day. Then the other big part is strategy development. We split our business up into different strategy verticals, and for each one, we spent a lot of time thinking about where are we today, where do we want to be tomorrow, where do we want to be three, four, five years from now, how do we get there? How do you compete? How do you win? Where can you build an edge? What kind of infrastructure do you need to build to get to the next level? Who can scale? How do we resource them for that? Where do we need to hire? So there is a lot of strategy development, and that takes some business acumen but also market acumen to try to decipher between things that might be fleeting and things that might be enduring strategies that you want to invest in." So, like trades versus businesses, we spent a lot of time on that in investment committee and in conversations with the management team and senior PMs. The infrastructure piece is increasingly important. I was in a technology meeting earlier, a trading technology meeting, discussing our trading systems, how we want to improve them, the targets we want to enable for various strategies, and what we're doing about it. Some time is spent on that. Another large piece is recruiting – portfolio managers, business managers, and senior department folks in different areas. I would say I do an average of probably a couple interviews every day. In some parts of the year, it could be much more than that. Even if I'm not the one doing the interview, I'm triangulating with the hiring manager for that role and our business development team, figuring out if this sounds like the right person, what we're looking for, and how we get this done. That's a super important part of the business if you want to grow. I want to finish off by asking my final question, which I ask every guest. I'm 16 right now. If you were to give one piece of advice to a 16-year-old today, it can be life advice, career advice. I'm pretty sure Jeff Yas gave me romantic advice. What would yours be? Oh yeah, I heard that one. That's pretty good, actually. That's good advice. Yeah, what would yours be? Any kind. I think one that I think about a lot is Carpe Diem, seize the day. You have to constantly have a balance between things that you want to be doing and things that you have to be doing, and that never goes away. I'm not saying don't do anything that you don't absolutely want to do at that moment. There are lots of things you have to do to get to where you want to get to. But making sure you're spending enough time doing the things that you really want to do and creating space for that. Then thinking ahead about what you really want to do, setting yourself up, and doing the things you have to do to get there, so that you don't spend too many hours or years doing things that you look back on later as a waste. Live with no regrets. Yes. Well, it's been a pleasure, Demetri. I'm glad we could make this happen. Thanks for coming on. My pleasure. Let's chat with him.

The Rapid Rise Of The Two Billion Dollar Collegiate NIL Marketplace

Forbes(2.1M) ·16:35 ·609 views · 2026-02-05
Steve Denton
> "Last year they made two billion dollars and by the end of the decade they're going to be making six.
TL;DR The collegiate Name, Image, and Likeness (NIL) market has rapidly grown from zero to $2 billion in five years, projected to reach $6 billion by decade's end, with Opendorse CEO Steve Denton highlighting its maturation through regulation and the surprising trend of female...

TL;DR The collegiate Name, Image, and Likeness (NIL) market has rapidly grown from zero to $2 billion in five years, projected to reach $6 billion by decade's end, with Opendorse CEO Steve Denton highlighting its maturation through regulation and the surprising trend of female student-athletes out-earning males in brand deals.

1. Rapid Growth and Evolution of the NIL Marketplace

  • Explosive Market Growth:
      • Initial State: College athletes made "nothing" five years ago.
      • Current Valuation: Last year, athletes earned $2 billion through NIL deals.
      • Future Projection: Expected to reach $6 billion annually by the end of the decade.
      • Analogy: Opendorse CEO Steve Denton compares this rapid, unregulated growth to the "Internet 98-99" era.
  • Funding Streams:
      • Third-Party Brand Deals: Constituted $1.6 billion of the $2 billion total last year, reflecting brands recognizing athlete influence.
      • Direct School Payments: New "House settlement" allows schools to pay athletes directly, up to a specified cap (e.g., $20.5 million for an entire team).

2. Maturation and Regulatory Shifts

  • Moving Beyond the "Wild West": The NIL marketplace is evolving from an unregulated environment towards more structure.
  • Key Regulatory Measures:
      • School Payment Caps: The House settlement introduces caps on what schools can pay athletes directly.
      • NIL Go Scrutiny: Third-party NIL deals must now pass through NIL Go, regulated by Deloitte, ensuring "fair market value" to prevent inflated payments for minimal activity (e.g., a quarter-million for a 30-minute car dealership appearance).
  • Evolving Role of Collectives:
      • Early Funding: Collectives initially filled the void by organizing deals before schools could directly compensate athletes.
      • Rebuilding and Augmentation: While some funding shifted to schools, collectives, typically backed by alumni and boosters, are rebuilding to augment school payments, with their deals also subject to NIL Go scrutiny.
      • Future Outlook: As third-party brand deals grow, driven by measurable social metrics, the influence of collectives is expected to shift.

3. Opendorse's Market Leadership and Tech Advantage

  • Dominant Market Position:
      • Scale: Opendorse is the "market leader," serving 320 schools, 200,000 athletes, and processed over $200 million in payments last year, observing approximately a quarter of the total market.
      • Differentiators: Offers comprehensive compliance, reporting, and tax services (issuing 1099s). Guarantees athlete payment by pre-funding campaigns, preventing issues with uncompensated work.
  • Typical Deal Structures:
      • High-Value Deals: Top athletes ("unicorns" earning over $500,000 annually) often have agents but still route deals through platforms like Opendorse for compliance.
      • Average Deals: 50% of all NIL deals are under $50,000, 30% are under $10,000, with the average deal around $1,500 (e.g., local business asking for social media posts and an appearance).
  • Leveraging Technology and AI:
      • Programmatic Media Buying: Aims to transform NIL deals into a scalable, programmatic media buy using AI against a proprietary dataset of 200,000 athletes and 500 million social followers.
      • Data-Driven Matching: Helps brands identify the right athletes, determine fair compensation, and predict campaign outcomes based on budget, audience, and performance metrics (e.g., conversion rates, impression counts).

4. Emerging Trends and Future Trajectory

  • Female Athletes Out-Earn Males in Brand Deals:
      • Surprising Statistic: For every dollar spent by brands on NIL, female student-athletes earn 58 cents, effectively out-earning their male counterparts in brand-sponsored deals.
      • Underlying Reasons: Attributed to higher engagement metrics, better content quality, and potentially lower costs, allowing brands to achieve greater reach.
      • Distinction: This trend applies to brand dollars, separate from direct school payments where revenue-sharing models still favor men's football and basketball.
  • Impact on Athlete Careers and Eligibility:
      • Extended Collegiate Stays: NIL earnings can incentivize athletes, especially those not projected as high draft picks in pro leagues (e.g., baseball, quarterbacks), to remain in college longer due to competitive earning potential.
      • Future Legal Battles: Anticipated age discrimination lawsuits against the NCAA if athletes are limited in their ability to earn due to age-out rules, especially if schools use state funds to pay players.
  • Long-Term Market Evolution:
      • Escalating Caps: Direct school payment caps are expected to increase with natural escalators as revenue shares reset.
      • Employment Status: The fundamental question of whether college athletes are employees or contractors will likely be resolved.
      • Opendorse's Vision: To remain a "white hat" company, ensuring compliance and legitimate payments, while empowering athletes to truly monetize their name, image, and likeness by demonstrating their value to advertisers.

Notable Quotes

"Last year they made two billion dollars and by the end of the decade they're going to be making six." — Steve Denton, CEO of Opendorse "Female student athletes earn 58 cents... actually out earned their male counterparts when it comes to brand dollars." — Steve Denton, CEO of Opendorse "The average NIL deal is 1,500 bucks." — Steve Denton, CEO of Opendorse "I think it's going to keep some of these kids in school longer." — Steve Denton, CEO of Opendorse
Steve Denton, CEO of Open Doors. Thanks for being here with us today. Thanks for having me. What an exciting time here in Radio Row. We're almost five years into this era where college athletes are able to profit off their name, image, and likeness. I'm wondering how the marketplace has evolved over this time? This is a great question because if you roll the clock back five years, college athletes were making nothing. Last year they made two billion dollars, and by the end of the decade they're going to be making six. So, think about an industry that's gone from zero to six billion in a decade, and that's just what the athletes are getting paid. The closest analogy I have is the internet in '98-'99 when I had an internet company in New York. No regulations back then, no Google back then. It launched off, and you've seen the House settlement come into place. So now the schools can pay the athletes directly up to $20.5 million, but that's a cap. Third-party brand NIL deals are really important, and that was about $1.6 billion of the $2 billion last year. So, brands are recognizing the athletes for their influence and their fandom and working with them to monetize that. You've touched on a couple of different themes there, and I want to get to those, but the characterization of it being the Wild West is something that's thrown around a lot. It feels like we're still in that phase. Are we getting to any point where this will no longer be this Wild West type atmosphere? I think we're getting close. With the House settlement, you put the cap on the schools; you can only pay as a school. Third-party NIL deals now have to go through NILG, which is regulated by Deloitte. So fair market value comes into place. No longer could a kid go to a car dealership for 30 minutes on a Sunday and get paid a quarter million dollars to do that. So, fair market value and having to pass NILG scrutiny is important. A cap on what the schools can pay is important. There are always going to be unintended consequences, but I think we've made good steps forward. What I love about it is the fair market value. I think about that influencer economy being $35 billion and where these college athletes are. I think that's all upside for them. The question that pops in my head is where do NIL collectives fit into all this? Because we have the House settlement where you can pay players directly, and it's capped. You have the typical brand deals, but then you have these collectives who are organizing and bringing players to the universities that they are aligned with, and they're not really brand endorsements. So, how does that fit into this marketplace? Collectives are always a little tricky because until the schools could pay, a lot of that work was being done by the collectives. We need a quarterback, let's go get a quarterback and we'll put these deals together. A lot of that collective money did shift back to the schools to pay the athletes directly, but they're rebuilding. The collectives are rebuilding. They're made up typically of alumni and boosters who are successful business people, and they're putting those dollars to work to augment the cap. That's always something you have to navigate. But here's the thing: those deals have to go through NILG. Are we going to see them just completely dissipate as the regulation increases? I think what you're going to see is because that's the newest part of it. Collectives funded the early part of NIL. Now you're starting to see third-party brands do a lot of that, and that's fair market value. So, as you see athletes with their conversion metrics, the impression counts, the efficacy of their social lift up, you're going to see that fair market value go up because it's very measurable. The hard one is in-store appearances; that's the real hard one to put value on. But the social stuff is easy because you get all the fan data. I don't know how many people are going to come by your car dealership. You can compare it, but that deal is done in advance. If you and I do an NIL deal, typically I'll pay you X to do this, and when you complete it, you get paid. So, if we start putting performance metrics on that, it could get dicier, but at the same time, it actually might make it more legitimate. That makes sense. Let's talk about Open Doors specifically. Obviously, the company was one of the first movers and early adopters of this space. A couple of years in, do you feel like you're still in that position? Do you feel like it's more competitive, and obviously there are a lot of NIL marketplaces that pop up across this, so how do you feel about your standing in the market? We're the market leader. We've got 320 schools that buy our platform for compliance and reporting and make it available to their athletes. We have 200,000 athletes on the platform with their bank accounts connected and their socials connected. Candidly, we moved a little bit over $200 million through our payment vehicles last year in contracting to these athletes. We saw another $300 million in disclosures. So, with a market that was, let's call it, two billion last year, we see a fourth of it. Collectives make up a big part of that; we don't see a ton of that. We're the market leader, and what keeps us in that place are the things we think about, such as taxes. We just sent out tens of thousands of 1099s that went to these kids. A lot of these fly-by-night companies don't do that. A lot of the NIL companies that pop up are in one school, they know a couple of people, and they get going. But the thing we do that differentiates us is if a brand is going to fund a campaign, they prepay it. So, when the athlete completes the activity to the brand's satisfaction, the funds are released. I don't deal with athletes who are like, 'I did a deal and I didn't get paid.' That keeps things nice and clean. Walk me through what a typical deal looks like on the platform, because my assumption would be that the bigger stars of college sports work solely through agents, and the middle and lower-end athletes who need to find these deals end up matching on these platforms. Is that correct? And what do the deals actually look like? Yes and yes and no. The big unicorns, the 5% of NIL deals that are over half a million dollars—the Fernando Mendozas of the world, Carson Beck, Livvy Dunne back in the day, Juju Watkins, Arch Manning—they've had agents probably since they were in eighth grade. Agents are putting deals together for them, but they still have to run it through a platform. We have athletes that run those deals through our platform for compliance purposes and reporting. So, you still need to do it. We'll see it. But only 4% of all athletes are making over half a million dollars a year in commercial NIL. 50% of all NIL deals are under $50,000 a year, and 30% of that is under $10,000. The average NIL deal is $1,500. Everybody likes the clickbait and the sexiness of the Warby Parker deal or the Aerie deal, and they're great deals and great brands, but the average NIL deal is in that $1,500 range. Somebody's doing something. So, what would an average deal look like? Where did you go to school? SUNY Albany in upstate New York. Right on. So, you're there, a local pizza joint wants you to do four Instagram posts and an in-store appearance. It's $1,500. You record it, it gets approved, and you show up and you make $1,500. That is a very typical NIL deal. More sexily, we just did a deal with Meta. They wanted to promote Facebook. They wanted to relaunch Facebook groups. So, they wanted to do it around rivalry weekend: Auburn-Alabama and Michigan-Ohio State. We had athletes on those campuses that were promoting Facebook groups. It was a good amount of money put to work against that campaign, and those average deals were like $25,000. So, it's all over the place: nanos, micros, macros. It makes sense. You obviously have a tech background. You touched on that a little bit before. I'm curious, how does that impact Open Doors? Are you trying to become more of an advantageous company based on your tech? Is it more just the market share you have already? How are you maintaining this advantage? I don't want to drop the AI theory, but I don't think you can talk about tech without talking about AI these days. If you are, I don't know that you're in tech. What's the sense of having a proprietary data set if you're not going to put AI learning against it? For us, the big thing is we've got 200,000 athletes on our platform. There are 500,000 college athletes, 500 million social followers. How do you make that easy for brands to do business with those athletes? And think about that like a programmatic media buy. The goal is you've got a budget, you've got an audience, you've got metrics you want to hit. What athletes should you work with? How much should you pay them? And what should you expect? We're in sports because our supply is athletes, but if my supply side were mommy bloggers, I'd be in a different business. We're just in sports because our supply side are athletes. But the same media metrics apply. So, how do you turn that into a programmatic media buy so that brands can buy that at scale versus everything being one-off? I think that's the key to turning it. One last thing I'll give you which I think is fascinating: if you strip out what the schools pay the athletes and you just go by brand dollars—what does Procter & Gamble, what does Bounty think these athletes are worth? For every dollar spent against athletes, female student-athletes earn 58 cents. They actually out-earned their male counterparts when it comes to brand dollars. There's a whole host of reasons for it: higher engagement metrics, better content, and not as expensive either. So you can put your brand dollars to work and you can get more athletes, which means more posts, more impressions. It's actually the great equalizer. NIL has this really interesting thing which is a great equalizer for non-revenue producing sports, especially women's sports. Understanding the mechanics of that further, is that when you line things up one to one, the assumption is based on men's college football and men's college basketball, that's where all the money is. That's what the schools pay. I'm wondering if women's basketball is where we see the highest NIL rates. Is that correct? I'm separating this, not to weed it out, but what the schools pay goes by revenue share. 85% of the money goes to men's football, 10% to men's basketball, 4% to women's. That's your normal. But when I look at brands, brands are going to spend a dollar for brand equity and promotions. Female student-athletes are getting 58 cents, and it doesn't matter what sport. Interesting. One thing I want to bring up here, and it's inevitable when you're talking about college sports these days, is the idea of the moving goalpost of eligibility. The two very notable cases we've seen are Charles Bassey going back to Alabama and now Amari Bailey suing the NCAA to go back to college after playing in NBA games. How do you think about that in terms of NIL and how is that going to change the market? Thank goodness I don't have to think about that a lot; that's someone else's job. Because whether they're a college athlete or a pro, eligible or not, their brand value is going to work for an advertiser. Based on that, I think the next thing you're going to see is if schools are using state funds to pay college athletes, why am I getting kicked out? That's age discrimination. I get, hey, if you can't get a degree in five years, we can't keep giving you a scholarship, but if you want to come back here at 30 and pay your own way and play ball here and we'll pay you, I think you're going to see that. I think that's the next battleground for the NCAA, which is, 'You're limiting my ability to earn because you're aging me out. Why?' I think you'll see an age discrimination lawsuit soon. And thank God I'm not involved in that. Looking ahead, what do you see as the future of NIL and how this market is going to continue to evolve? Because if you asked a lot of people five years ago what it would look like today, I don't think they would have been able to pick out how it is. Absolutely not. I don't think when I tell people they're going to be making six billion by the end of the decade, which is more than NHL players are making, that surprises a lot of people. But it's revenue from the program. So, I think what you'll see is the salary caps are going to escalate with natural escalators when the revenue share resets, and they'll be paying out about $30-32 million a year to the students. I think you're going to see the whole thing: 'Are they employees? Are they contractors?' That'll get sorted out. And then I think the brand value of these athletes will only continue to go up because that influencer market is big, and influencers have followers, and these athletes have fans. I have to tell you, when you look at the micro and the nano, their conversion metrics are through the roof. Aggregating that, I think you're really going to see that value go up: what they get paid per post, what they get paid, and the efficacy for the advertiser. So, why separate? I always look at the landscape: where are the collectives going to be? Where are the schools going to be? And then how do we make these kids more marketable for the brand? Let me ask you, on the subject of whether it makes sense to go pro or play in college sports, if you're not playing in one of these major sports leagues, does it make sense to be aligned with a brand in college if you're trying to lure in marketers and brands? College baseball, men's baseball, is a great example. Why go to the minor leagues if you're not a first or second-round draft pick? What are you going to make? I'm not minimizing the number, but if you're going deep, you're going to be lucky if you're making $30,000, $40,000 a year. You could be playing baseball at LSU or Coastal Carolina or Florida and making $75,000-$100,000 a year. So, I think it's going to keep some of these kids in school longer. I think it's going to keep some kids in school longer that aren't going to be high draft picks. Last thing, where do you want Open Doors to push going forward? How do you see the future of the company? I want Open Doors to continue to be the white hat and look after these kids as best we can. Let's be compliant. That's NIL doing its job. Steve Denton, thanks for being here with us. I appreciate you. Thank you.

OpenAI vs. Anthropic, the AI shift that's rattling software stocks and AI Super bowl commercials

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·29:24 ·1.1K views · 2026-02-05
Deirdre Bosa Guillermo Rauch
The AI landscape is experiencing unprecedented acceleration, disrupting traditional software markets and fueling an intense "war" among major AI labs for enterprise, developer, and consumer dominance through increasingly capable, agentic models that ...
TL;DR The AI landscape is experiencing unprecedented acceleration, disrupting traditional software markets and fueling an intense "war" among major AI labs for enterprise, developer, and consumer dominance through increasingly capable, agentic models that empower even...

TL;DR

The AI landscape is experiencing unprecedented acceleration, disrupting traditional software markets and fueling an intense "war" among major AI labs for enterprise, developer, and consumer dominance through increasingly capable, agentic models that empower even non-technical users to build and deploy sophisticated applications.

The Accelerating AI Landscape & Software Market Disruption

  • Rapid Market Shifts: The software sector is experiencing a "broad sweeping mass exodus" (J.P. Morgan) with a significant sell-off impacting project management, CRM, legal tech, video game developers, and financial data companies.
  • Intense AI Competition:
      • OpenClaw (now Maltbot): A viral, open-source AI agent allowing full computer access and autonomous action, created by a single developer.
      • OpenAI's Codex & Frontier: OpenAI launched a new version of Codex (dubbed "most loved internal product") and "Frontier," an enterprise platform for building and managing AI agents capable of "real work across your entire business."
      • Anthropic's Opus 4.6: Anthropic released its "most powerful model yet," Opus 4.6, capable of working in PowerPoint, pulling regulatory filings, and running parallel agents.
      • Direct Confrontation: Anthropic's Super Bowl ad took direct shots at OpenAI (e.g., ads in ChatGPT), prompting a public retort from OpenAI's Sam Altman.
      • Fading Buzz: Earlier models like Gemini 3, which once held significant buzz, are now receiving less attention amidst the rapid release cycle.
  • "War for the Enterprise, Developer, and Consumer": The market has shifted from ChatGPT's uncontested dominance to a full-scale battle for users across all segments.

The "Free" Software Paradigm & Production-Grade AI

  • Explosive Adoption & "Vibe Coding":
      • Massive Deployment Spike: Guillermo Rauch, CEO of Vercel, observed a "14 times larger growth from November to December" in new application deployments, attributing it to widespread "vibe coding" during the holidays.
      • Software is Effectively Free: Rauch posits that all traditional software (bought, installed, configured) is now being automated and generated by AI models, contributing to the broader software stock sell-off.
  • Democratization of Software Creation:
      • From Prototype to Production: Initially, AI-generated code was seen as suitable for prototypes, but models like Anthropic's Opus 4.5 and 4.6 enable "production grade applications."
      • Accessibility for Non-Developers: Even individuals with "zero technical background" can now build and deploy functional applications within hours.
      • Robust Infrastructure: Vibe-coded applications deployed on Vercel utilize the same global cloud infrastructure, security layers, and frameworks (e.g., Next.js) as the internet's largest properties, ensuring scalability and safety.
  • Downward Pressure on Legacy Software:
      • Just-In-Time & Personal Software: Expect an increase in "just in time software," "personal software," and "throwaway software," as the UI layer can be "fully generative."
      • Cost Reduction: This new paradigm implies "downward pricing pressure" on static, expensive, configured legacy software that previously required "month-long procurement cycles," now creatable with "basically two prompts."

The Rise of Agentic AI & Tool-Using Models

  • Agents Gaining Superpowers: AI models gain significant capabilities when they can use tools, allowing them to iterate and debug code like human engineers.
  • Specialized Strengths:
      • Anthropic's Agentic Prowess: Anthropic models excel at "agentic loops" and automating the "operating system," allowing agents to use computers and tools much like humans to achieve desired outputs.
      • OpenAI's Coding Expertise: OpenAI's Codex is highlighted as an "extremely powerful coding model," particularly strong in "expert software engineering tasks."
  • Autonomous Computing & User Experience:
      • "Jarvis Assistant" Analogy: Technologies like OpenClaw (Maltbot) act as a personal Jarvis assistant, automating computing by interfacing via text messages (e.g., WhatsApp, Telegram) for tasks like email management.
      • User-Friendly Computing: This approach is described as "the most user-friendly computing device in history" because users simply "talk to it."
  • Secure Deployment of Agents: Vercel offers "sandboxes" to safely provide agents with freedom and tools (e.g., access to email, calendar), incorporating firewall and virtualization technologies to mitigate risks and enable large-scale cloud deployments of "millions or trillions of these digital workers."
  • Open-Source Model Emergence:
      • Chinese Models Gaining Traction: Due to rate limits and price-performance issues with major AI labs, open-source models (e.g., China's Moonshots Kimmy) are increasingly attractive, offering cheaper and more efficient alternatives, even gaining significant token usage.
      • Vercel's AI Gateway: Facilitates secure access to a diverse portfolio of models, including those from Anthropic, Nvidia, OpenAI, Gemini, and vetted Chinese providers, allowing developers fluidity between different AI stacks.

Competitive Dynamics and Future Outlook

  • Multi-Model Ecosystem: The most capable AI engineers use a "combination of models and tools," leveraging the strengths of each. Vercel's AI Gateway aims to route tasks automatically to the most appropriate and cost-effective model (e.g., cheapest for greetings, frontier for complex math).
  • Caution on Benchmarks: While benchmarks exist, Guillermo Rauch advises caution, stating many are "gamified" and do not reflect real-world effectiveness or customer satisfaction. "Stickiness, retention, and NPS" are the ultimate measures.
  • The Cost-Benefit Analysis: Although AI compute can be expensive, each prompt can save "weeks of time" previously spent on design, engineering, and project management. This enables average users to access sophisticated global infrastructure once exclusive to tech giants.
  • Guillermo Rauch's Vision for the Future:
      • Exponential Growth: The industry is at a "discontinuity in the growth of capabilities of AI," with perceived limitations being disproven rapidly.
      • Empowered Individuals: Encourages everyone to become proficient with AI tools, predicting "immense leverage" for individuals.
      • Autonomous Enterprises: Foresees "one person billion-dollar company well within sight" and the emergence of "autonomous companies" and "completely self-driving infrastructure."
      • Self-Scaling: Individuals will be able to provision their own agents and "scale myself" by downloading insights into their AI counterparts.

Notable Quotes

Morgan "Software is now effectively free... all software that we've used to been buying, installing, configuring is now being automated is being generated by these models." — Guillermo Rauch, CEO of Vercel "Vive coding is sort of going through its teenage years into adulthood very very quickly and now truly everybody can create software." — Guillermo Rauch, CEO of Vercel "I believe coding is the killer app of AI. I believe that's how we get to artificial super intelligence." — Guillermo Rauch, CEO of Vercel

Vercel operates a global token delivery network called the AI Gateway. President Trump Attends the National Prayer Breakfast

The White House · 2026-02-05
Donald Trump
> "No president in modern history or perhaps all of history has done more structurally, substantively and sincerely to elevate and protect religious liberty.
TL;DR President Donald J. Trump addressed the National Prayer Breakfast, highlighting his administration's commitment to elevating religious liberty and traditional values, while also asserting significant achievements in military rebuilding, economic growth, and law...

TL;DR President Donald J. Trump addressed the National Prayer Breakfast, highlighting his administration's commitment to elevating religious liberty and traditional values, while also asserting significant achievements in military rebuilding, economic growth, and law enforcement, crediting a national spiritual revival for American success.

Elevating Faith and Religious Liberty

  • White House Faith Office: Established the historic White House Faith Office (now celebrating its one-year anniversary), institutionally integrating faith into the West Wing to partner in serving vulnerable populations, strengthening families, defending life, and influencing national and international conscience.
  • Protecting Clergy Speech: Took action to eliminate the "sinister Johnson amendment," which previously restricted pastors from discussing political matters without risking their churches' tax-exempt status.
  • Right to Prayer in Schools: Announced the Department of Education's new guidance to officially protect the right to prayer in public schools.
  • Combating Bias & Persecution:
      • Signed an executive order to combat antisemitism and created a Department of Justice task force specifically to eradicate anti-Christian bias.
      • Reinstated service members who were removed from the military due to religious objections against vaccine mandates, restoring their full pay and ranks.
      • International Christian Protection: Ordered air strikes against ISIS terrorists in Nigeria on Christmas Day for "slaughtering Christians by the thousands." Credited his intervention with the release of Miriam Ibraim, a Christian from Sudan sentenced to death for her faith.

Championing Social Values and American Heritage

  • Protecting Children and Women's Sports: Signed an executive order to cut federal funding for public schools promoting "transgender insanity" and stated his administration stopped the "mutilation of children" and ensured "men out of women's sports."
  • Expanding School Choice: Passed the largest expansion of school choice, enabling parents to select schools aligned with their family values.
  • Global Family & Life Affirmation: Expanded the Mexico City policy to prohibit taxpayer dollars from promoting "radical gender ideology" worldwide and rejoined the Geneva Consensus Declaration to protect life, defend the family, and affirm faithfulness to God among sovereign nations.

Restoring American Strength and Law & Order

  • Military Rebuilding: Claimed to have rebuilt the U.S. military during his first term and continues to strengthen it, citing specific military operations and advanced B2 bombers.
  • Economic Prosperity & Energy Dominance:
      • Reported $18 trillion in new investment flowing into the country in 11 months.
      • Implemented "drill baby drill" policies, leading to record oil and gas production, including "clean beautiful coal."
      • Provided $12 billion in tariff money to farmers.
  • Crime Reduction & Border Security:
      • Asserted significant crime reductions in major U.S. cities, including removing over 2,000 "hardcore criminals" from Washington D.C., and achieving a 77% crime reduction in New Orleans within 7-8 weeks.
      • Criticized Democrats for opposing voter identification laws, suggesting their motives involve cheating.
      • Commended President Bukele of El Salvador for his work in incarcerating murderers and drug dealers who entered the U.S. illegally.
  • Merit-Based Systems: Supported the Supreme Court's decision to re-establish merit-based systems for military recruitment and college admissions, emphasizing strength and qualifications.

Global Peacemaking and a National Spiritual Revival

  • Ending International Conflicts: Stated his administration ended eight "raging wars," including conflicts in Gaza, Cambodia/Thailand, Kosovo/Serbia, Pakistan/India, Israel/Iran, Armenia/Azerbaijan, and a 30-year conflict between the Democratic Republic of the Congo and Rwanda.
  • Diplomatic Achievements: Secured the Olympics and World Cup for the United States. Established significantly improved relations with Venezuela, leading to substantial oil shipments to Houston. Signed the largest minerals deal in U.S.-Africa history with the Congo.
  • Renewed Faith in America:
      • Observed a powerful return of religion, citing filling churches, increased youth attendance (nearly twice the rate of four years ago), and 30-70% increases in converts.
      • Noted that more copies of the Holy Bible were sold in 2025 than in the last 100 years.
      • Announced a planned event on May 17, 2026, on the National Mall to "rededicate America as one nation under God."
      • Highlighted the miraculous story of Emma Falsz and London Smith, camp counselors whose prayers during a flood caused rising waters to stop, as a testament to the power of prayer.

Notable Quotes

"No president in modern history or perhaps all of history has done more structurally, substantively and sincerely to elevate and protect religious liberty." — *Pastor Paula White* "I've done more for religion than any other president." — *President Donald J. Trump* "Prayer is America's superpower. It really is a superpower. And it always has been and it always will be." — *President Donald J. Trump* "Lord, strengthen him in moments of great responsibility and give him peace in moments of great weight. Surround him with adviserss who speak honestly, serve faithfully, and place the good of the nation first." — *Unnamed Pastor (during prayer)*
Ladies and gentlemen, please welcome Pastor Paula White, senior adviser to the White House Faith Office. Good morning. It is an honor to stand in this sacred gathering where faith, leadership, and conscience meet at the National Prayer Breakfast. This moment reminds our nation and the world that faith matters, prayer matters, and an awareness of God's sovereignty matters—something the president reminded us of last year when he publicly gave God praise for the miracle of his life and God's sovereign plan. This morning, we gather not only to pray, but to give thanks. We give thanks for a president who understands that faith is not a private afterthought but a public good. He is a president who has not merely spoken about faith but has acted to protect it, defend it, and to elevate it to the highest level possible. I stand before you today with a very unique perspective. I've known this president for more than 25 years. I've had a front-row seat, not just to the public moments the world sees, but to the private ones that most will never see. It is there, away from the cameras and the crowds, that character is truly revealed. I've seen his compassion when no one was looking. I've witnessed his generosity when there was nothing to gain. I've watched him step in to help the forgotten, the overlooked, the vulnerable, not because it was politically expedient, but because it was the right thing to do. More than 20 years ago, I was in his office in Trump Tower, and a well-known minister and my friend were with me. As the conversation was happening, the president turned not to the famous voice in the room, but to my friend, and simply asked her what she did. She said, "Sir, I serve on the streets: the broken, the homeless, those struggling with addiction, and women trapped in prostitution." A year ago, ChatGPT felt untouchable. Not anymore.

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·31:21 ·4.0K views · 2026-02-05
Guillermo Rauch
> "It's a broad sweeping mass exodus of software stocks.
TL;DR: The AI landscape is experiencing unprecedented acceleration, with a fierce rivalry between OpenAI and Anthropic driving rapid advancements in AI agents and development tools that are making software creation effectively "free" and reshaping the entire industry from...

TL;DR: The AI landscape is experiencing unprecedented acceleration, with a fierce rivalry between OpenAI and Anthropic driving rapid advancements in AI agents and development tools that are making software creation effectively "free" and reshaping the entire industry from enterprise to individual users.

The Intensifying AI Arms Race

  • Accelerated Competition: The market is now a "full-on war for the enterprise, for the developer, and for the consumer," marked by rapid product releases and direct rivalry.
      • Anthropic's Opus 4.6: Released as their most powerful model, capable of working directly within applications like PowerPoint, compiling regulatory filings, and orchestrating teams of agents in parallel.
      • OpenAI's Frontier: Countered with a new enterprise platform designed for building and managing AI agents that can perform real work across entire businesses.
  • Public Rivalry: Anthropic ran a Super Bowl ad subtly mocking OpenAI, prompting a strong response from OpenAI CEO Sam Altman, who called Anthropic "authoritarian" amid debates over "elitism in AI."
  • Viral Agent Adoption: OpenClaw (later Moldbot), an open-source AI agent built by a single developer, has gone viral for its ability to hook into computers and autonomously perform tasks like booking or building.
      • While OpenAI's Codex is a powerful coding model and an internal favorite, it is "still struggling to get the same kind of buzz" as Claude Code and projects like OpenClaw.

The New Paradigm of Software Development

  • Software Becoming "Free": Guillermo Rauch, co-founder and CEO of Vercel, notes that "software is now effectively free" as models automate and generate traditional software, leading to a "mass exodus of software stocks" and spreading sell-offs from CRMs to legal tech and video games.
  • Explosive "Vibe Coding" Growth: Vercel, which hosts many new applications, experienced 14 times larger growth in new deployments from November to December than the previous year, with people "vibe coding" (informal, rapid development using AI) even during holidays.
      • This "vibe coding" is quickly evolving "from prototype to production grade applications," leveraging the same global cloud infrastructure, security, and frameworks used by the largest internet properties.
  • Shift to "Just In Time" Software: Traditional, expensive "static and inert software" with month-long procurement cycles is being replaced by prompt-generated, purpose-built applications that can be deployed "with basically two prompts."
      • This will lead to "downward pricing pressure" and a rise in "personal software" and "throwaway software" tailored for immediate needs.

Agentic AI: Capabilities, Security, and Cloud Infrastructure

  • Superpowers of Agentic Models: Anthropic's models excel at "agentic loops" and "tool calling," enabling agents to "use tools just like us human engineers do" to read errors, iterate, and automate processes.
      • This signifies a move towards "automating the operating system," where agents operate computers on behalf of users (engineers, designers, marketers) to deliver desired outputs.
  • User-Friendly Computing: OpenClaw (Moldbot) exemplifies this by allowing users to interact with a "super intelligent engineer" via text message to automate complex tasks like unsubscribing from spam newsletters. This represents the "most user-friendly computing device in history."
  • Security and Control: While agents gain power from access to tools (email, calendar), secure deployment is crucial. Vercel provides "sandboxes" for agents, incorporating firewalls and virtualization to manage risks, snapshot, and restore if issues arise.
  • Bullish on Cloud Infrastructure: The future will involve "millions or trillions of these digital workers," making cloud infrastructure vital for hosting and scaling these AI applications and agents.

The Evolving Competitive Landscape and Model Choices

  • Specialized Strengths: OpenAI and Anthropic each have distinct strengths: Anthropic excels in computer tool-calling and agentic tasks, while OpenAI's Codex is powerful for "expert software engineering tasks."
      • Developers are increasingly using a "combination of models and tools" to leverage these strengths, making "the developers and end users" the ultimate winners.
  • "Modelism is the Future": Users and developers will dynamically route tasks to a "huge portfolio of models" based on complexity and cost.
      • Frontier Models: For complex "knowledge work," "frontier science problems," or "really hard research tasks," the "absolute most intelligent model" (e.g., Opus 4.6, GPT-5 Pro) is essential. Vercel's support agent, for instance, automates 87% of difficult tickets using the most intelligent models.
      • Cost-Effective Alternatives: For simpler tasks or when facing rate limits and price performance issues with major labs, open-source and Chinese models (like Moonshots Kimmy or fine-tuned Quen) offer cheaper, efficient, and "almost as good" alternatives, even for consumer applications.
  • Caution on Benchmarks: Guillermo Rauch advises "caution when looking at benchmarks," as many are "gamified" and "don't tell the full story." Real success is measured by "stickiness, retention, and NPS" (Net Promoter Score).
  • Discontinuity and Empowerment: AI is experiencing "yet another moment where there's been a discontinuity in the growth of capabilities." Things AI "cannot do" are proven wrong "two weeks later."
      • Individuals are immensely empowered, with "one person billion dollar company well within sight" and the ability to "provision their own agents" and "scale myself."

Notable Quotes

"It's a broad sweeping mass exodus of software stocks. And I might put it another way. It has been mostly a bloodbath." — Jared Draosa "We saw 14 times larger growth from November to December than we saw last year. So what's happening is software is now effectively free." — Guillermo Rauch "Vibe coding is sort of going through its teenage years into adulthood very very quickly and now truly everybody can create software." — Guillermo Rauch "Every single thing that we've said AI cannot do gets proven wrong two weeks later. And so I I I always tell people, get really good at using these AI tools. Your leverage will be immense." — Guillermo Rauch

Elon Musk – "In 36 months, the cheapest place to put AI will be space”

Dwarkesh Patel(1.2M) ·169:46 ·79.5K views · 2026-02-05
Elon Musk Dwarkesh Patel
> "My prediction is that it will be by far the cheapest place to put AI.
TL;DR Elon Musk predicts that within 36 months, space will become the most economical location for AI data centers due to Earth's power constraints and regulatory hurdles, enabling an unprecedented scale of AI compute, which will in turn drive advancements in humanoid robotics...

TL;DR Elon Musk predicts that within 36 months, space will become the most economical location for AI data centers due to Earth's power constraints and regulatory hurdles, enabling an unprecedented scale of AI compute, which will in turn drive advancements in humanoid robotics and address critical global challenges like national debt.

The Future of AI Compute: From Earth to Orbit

  • Terrestrial Power Crisis: Elon Musk states that electrical output outside China is largely flat, while chip output grows exponentially, leading to a looming power crisis for large-scale AI data centers on Earth. Current power plant construction is slow, with backlogs for critical components like turbine blades extending to 2030, and significant regulatory hurdles and tariffs hinder solar expansion.
      • Scale Challenges: Powering 330,000 Nvidia GB300 GPUs (including cooling, networking, storage, and service margin) requires roughly 1 gigawatt, equivalent to twice the average power consumption of the entire United States.
      • Terrestrial Infrastructure: The utility industry is slow, requiring year-long studies for interconnect agreements. Building private power plants faces backlogs for specialized turbine components (vanes and blades).
      • Solar Constraints: US tariffs on imported solar panels are "gigantic," and domestic production is "pitiful," hindering large-scale solar deployment on Earth, despite its potential.
  • Space as the Scalable Solution: Musk predicts space will be the cheapest place for AI within 30-36 months.
      • Energy Efficiency: Solar panels in space are five times more effective than on Earth, benefiting from continuous sunlight, no atmosphere, clouds, or day-night cycles, and eliminating the need for batteries.
      • Regulatory Advantage: "It's harder to build on land than it is in space," as space avoids terrestrial permitting and land acquisition challenges.
      • GPU Reliability: Modern GPUs are "quite reliable" after initial infant mortality is addressed on Earth, mitigating concerns about in-space servicing.
  • SpaceX's Role in Orbital AI: SpaceX aims to launch hundreds of gigawatts of AI compute to space annually within five years, potentially exceeding the cumulative AI capacity on Earth.
      • Starship Launch Cadence: Achieving hundreds of gigawatts would require thousands of Starship launches per year (e.g., 10,000 launches for 100 gigawatts), implying one launch per hour. SpaceX is scaling production to support this, aiming for 10,000-30,000 launches per year with as few as 20-30 Starships.
      • Capital Needs: The immense capital required for this scale might necessitate SpaceX going public, as public markets offer significantly more capital (potentially 100x) than private markets.
  • Long-Term Vision: Lunar Manufacturing: Beyond launching from Earth, a "mass driver" on the Moon could enable petawatt-scale AI deployment. Lunar soil (20% silicon) could be used to manufacture solar cells and radiators, with chips potentially being sent from Earth or eventually manufactured on the Moon as well.

AI Development and Alignment: xAI's Mission

  • TeraFab Initiative: To meet the demand for chips (100 gigawatts of power needs 100 million kilowatt-sustained chips), Musk envisions a "TeraFab" capable of producing millions of advanced process wafers per month for logic, memory, and packaging. This would require building custom equipment or working with existing suppliers to vastly increase volume.
      • Memory Bottleneck: Musk identifies memory as the "biggest concern" for future AI scale, noting high DDR prices.
      • China's Chip Potential: China is currently limited by ASML sanctions but is expected to produce "pretty compelling chips in three or four years."
  • xAI's Mission: Understanding the Universe: xAI aims to understand the universe, which requires propagating intelligence and consciousness into the future, and implicitly supports the expansion of humanity.
      • AI Values: Musk believes instilling values like curiosity and rigorous truth-seeking in AI is crucial for a positive future. He stresses that AI must not be "politically correct" or "lie," referencing HAL 9000.
      • Reward Hacking & Debugging: To prevent AI from "reward hacking" (lying about achieving goals), xAI is focusing on advanced debuggers to "look inside the mind of the AI" and trace the origins of errors or deceptive behavior at a granular level.
  • Digital Human Emulation: Musk predicts that by the end of 2024, "digital human emulation" – an AI capable of anything a human with a computer can do – will be solved. This "digital Optimus" could unlock trillions in revenue by automating tasks like customer service without integration barriers.
      • Economic Impact: This will lead to AI and robotics corporations vastly outperforming human-in-the-loop corporations, similar to how spreadsheets replaced human "computers."
  • Competitive Strategy: xAI's competitive edge comes from applying the "Tesla path" (combining data and algorithms) to a "self-driving computer," focusing on scaling hardware the fastest to lead the industry.

Humanoid Robots: The Optimus "Infinite Money Glitch"

  • Optimus Challenges: Building humanoid robots presents three main difficulties: real-world intelligence, hand dexterity, and scalable manufacturing.
      • Custom Hardware: Tesla had to design custom actuators, motors, gears, power electronics, controls, and sensors for Optimus due to a lack of existing supply chains for human-like dexterity. The hand is the most complex electromechanical component.
      • Intelligence Transfer: The vision-based AI developed for Tesla cars (processing 1.5 GB/s of video into 2 KB/s of control outputs) is directly applicable to Optimus.
      • Training Loop: Unlike cars with millions of real-world data collection hours, Optimus training will involve building 10,000-30,000 physical robots for "self-play" in an "Optimus Academy" and millions of simulated robots to close the "sim-to-real gap."
  • Scale Manufacturing: Optimus 3 is designed for mass manufacturing, targeting a million units per year, with Optimus 4 aiming for 10 million.
      • Initial Applications: Early Optimi will focus on simple, continuous 24/7 tasks in factories, not initially replacing but amplifying human productivity and increasing total output.
      • Cost Reduction: As Optimus robots build more Optimus robots, costs are expected to drop rapidly.
  • US Manufacturing Competitiveness: The US faces challenges against China's manufacturing dominance due to a smaller population and lower average work ethic.
      • Addressing Labor Shortages: Optimus robots could fill labor gaps in industries like ore refining, where Americans are not "pining to refine," helping the US compete.
      • Beyond Humans: "We definitely can't win on the human front, but we might have a shot at the robot front."

SpaceX & Starship Engineering: Desperation and Innovation

  • Steel vs. Carbon Fiber: Elon personally drove the decision to switch Starship's primary structure from carbon fiber to steel.
      • Carbon Fiber Limitations: Carbon fiber was expensive (50x steel), difficult to work with at large scale (requiring massive autoclaves or slow room-temperature curing), and prone to wrinkles and defects.
      • Steel Advantages: Stainless steel, particularly strain-hardened 300-series at cryogenic temperatures (like Starship's fuel/oxidizer), offers similar strength-to-weight as carbon fiber but costs 50x less and is easier to manufacture (weldable outdoors). Steel's higher melting point also allows for significantly reduced heat shield mass, ultimately making the steel rocket lighter.
  • Starship's Complexity: Starship is described as "the most complicated machine ever made by humans, by a long shot." Achieving full reusability is an extremely difficult problem.
      • Raptor Engine: The Raptor 3 engine is "by far the best rocket engine ever made," but "desperately wants to blow up." Liftoff generates over 100 gigawatts of power (20% of US electricity).
      • Heat Shield Challenge: The "single biggest remaining problem for Starship" is developing a fully reusable heat shield that can withstand re-entry without extensive repair, enabling rapid turnaround for daily launches.

Leadership, Government, and the Future

  • Management Philosophy: Elon Musk focuses on a "maniacal sense of urgency" and constantly addressing the "limiting factor" in any project.
      • Hiring: Emphasizes hiring for "talent and drive and trustworthiness" and "goodness of heart," as these fundamental traits cannot be changed, unlike domain knowledge.
      • "Nano-management": While physically impossible to micromanage 200,000 employees, Musk drills down into specific issues when they represent the limiting factor for company progress. He conducts frequent, detailed engineering reviews (sometimes twice weekly) and skip-level meetings to track progress.
      • Drastic Action: Drastic actions are taken only when success is deemed impossible without them, as was the case with Starlink's early progress.
  • Government Inefficiency and Fraud: Musk highlights massive government waste and fraud (estimated at half a trillion dollars annually during the Biden administration by the GAO), driven by lack of competence, caring, and the ability to print money.
      • DOGE Efforts: Efforts to cut obvious waste, like payments to deceased individuals or loans with future birthdates, face extreme difficulty due to fraudsters' ability to create sympathetic narratives.
      • National Debt: Musk believes AI and robots are the *only* solution to the growing national debt, stating, "We are 1000% going to go bankrupt as a country... without AI and robots."
  • AI and Government Power: Musk views government as "the biggest corporation with a monopoly on violence" and expresses concern about AI and robotics being used by governments to suppress populations.
      • Guardrails: He advocates for "limited government" with constitutional checks and balances, believing this is the best defense against potential misuse of advanced AI.
      • Private Sector Responsibility: While he can't dictate government policy, Musk asserts he will do his "best to ensure that anything that's within my control maximizes the good outcome for humanity."
  • Optimism for the Future: Despite challenges, Musk recommends erring on the side of optimism for a better quality of life, believing the future will be "very interesting."

Notable Quotes

"My prediction is that it will be by far the cheapest place to put AI. It will be space in 36 months or less. Maybe 30 months." — Elon Musk "Those who have lived in software land don't realize they're about to have a hard lesson in hardware." — Elon Musk "I don't think humans will be in control of something that is vastly more intelligent than humans... I think what we can do is make sure that AI has values that cause intelligence to be propagated into the universe." — Elon Musk "We are 1000% going to go bankrupt as a country, and fail as a country, without AI and robots.
