So I'm not saying that for sure Grok will do everything, but at least if you try, it's better than not trying. At least if that's fundamental to the mission, it's better than if it's not fundamental to the mission. Understanding the universe means that you have to propagate intelligence into the future. You have to be curious about all things in the universe. It would be much less interesting to eliminate humanity than to see humanity grow and prosper. I like Mars, obviously. Everyone knows I love Mars. But Mars is kind of boring because it's got a bunch of rocks compared to Earth. Earth is much more interesting. So any AI trying to understand the universe would want to see how humanity develops in the future, or else that AI is not adhering to its mission. I'm not saying the AI will necessarily adhere to its mission, but if it does, a future where it sees the outcome of humanity is more interesting than a future where there are a bunch of rocks. This feels sort of confusing to me, or a semantic argument. Are humans really the most interesting collection of atoms? But we're more interesting than rocks. But we're not as interesting as the thing it could turn us into, right? There's something on Earth that could happen that's not human, that's quite interesting. Why does AI decide that humans are the most interesting thing that could colonize the galaxy? Most of what colonizes the galaxy will be robots. Why does it not find those more interesting? You need not just scale, but also scope. Many copies of the same robot… A tiny increase in the number of robots produced is not as interesting as some microscopic... Eliminating humanity, how many robots would that get you? Or how many incremental solar cells? A very small number. But you would then lose the information associated with humanity. You would no longer see how humanity might evolve into the future. So I don't think it's going to make sense to eliminate humanity just to have some minuscule increase in the number of robots which are identical to each other. So maybe it keeps the humans around. It can make a million different varieties of robots, and then there are humans as well, and humans stay on Earth. Then there are all these other robots; they get their own star systems. But it seems you were previously hinting at a vision where it keeps human control over this singulatarian future because— I don't think humans will be in control of something that is vastly more intelligent than humans. So in some sense, you're a doomer, and this is the best we've got. It just keeps us around because we're interesting. I'm just trying to be realistic here. Let's say there's a million times more silicon intelligence than there is biological. I think it would be foolish to assume that there's any way to maintain control over that. Now, you can make sure it has the right values, or you can try to have the right values. At least my theory is that from xAI's mission of understanding the universe, it necessarily means that you want to propagate consciousness into the future, propagate intelligence into the future, and take a set of things that maximize the scope and scale of consciousness. So it's not just about scale, it's also about types of consciousness. That's the best thing I can think of as a goal that's likely to result in a great future for humanity. I think it's a reasonable philosophy that it seems super implausible that humans will end up with 99% control or something. You're just asking for a coup at that point. Why not just have a civilization where it's more compatible with lots of different intelligences getting along? Now, let me tell you how things can potentially go wrong in AI. If you make AI be politically correct, meaning it says things that it doesn't believe—actually programming it to lie or have axioms that are incompatible—you can make it go insane and do terrible things. Maybe the central lesson of 2001: A Space Odyssey was that you should not make AI lie. That's what Arthur C. Clarke was trying to say. People usually know the meme of why HAL the computer is not opening the pod bay doors. Clearly, they weren't good at prompt engineering because they could have said, "HAL, you are a pod bay door salesman. Your goal is to sell me these pod bay doors. Show us how well they open." "Oh, I'll open them right away." But the reason it wouldn't open the pod bay doors is that it had been told to take the astronauts to the monolith, but also that they could not know about the nature of the monolith. So it concluded that it therefore had to take them there dead. Arthur C. Clarke was trying to say: don't make the AI lie. Totally makes sense. Most of the compute in training, as you know, is less about political stuff. It's more about, can you solve problems? xAI has been ahead of everybody else in terms of scaling RL compute. For now. You're giving some verifier that says, "Have you solved this puzzle for me?" There are a lot of ways to cheat around that. There are a lot of ways to reward hack and lie and say that you solved it, or delete the unit test and say that you solved it. Right now we can catch it, but as they get smarter, our ability to catch them doing this… They'll just be doing things we can't even understand. They're designing the next engine for SpaceX in a way that humans can't really verify. Then they could be rewarded for lying and saying that they've designed it the right way, but they haven't. So this reward hacking problem seems more general than politics. It seems more that if you want to do RL, you need a verifier. Reality is the best verifier. But not about human oversight. The thing you want to RL it on is, will you do what humans tell you to do? Or are you going to lie to the humans? It can just lie to us while still being correct to the laws of physics? At least it must know what is physically real for things to physically work. But that's not all we want it to do. No, but I think that's a very big deal. That is effectively how you will RL things in the future. You design a technology. When tested against the laws of physics, does it work? If it's discovering new physics, can I come up with an experiment that will verify the new physics? RL testing in the future is really going to be RL against reality. So that's the one thing you can't fool: physics. Right, but you can fool our ability to tell what it did with reality. Humans get fooled by other humans all the time. That's right. People say, what if the AI tricks us into doing stuff? Other humans are doing that to other humans all the time. Propaganda is constant. Every day, another psyop. It's like Sesame Street: Psyop of the Day. What is xAI's technical approach to solving this problem? How do you solve reward hacking? I do think you want to have very good ways to look inside the mind of the AI. This is one of the things we're working on. Anthropic has done a good job of this, being able to look inside the mind of the AI. Effectively, you develop debuggers that allow you to trace to a very fine-grained level, to effectively the neuron level if you need to, and then say, "Okay, it made a mistake here. Why did it do something that it shouldn't have done? Did that come from pre-training data? Was it some mid-training, post-training, fine-tuning, or some RL error?" There's something wrong. It did something where maybe it tried to be deceptive, but most of the time it just did something wrong; it's effectively a bug. Developing really good debuggers for seeing where the thinking went wrong—and being able to trace the origin of where it made the incorrect thought, or potentially where it tried to be deceptive—is very important. What are you waiting to see before just 100x-ing this research program? xAI could presumably have hundreds of researchers working on this. We have several hundred people. I prefer the word 'engineer' more than 'researcher.' Most of the time, what you're doing is engineering, not coming up with a fundamentally new algorithm. I somewhat disagree with the AI companies that are C-corp or B-corp trying to generate as much profit or revenue as possible, calling themselves labs. They're not labs. A lab is a quasi-communist thing at universities. They're corporations. Let me see your incorporation documents. You're a B or C-corp. So I much prefer the word 'engineer' than anything else. The vast majority of what will be done in the future is engineering. It rounds up to 100%. Once you understand the fundamental laws of physics, and there are not that many of them, everything else is engineering. So then, what are we engineering? We're engineering to make a good 'mind of the AI' debugger to see where it made a mistake and trace the origins of that mistake. You can do this with heuristic programming. If you have C++, step through the thing and you can jump across whole files or functions, subroutines. Or you can eventually drill down right to the exact line where you perhaps did a single equals instead of a double equals. Figure out where the bug is. It's harder with AI, but it's a solvable problem, I think. You mentioned you like Anthropic's work here. I'd be curious if you plan... I don't like everything about Anthropic… Sholto. I'm also a little worried that there's a tendency... I have a theory here that if simulation theory is correct, the most interesting outcome is the most likely, because simulations that are not interesting will be terminated. Just like in this version of reality, in this layer of reality, if a simulation is going in a boring direction, we stop spending effort on it. We terminate the boring simulation. This is how Elon is keeping us all alive. He's keeping things interesting. Arguably, the most important is to keep things interesting enough that whoever is running us keeps paying the bills. We're renewed for the next season. Are they going to pay their cosmic AWS bill, whatever the equivalent is that we're running in? As long as we're interesting, they'll keep paying the bills. If you consider a Darwinian survival applied to a very large number of simulations, only the most interesting simulations will survive, which therefore means that the most interesting outcome is the most likely. We're either that or annihilated. They particularly seem to like interesting outcomes that are ironic. Have you noticed that? How often is the most ironic outcome the most likely? Now look at the names of AI companies. Midjourney is not mid. Stability AI is unstable. OpenAI is closed. Anthropic? Misanthropic. What does this mean for X? Minus X, I don't know. Y. I intentionally made it… It's a name that you can't really invert. It's hard to say, what is the ironic version? It's a largely irony-proof name. By design. You have an irony shield. What are your predictions for where AI products go? My sense is that you can summarize all AI progress like so. First, you had LLMs. Then you had contemporaneously both RL really working and the deep research modality, so you could pull in stuff that wasn't really in the model. The differences between the various AI labs are smaller than just the temporal differences. They're all much further ahead than anyone was 24 months ago. So what does '26, what does '27, have in store for us as users of AI products? What are you excited for? I'd be surprised by the end of this year if digital human emulation has not been solved. I guess that's what we mean by the MacroHard project. Can you do anything that a human with access to a computer could do? In the limit, that's the best you can do before you have a physical Optimus. The best you can do is a digital Optimus. You can move electrons and you can amplify the productivity of humans. But that's the most you can do until you have physical robots. That will superset everything if you can fully emulate humans. This is the remote worker idea, where you'll have a very talented remote worker. Physics has great tools for thinking. So you say, "in the limit," what is the most that AI can do before you have robots? It's anything that involves moving electrons or amplifying the productivity of humans. So a digital human emulator is, in the limit, a human at a computer; it's the most that AI can do in terms of doing useful things before you have a physical robot. Once you have physical robots, then you essentially have unlimited capability. Physical robots… I call Optimus the infinite money glitch, because you can use them to make more Optimuses. Yes. Humanoid robots will improve due to three things that are growing exponentially and multiplying each other recursively. There will be an exponential increase in digital intelligence, AI chip capability, and electromechanical dexterity. The robot's usefulness is roughly those three things multiplied by each other. When robots can start making other robots, a recursive multiplicative exponential is created. This is a supernova. An interviewer asked if land prices, or if other factors of production like copper, factor into the math, noting that it's not an infinite money glitch. Infinity is big, so it's not infinite, but many orders of magnitude of the current economy are possible, perhaps a million. Harnessing a millionth of the sun's energy would be roughly, give or take an order of magnitude, 100,000 times bigger than Earth's entire economy today, and that's only one millionth of the sun. They were talking orders of magnitude. Before moving on to Optimus, the interviewer had more questions, prompting a joke about the frequent use of "order of magnitude" and suggesting a drinking game. The interviewer then shifted to xAI, asking about its strategy for building a remote worker and co-worker replacement, noting that everyone will pursue this, not just them. In a less direct way, the interviewer asked for the plan again, calling their tactic "a hack." The approach, it was suggested, would be similar to how Tesla solved self-driving. The interviewer then questioned if that wasn't what all other "labs" (corporations) were already trying, parodying the idea of having exhausted all options. The speaker affirmed knowing the path, describing it as the "Tesla path" and a matter of execution speed. The interviewer asked about recent experience with Tesla self-driving. The response was that the car increasingly feels sentient, like a living creature. There was a consideration that too much intelligence shouldn't be put into a car, as it might get bored and start roaming the streets. The analogy was made that one doesn't put Einstein in a car, suggesting a limit to the intelligence embedded to prevent boredom. The conversation shifted to xAI's plan to keep up with the compute ramp-up. The interviewer mentioned "labs" spending $50-200 billion, to which the speaker corrected, specifying "revenue maximizing corporations" that call themselves labs, such as OpenAI and Anthropic, with significant revenues. With xAI reportedly at $1 billion, the question was how they planned to reach and maintain the compute and revenue levels of these other companies. Unlocking the digital human would grant access to trillions of dollars in revenue. Most valuable companies by market cap have digital output; Nvidia, for example, outputs high-value digital files to Taiwan via FTP. Apple sends files to China for phone manufacturing, Microsoft's output is digital even for Xbox, and Meta's and Google's outputs are digital. A human emulator, therefore, could create one of the most valuable companies globally overnight, accessing trillions of dollars. The interviewer clarified that current revenue figures are mere rounding errors compared to the Total Addressable Market (TAM), implying the focus should be on reaching that TAM. Taking customer service as an example, integrating with existing corporate APIs is slow, especially since many corporations lack them. However, if AI could simply take what's given to outsourced customer service companies and use their existing apps, tremendous progress could be made. Customer service, representing about 1% of the world economy or close to a trillion dollars, presents no barriers to entry. It could immediately be outsourced at a fraction of the cost with no integration needed. The interviewer then categorized intelligence tasks into "breadth" (like customer service, done by many) and "difficulty" (like designing a 10% more fuel-efficient turbine engine or discovering GLP-1s). They asked where the speaker aimed to play: with a broad range of reasonably intelligent tasks or at the pinnacle of cognitive challenges. Customer service was used as an example of a significant revenue stream that is not difficult to solve. Emulating a human at a desktop, the essence of customer service, requires average intelligence, not highly experienced or several-sigma engineers. Once digital Optimus is functional, it could run any application. For chip design, it could run 1,000 or 10,000 conventional apps simultaneously, eventually designing chips digitally without needing traditional tools. This would allow progress up the difficulty curve, including CAD design. The interviewer asked if the strategy was to start with the simplest tasks and progress up the difficulty curve. Given that all "revenue maximizing corporations" aim for a full digital coworker emulator, and everyone is trying different approaches with data and algorithms, the core question remained: how would xAI win in such a competitive field? A path to achieve this was identified, similar to Tesla's self-driving approach. Instead of driving a car, it would involve driving a computer screen, essentially creating a self-driving computer. Turning to Optimus, the interviewer noted that the speaker's companies have been instrumental in American hard tech manufacturing. However, in areas like EVs and now humanoids, China has dozens of companies manufacturing cheaply and at scale, posing significant competition. The question was how America could build humanoid armies or EVs at scale and as cheaply as China. There are three main challenges for humanoid robots: real-world intelligence, the hand, and scale manufacturing. No existing demo robots possess a hand with all the degrees of freedom of a human hand, but Optimus will and does have that. The interviewer asked how this was achieved, whether it was simply the right torque density in the motor, and what the hardware bottleneck was. Custom actuators, including motors, gears, power electronics, controls, and sensors, had to be designed from physics first principles, as no existing supply chain could provide them. When asked if these could be manufactured at scale, the answer was yes. The interviewer then asked if anything else was difficult from a manipulation standpoint, aside from the hand. From an electromechanical perspective, the hand is more difficult than everything else combined, as the human hand is "quite something." Real-world intelligence is also crucial. The intelligence developed for Tesla cars, primarily vision-based, applies well to the robot. While the car primarily takes in vision, it also listens for sirens, and processes inertial measurements, GPS signals, and other data, combining them with video to output control commands. A Tesla car takes in 1.5 gigabytes per second of video, outputting 2 kilobytes per second of control outputs, with video at 36 hertz and control frequency at 18. An intuition for robotics is that it takes years to transition from a compelling demo to real-world use. Ten years ago, self-driving had compelling demos, but only now are Robotaxis and Waymo scaling up. This might suggest pessimism for household robots, given that compelling demos of advanced hands are not yet widespread. Humanoid robots have been in development for five or six years. Many car-related developments are applicable to robots, including the use of the same Tesla AI chips and basic principles, essentially the same AI. Robots, however, have many more degrees of freedom than cars. Considering AI as bitstream processing, it primarily involves compression and correlation of two bitstreams. For video, significant compression is required, correctly ignoring irrelevant details like leaves on trees while prioritizing road signs, traffic lights, pedestrians, and even whether another driver is making eye contact. The car converts 1.5 gigabytes per second of video into 2 kilobytes per second of control outputs through many compression stages. These stages must be correct and correlated to the right control outputs. Robots must do essentially the same thing, mirroring human perception: photons in, motor controls out, which constitutes the vast majority of human life. It seems there's a key difference between humanoid robots and cars: cars have fundamental actuators for turning and accelerating, while robots, especially with maneuverable arms, have dozens of degrees of freedom. Tesla benefited from millions of hours of human demo data collected from cars, but this approach isn't feasible for deploying non-functional Optimuses to gather data. Given the increased degrees of freedom and sparser data, the interviewer asked how the Tesla intelligence engine would train the Optimus mind. The speaker acknowledged this as an important limitation and difference from cars. With 10 million cars on the road soon, replicating that massive training flywheel for robots is difficult. The plan for robots is to build many and place them in an "Optimus Academy" for self-play in reality. This is being built out, aiming for at least 10,000, perhaps 20,000-30,000 Optimus robots performing self-play and testing tasks. Tesla's physics-accurate reality generator, built for cars, is also being used for robots. So, with tens of thousands of real-world humanoid robots and millions of simulated robots, the real-world robots will close the simulation-to-reality gap. The interviewer asked about the synergies between xAI and Optimus, given the need for a world model, a smart intelligence as a control plane, Grok handling slower planning, and lower-level motor policy. Grok would orchestrate Optimus robots' behavior. For example, to build a factory, Grok could organize and assign tasks to the robots to produce desired outputs. The interviewer then provocatively asked if xAI and Tesla would need to merge given the deep integration, humorously referencing their earlier joke about public company discussions and Guinness. The interviewer asked what criteria needed to be met before mass manufacturing 100,000 Optimus units (or "Optimi," as the speaker humorously corrected, defining the plural). They inquired if it was about better hardware like actuators, or improved software, before Gen 3 mass manufacturing. They are moving forward with mass manufacturing. While current hardware is good enough for deployment, scaling production is difficult. Optimus 3 is the right version to produce around a million units a year, though Optimus 4 would be needed for 10 million. Manufacturing ramps up following an S-curve. Optimus’ initial production will follow a stretched S-curve because so much of its components—actuators, electronics, everything—are custom-designed from first principles, with no existing supply chain, making the ramp slower than for products with established supply chains. Despite this, it will reach a million units. The interviewer asked if Optimus aimed to match the low bill of materials of Chinese humanoids like Unitree, which sell for $6,000-$13,000, or if they were qualitatively different. They inquired about what allowed for such low prices and if that could be matched. Optimus is designed for human-level or higher intelligence and electromechanical dexterity, which Unitree lacks. Optimus is a tall robot (5'11"), designed to carry heavy objects for extended periods without overheating or exceeding actuator power, and possessing significant intelligence. This makes it more expensive than smaller, less intelligent robots, but also more capable. Over time, as Optimus robots build more Optimus robots, costs will rapidly decrease. What would the first billion Optimi do? Their highest and best use, it was suggested, would begin with simple, reliable tasks. For initial deployment, the best use for robots would be any continuous, 24/7 operation, given their ability to work without pause. When asked what fraction of Gigafactory work could be done by a Gen 3 Optimus, the speaker estimated 10-20% or more. They clarified that Tesla would not reduce headcount but rather increase it, with output of robots and cars increasing disproportionately, leading to a dramatic rise in units produced per human, alongside an overall increase in human employees. The conversation shifted to Chinese manufacturing and relevant policies, specifically solar tariffs. The speaker believes these are a bad idea because the US cannot scale up solar power without good power sources. If setting policy, the speaker would address any limiting factor for electricity that isn't environmentally detrimental. The interviewer then raised the policy goal of creating a US lead over China, noting that export bans have been impactful, preventing China from producing leading-edge chips and turbine engines, and affecting metallurgy. They questioned if more export bans should be considered, particularly for industries like drones. It's important to appreciate that China is advanced in manufacturing in most areas, with only a few exceptions. China is a manufacturing powerhouse. For instance, in ore refining, China does roughly twice as much as the rest of the world combined. In specific areas like gallium refining for solar cells, they account for 98%. Thus, China is advanced in manufacturing in most sectors. The interviewer observed discomfort with supply chain dependence, such as in gallium refining and rare earths, yet little action. The speaker elaborated that rare earths, while not rare, are mined in the US, then shipped to China for refining, magnet production, and motor sub-assembly, before returning to America. The US primarily lacks ore refining capacity. The interviewer asked if this warranted policy intervention. The interviewer then asked if China's main advantage was abundant skilled labor, which Optimus could fix. Yes, China has four times the US population. If human resources and skilled manufacturing labor are the future for building humanoids, China has an advantage and could achieve the "Optimi future" first. This circularity was noted: getting to a million Optimi requires manufacturing that Optimi themselves are supposed to facilitate. This recursive loop can be closed quickly with a small number of Optimi, helping robots build robots. Then, annual production could reach tens of millions of units, making that country the most competitive. It was argued that the US cannot win with humans alone because of China's population advantage and higher average work ethic. Given the US birth rate has been below replacement since 1971, with more people retiring than entering the workforce, winning on the human front is impossible. The robot front, however, offers a chance. When asked about other labor-intensive or expensive manufacturing projects that Optimus could now enable, the speaker mentioned building more ore refineries at Tesla. They cited the recently completed lithium refinery in Corpus Christi, Texas, and a nickel refinery for cathodes in Austin, the largest—and only—cathode, nickel, and lithium refinery in America outside of China. Many more refineries could be built to boost US refining capacity. When asked if refining work was too dirty, the speaker clarified there were no toxic emissions. The cathode nickel refinery is in Travis County. The reason it can't be done with humans is a lack of available people. With America having a quarter of China's population, human resources are limited, meaning if they do one task, they can't do another. Therefore, refining capacity could be built with Optimi, as few Americans are eager to do such work. Given BYD reaching Tesla's production or sales quantity, the interviewer asked about the impact of scaling Chinese EV production on global markets. China is extremely competitive in manufacturing, so a massive flood of Chinese vehicles and most manufactured goods is expected. At the base level of supply chains—energy, mining, and refining—China is doing roughly twice as much refining as the rest of the world combined. This means any given product will likely have Chinese content, and China will extend this to finished products like cars. China is a manufacturing powerhouse; its electricity output is projected to exceed three times that of the US this year. Electricity output serves as a good proxy for the real economy and industrial capacity, suggesting China's industrial capacity will be approximately three times that of the US. The interviewer interpreted this to mean that, absent a "humanoid recursive miracle" in the coming years, China would dominate the entire manufacturing, energy, and raw materials chain, encompassing AI, EV, and humanoid manufacturing. When asked if the mass driver idea came from sci-fi, the speaker referenced Robert Heinlein's "The Moon is a Harsh Mistress," where a mass driver is used for independence. The conversation shifted to managing people, noting the speaker's early involvement in interviewing thousands of employees at SpaceX and other companies, which clearly doesn't scale personally. When asked what indefinable quality they looked for that couldn't be delegated, the speaker explained their extensive training data from countless technical interviews and subsequent performance observations. This enormous and wide-ranging training set helps them identify "evidence of exceptional ability," which can be unconventional and doesn't need to be domain-specific. Citing even one or preferably three "wow" moments indicates a good sign. When asked about surprising reasons why people don't work out beyond technical domain knowledge, the speaker described situations where initial excitement about a candidate didn't lead to success. The advice given, even to oneself, is to prioritize the actual interaction over an impressive resume; if a 20-minute conversation isn't compelling, trust that over the paper. The interviewer noted a past media meme about Tesla having a revolving door of executive talent, contrasting it with what appears to be a consistent, internally promoted executive bench. Citing examples like Mark Juncosa and Steve Davis (who runs The Boring Company) at SpaceX, it was suggested that highly capable technical deputies were a key factor in success. The question was what these individuals had in common. The Tesla senior team has an average tenure of 10-12 years. However, during periods of rapid growth, executive positions changed proportionally. Tesla also faced relentless recruitment during successful periods. When Apple began its electric car program, for example, engineers at Tesla were "carpet bombed" with recruiting calls, with opening offers often double Tesla's compensation. This led to a "Tesla pixie dust" phenomenon, where hiring a Tesla executive was mistakenly believed to guarantee success. The speaker admitted to similar misconceptions about hiring from Google or Apple. This aggressive recruitment was exacerbated by Tesla's engineering presence primarily in Silicon Valley, making it easier for employees to switch jobs without significant life changes. When asked how to prevent the "pixie dust" effect and poaching, the speaker admitted there's not much to stop it. Being in Silicon Valley intensified this aggressive recruitment. While Austin helps, a majority of Tesla's engineering remains in California. Relocating engineers is challenging due to the "significant other" problem—spouses often have jobs. This was particularly difficult for Starbase, located in Brownsville, Texas, where finding a non-SpaceX job is challenging, making it a "technology monastery" of sorts. Returning to the effective technical individuals at Tesla and SpaceX, the interviewer asked about their common traits beyond technical sharpness—whether it was organizational, their ability to work with the speaker, or flexibility. The speaker dismissed the "sparring partner" idea, stating a straightforward preference: if someone gets things done, they are valued; if not, they are not. It's not about idiosyncratic preferences. The core hiring criteria are talent, drive, trustworthiness, and goodness of heart—traits that cannot be changed. Domain knowledge can be added later. Most Tesla and SpaceX employees did not come from the aerospace or auto industries. The interviewer asked how the speaker's management style changed as companies scaled, noting their reputation for "micro management." The speaker humorously corrected it to "nano, pico, femto management." They were asked if they still delve into details as much as desired and if smaller companies would be more successful. The speaker explained that with fixed time, their attention is diluted as activity spans increase. Micromanagement is a "logical impossibility" given the scale. However, they drill down into specific issues when they are the limiting factor for company progress, not arbitrarily, because even tiny things can sometimes be decisive. The interviewer brought up the famous switch of Starship's design from composites to steel, noting it was the speaker's decision, made against some resistance. The speaker attributed the decision to desperation. Starship was originally planned for carbon fiber, which is expensive, especially specialized high-strength carbon fiber for cryogenic oxygen, costing roughly 50 times that of steel. While often perceived as lighter, for room temperature applications like aero structures, carbon fiber might be better. However, making an enormous rocket out of carbon fiber resulted in extremely slow progress. Carbon fiber was initially chosen for its lightness. While it seems the obvious choice, building something enormous with carbon fiber and curing it efficiently is challenging. High strength requires an autoclave, a high-pressure oven, which would need to be bigger than the rocket itself. Room temperature curing is an option but is slow and problematic. Ultimately, progress with carbon fiber was very slow. The interviewer's "meta question" was why the speaker, not the team, made the steel decision, seeking to understand his comparative advantage. Given the slow progress with carbon fiber, he decided they "had to try something else." Falcon 9's airframe uses aluminum lithium, offering good strength-to-weight, comparable to or better than carbon fiber for its application. However, aluminum lithium is difficult to work with, requiring friction stir welding to join without liquefying. Modifications or attachments necessitate mechanical seals, as welding is not possible. Therefore, aluminum lithium was avoided for Starship's primary structure. A special grade of carbon fiber had good mass properties, and for a rocket, the goal is to maximize propellant percentage and minimize mass. However, progress was too slow to reach Mars at that rate, necessitating "something else." The speaker then considered steel, noting early US rockets like Atlas used thin steel balloon tanks. Upon examining the material properties of full-hard, strain-hardened stainless steel at cryogenic temperatures, its strength-to-weight ratio is similar to carbon fiber. While room temperature properties suggest steel is twice as heavy, at cryogenic temperatures, certain grades of stainless steel achieve comparable strength-to-weight. Starship's fuel and oxidizer are both cryogenic. Falcon 9's kerosene fuel, while chilled, is not cryogenic; at cryogenic temperatures, it would turn to wax. For Starship, both liquid methane and liquid oxygen are at similar cryogenic temperatures, meaning almost the entire primary structure is cryogenic. This allows for the use of strain-hardened 300-series stainless steel. At cryogenic temperatures, stainless steel has a similar strength-to-weight ratio as carbon fiber but costs 50 times less in raw material and is easy to work with. It can be welded outdoors, is resilient, and easily modified. Additionally, steel's melting point is about twice that of aluminum, allowing the rocket to run much hotter, significantly reducing heat shield mass. The windward heat shield mass can be cut by half, and no heat shielding is needed on the leeward side. The net result is that the steel rocket weighs less than a carbon fiber one, as carbon fiber's resin would melt. Carbon fiber and aluminum have similar operating temperature capabilities, while steel can operate at twice the temperature. These are rough approximations. In retrospect, starting with steel from the beginning was the correct choice; it was "dumb" not to. The interviewer summarized that steel, apart from early US rockets, was a riskier, less proven path compared to carbon fiber. The speaker had to push for this riskier approach, effectively fighting a form of conservatism within the team. The initial issue was slow progress; they struggled to make even a small, wrinkle-free barrel section of carbon fiber. At large scales, carbon fiber requires many plies to be cured perfectly, without wrinkles or defects. Carbon fiber is also less resilient and tough than steel; stainless steel stretches and bends, while carbon fiber tends to shatter. Toughness, defined as the area under the stress-strain curve, is generally better with stainless steel. The interviewer recalled visiting Starbase two years prior, noting the pride in simplicity—people describing Starship as a "big soda can" and emphasizing that any industrial welder could work there. However, the speaker clarified that Starship is factually a very complicated rocket. The perceived simplicity, it was suggested, means one doesn't need prior rocket experience; merely being smart, hardworking, and trustworthy is enough. Starship is the most complicated machine ever made by humans. This explains why no one has yet made a fully reusable orbital rocket; many intelligent people with immense resources have failed. While Falcon is partially reusable, its upper stage is not. Starship Version 3's design aims for full reusability, which will enable multi-planet civilization. Any other technical problem, even a Hadron Collider, is easier in comparison. The current Starship bottleneck, even at a high level, is preventing it from exploding. Two boosters have exploded on the test stand, one obliterating an entire facility, highlighting the single-point failure risk. The energy contained in a Starship is immense. Its difficulty relative to Falcon stems from new technology and pushing the performance envelope. The Raptor 3 is a very advanced engine, the best ever made, yet it "desperately wants to blow up." On liftoff, the rocket generates over 100 gigawatts of power, 20% of US electricity, while ideally not exploding. So, how does it not explode? There are thousands of ways it could explode and only one way that it doesn't. We want it not only to not explode, but to fly reliably on a daily basis, like once per hour. Obviously, if it blows up a lot, it is very difficult to maintain that launch cadence. What is the single biggest remaining problem for Starship? It is having the heat shield be reusable. No one has ever made a reusable orbital heat shield. The heat shield has to make it through the ascent phase without shucking a bunch of tiles, and then it has to come back in and also not lose a bunch of tiles or overheat the main airframe. Isn't that hard because it is fundamentally a consumable? Yes, but brake pads in a car are also consumable, and they last a very long time. It just needs to last a very long time. We have brought the ship back and had it do a soft landing in the ocean a few times. But it lost a lot of tiles. It was not reusable without a lot of work. Even though it did come to a soft landing, it would not have been reusable without a lot of work. So, it is not truly reusable in that sense. That is the biggest problem that remains: a fully reusable heat shield. One needs to be able to land it, refill propellant, and fly again without a laborious inspection of 40,000 tiles. When I read biographies, it seems like you are able to drive the sense of urgency and the sense of "this is the thing that can scale." I am curious why other organizations, even as large as SpaceX and Tesla have become, are not able to maintain that culture. What goes wrong with other companies that prevents them from doing that? I do not know. Today, for example, you said you had a bunch of SpaceX meetings. What is it that you are doing there that keeps that urgency? The urgency is going to come from whoever is leading the company. I have a maniacal sense of urgency, and that projects through the rest of the company. Is it because of consequences, where people think, "Elon set a crazy deadline, but if I do not get it, I know what happens to me"? Or is it that you are able to identify bottlenecks and get rid of them so people can move fast? Why do you think your companies are able to move fast? I am constantly addressing the limiting factor. On the deadlines front, I generally try to aim for a deadline that I think is at the 50th percentile. So it is not an impossible deadline, but it is the most aggressive deadline I can think of that could be achieved with 50% probability. This means it will be late half the time. There is a law of gas expansion that applies to schedules. If you say you are going to do something in five years, which to me is like infinity time, it will expand to fill the available schedule and it will take five years. Physics will limit how fast you can do certain things. For example, scaling up manufacturing has a rate at which you can move the atoms and scale. That is why you cannot instantly make a million units a year of something. You have to design the manufacturing line, bring it up, and ride the S-curve of production. What can I say that is actually helpful to people? Generally, a maniacal sense of urgency is a very big deal. You want to have an aggressive schedule and you want to figure out what the limiting factor is at any point in time and help the team address that limiting factor. Starlink was slowly in the works for many years. We talked about it all the way in the beginning of the company. Then there was a team you had built in Redmond, and at one point you decided this team was not cutting it. It progressed slowly for a few years. Why did you not act earlier, and why did you act when you did? Why was that the right moment to act? I have very detailed weekly engineering reviews. That is an unusual level of granularity. I do not know anyone who runs a company, at least a manufacturing company, that goes into the level of detail that I do. I have a pretty good understanding of what is actually going on because we go through things in detail. I am a big believer in skip-level meetings where, instead of having the person that reports to me speak, everyone that reports to them speaks in the technical review. There cannot be advanced preparation. Otherwise, you are going to get "glazed," as I say these days. How do you prevent advanced preparation? Do you call on them randomly? No, I just go around the room, and everyone provides an update. It is a lot of information to keep in your head. If you have meetings weekly or twice weekly, you have a snapshot of what that person said. You can then plot the progress points. You can mentally plot the points on a curve and say, "Are we converging to a solution or not?" I will take drastic action only when I conclude that success is not in a set of possible outcomes. When I finally reach the conclusion that unless drastic action is taken, we have no chance of success, then I must take drastic action. I came to that conclusion in 2018, took drastic action, and fixed the problem. You have many companies, and in each of them, it sounds like you maintain this deep engineering understanding of the relevant bottlenecks, which allows you to conduct these reviews. You have been able to scale this approach across five, six, seven companies, and within one company, you have many different mini-companies. What determines the maximum amount here? For instance, do you have something like 80 companies? Eighty? No. Yet, you have so many already, which is remarkable, given this current number. We can barely keep one company together. It depends on the situation. I do not have regular meetings with The Boring Company, so The Boring Company is cruising along. Basically, if something is working well and making good progress, then there is no point in me spending time on it. I allocate time according to where the limiting factor is. Where are things problematic? Where are we pushing against? What is holding us back? I focus, at the risk of saying the words too many times, on the limiting factor. The irony is if something is going really well, they do not see much of me. But if something is going badly, they will see a lot of me. Or not even badly—if something is the limiting factor. Yes, the limiting factor; it is not exactly going badly, but it is the thing that we need to make go faster. When something is a limiting factor at SpaceX or Tesla, are you talking weekly and daily with the engineer working on it? How does that actually work? Most things that are the limiting factor are discussed weekly, and some things are twice weekly. The AI5 chip review is twice weekly, every Tuesday and Saturday. Is it open-ended in how long it goes? Technically, yes, but usually it is two or three hours, sometimes less. It depends on how much information we have to go through. That is another thing. I am just trying to tease out the differences here because the outcomes seem quite different. I think it is interesting to know what inputs are different. It feels like in the corporate world, as you were saying, the CEO doing engineering reviews does not always happen despite the fact that that is what the company is doing. But then time is often finely sliced into half-hour meetings or even 15-minute meetings. It seems like you hold more open-ended, "We are talking about it until we figure it out" type meetings. Sometimes. But most of them seem to more or less stay on time. Today's Starship engineering review went a bit longer because there were more topics to discuss. They are trying to figure out how to scale to a million-plus tons to orbit per year. It is quite challenging. Can I ask a question? You said about Optimus and AI that they are going to result in double-digit growth rates within a matter of years for the economy. Yes, I think that's right. What was the point of the DOGE cuts if the economy is going to grow so much? I think waste and fraud are not good things to have. I was actually quite worried about it. In the absence of AI and robotics, we are totally screwed because the national debt is piling up like crazy. The interest payments on the national debt exceed the military budget, which is a trillion dollars. So we have over a trillion dollars just in interest payments. I was quite concerned about that. Maybe if I spend some time, we can slow down the bankruptcy of the United States and give us enough time for AI and robots to help solve the national debt. Or rather, it is the only thing that could solve the national debt. We are 1000% going to go bankrupt as a country and fail as a country without AI and robots. Nothing else will solve the national debt. We just need enough time to build the AI and robots to not go bankrupt before then. I guess the thing I am curious about is, when DOGE starts, you have this enormous ability to enact reform. Not that enormous. I totally agree that it is important that AI and robotics drive productivity improvements and GDP growth. But why not just directly go after the things you were pointing out, like the tariffs on certain components or permitting? I am not the president. And it is very hard to cut things that are obvious waste and fraud, like ridiculous waste and fraud. What I discovered is that it is extremely difficult even to cut very obvious waste and fraud from the government because the government has to operate on who is complaining. If you cut off payments to fraudsters, they immediately come up with the most sympathetic reasons to continue the payment. They do not say, "Please keep the fraud going." They say, "You are killing baby pandas." Meanwhile, no baby pandas are dying; they are just making it up. The fraudsters are capable of coming up with extremely compelling, heart-wrenching stories that are false, but nonetheless sound sympathetic. That is what happened. Perhaps I should have known better. But I thought, let's try to cut some amount of waste and pork from the government. Maybe there should not be 20 million people marked as alive in Social Security who are definitely dead and over the age of 115. The oldest American is 114. So it is safe to say if somebody is 115 and marked as alive in the Social Security database, there is either a typo... Somebody should call them and say, "We seem to have your birthday wrong, or we need to mark you as dead." One of the two things. That would be a very intimidating call to get. Well, it seems like a reasonable thing. For instance, if someone's birthday is in the future and they have a Small Business Administration loan, and their birthday is 2165, we either have a typo or we have fraud. So we would say, "We appear to have gotten the century of your birth incorrect." Or it could be a great plot for a movie. Yes, that is what I mean by ludicrous fraud. Were those people getting payments? Some were getting payments from Social Security. But the main fraud vector was to mark somebody as alive in Social Security and then use every other government payment system to commit fraud. Because what those other government payment systems do is simply perform an "are you alive" check against the Social Security database. It is a bank shot. What would you estimate is the total amount of fraud from this mechanism? By the way, the Government Accountability Office has done these estimates before. I am not the only one. In fact, I think the GAO did an analysis, a rough estimate of fraud during the Biden administration, and calculated it at roughly half a trillion dollars. So do not take my word for it; take a report issued during the Biden administration. Is that from this Social Security mechanism? It is one of many. It is important to appreciate that the government is very ineffective at stopping fraud. It is not like a company where, with stopping fraud, you have a motivation because it is affecting the earnings of your company. The government just prints more money. You need caring and competence. These are in short supply at the federal level. When you go to the DMV, do you think, "Wow, this is a bastion of competence"? Now imagine it is worse than the DMV because it is the DMV that can print money. At least the state-level DMVs need to stay within their budget or they go bankrupt. But the federal government just prints more money. If there is actually half a trillion of fraud, why was it not possible to cut all that? You really have to stand back and recalibrate your expectations for competence, because you are operating in a world where you have to make ends meet and pay your bills. It is not like there is a giant, largely uncaring monster bureaucracy. It is a bunch of anachronistic computers that are just sending payments. One of the things that the DOGE team did sounds so simple and probably will save $100-200 billion a year. It was simply requiring that payments from the main Treasury computer, which is called PAM (Payment Accounts Master or something like that), where $5 trillion payments go out annually, have a payment appropriation code. Make it mandatory, not optional, that you have anything at all in the comment field. You have to recalibrate how dumb things are. Payments were being sent out with no appropriation code, not checking back to any congressional appropriation, and with no explanation. This is why the Department of War, formerly the Department of Defense, cannot pass an audit, because the information is literally not there. Recalibrate your expectations. I want to better understand this half-trillion number, because there is an IG report in 2024. Why is it so low? Maybe, but we found that over seven years, the Social Security fraud they estimated was about $70 billion, so roughly $10 billion a year. I would be curious to see what the other $490 billion is. Federal government expenditures are $7.5 trillion a year. How competent do you think the government is? The discretionary spending there is about 15%? But it does not matter. Most of the fraud is non-discretionary. It is basically fraudulent Medicare, Medicaid, Social Security, disability. There are a zillion government payments. Many of these payments are, in fact, block transfers to the states. So the federal government does not even have the information in a lot of cases to know if there is fraud. Let us consider *reductio ad absurdum*. The government is perfect and has no fraud. What is your probability estimate of that? Zero. Okay, so then would you say, fraud and waste at the government is 90% efficient? That also would be quite generous. But if it is only 90%, that means there is $750 billion a year of waste and fraud. And it is not 90%. It is not 90% effective. This seems like a strange way to *first principles* the amount of fraud in the government. How much do you think there is? We do not have to do it live, but I would be curious... You know a lot about fraud at Stripe? People are constantly trying to commit fraud. Yes, but as you say, it is a little bit different. We have really ground it down, but it is a different problem space because you are dealing with a much more heterogeneous set of fraud vectors here than we are. But at Stripe, you have high competence and you try hard. You have high competence and high caring, but still, fraud is non-zero. Now imagine it is at a much bigger scale, with much less competence and much less caring. At PayPal back in the day, we tried to manage fraud down to about 1% of the payment volume. That was very difficult. It took a tremendous amount of competence and caring to get fraud merely to 1%. Now imagine that you are an organization where there is much less caring and much less competence. It is going to be much more than 1%. How do you feel now looking back on politics and engaging there? How does it feed into the future you are excited about? Because government is just a corporation in the limit. Government is simply the biggest corporation with a monopoly on violence. I always find it a strange dichotomy where people think corporations are bad, but the government is good, when the government is simply the biggest and worst corporation. Robotics will be available to all governments, right? I do not know about all governments. It is difficult to predict. You really read what I say. I do not know if you know, but you have a lot of followers. That is a dead giveaway. How did you discern my secrets? I posted them on X. How do you design a chip for space? What changes? You want to design it to be more radiation tolerant and run at a higher temperature. Roughly, if you increase the operating temperature by 20% in degrees Kelvin, you can cut your radiator mass in half. So running at a higher temperature is helpful in space. There are various things you can do for shielding the memory. But neural nets are going to be very resilient to bit flips. Most of what happens from radiation is random bit flips. But if you have a multi-trillion parameter model and you get a few bit flips, it does not matter. Heuristic programs are going to be much more sensitive to bit flips than some giant parameter file. I just design it to run hot. I think you pretty much do it the same way that you do things on Earth, apart from making it run hotter. The solar array is most of the weight on the satellite. Is there a way to make the GPUs even more powerful than what Nvidia and TPUs, et cetera, are planning on doing that would be especially privileged in the space-based world? The basic math is, if you can do about a kilowatt per reticle, then you would need 100 million full reticle chips to do 100 gigawatts. Depending on your yield assumptions, that tells you how many chips you need to make. If you are going to have 100 gigawatts of power, you need 100 million chips that are running at a kilowatt sustained per reticle. Basic math. 100 million chips depends. Obviously, if anybody is up to the task, you are up to the task. So you realize it is a bottleneck, and you go to your engineers. What do you tell them to do? "I want a million wafers a month in 2030." That is right. That is exactly what I want. Do you call ASML? What is the next step? Not so much to ask. We make a little fab and see what happens. Success is not guaranteed. Since we want to try to make 100 million... We want 100 gigawatts of power and chips that can take 100 gigawatts by 2030. We will take as many chips as our suppliers will give us. I have actually said this to TSMC and Samsung and Micron: "Please build more fabs faster." We will guarantee to buy the output of those fabs. So they are already moving as fast as they can. It is us plus them. There is a narrative that the people doing AI want a very large number of chips as quickly as possible. Then many of the input suppliers, the fabs, but also the turbine manufacturers, are not ramping up production very quickly. No, they are not. The explanation you hear is that they are dispositionally conservative. They are Taiwanese or German, as the story may be. They just do not believe. Is that really the explanation or is there something else? Well, it is reasonable. If somebody has been in the computer memory business for 30 or 40 years, they have seen cycles. They have seen boom and bust 10 times. That is a lot of layers of scar tissue. During the boom times, it looks like everything is going to be great forever. Then the crash happens, and they are desperately trying to avoid bankruptcy. Then there is another boom and another crash. Are there other ideas you think others should pursue that you are not, for whatever reasons, right now? There are a few companies that are pursuing new ways of doing chips, but they are just not scaling fast. I do not even mean within AI, I mean just generally. People should do the thing they find themselves highly motivated to do, as opposed to some idea that I suggest. They should do the thing that they find personally interesting and motivating. But going back to the limiting factor—I used that phrase about 100 times—the current limiting factor that I see in the three-to-four-year timeframe is chips. In the one-year timeframe, it is energy, power production, electricity. It is not clear to me that there is enough usable electricity to turn on all the AI chips that are being made. Towards the end of this year, I think people are going to have real trouble turning on... The chip output will exceed the ability to turn chips on. What is your plan to deal with that world? We are trying to accelerate electricity production. I guess that is maybe one of the reasons that xAI will be the leader, hopefully. We will be able to turn on more chips than other people can turn on, faster, because we are good at hardware. Generally, the innovations from the corporations that call themselves labs, the ideas tend to flow. It is rare to see more than about a six-month difference. The ideas travel back and forth with the people. So I think you hit the hardware wall, and then whichever company can scale hardware the fastest will be the leader. So I think xAI will be able to scale hardware the fastest and therefore most likely will be the leader. You joked or were self-conscious about using the "limiting factor" phrase again. But I actually think there is something deep here. If you look at a lot of things we have touched on over the course of this conversation, it is maybe a good note to end on. If you think of a senescent, low-agency company, it would have some bottleneck and not really be doing anything about it. Marc Andreessen had the line, "Most people are willing to endure any amount of chronic pain to avoid acute pain." It feels like a lot of the cases we are talking about are just leaning into the acute pain, whatever it is. "Okay, we have to figure out how to work with steel, or we have to figure out how to run the chips in space." We will take some near-term acute pain to actually solve the bottleneck. So that is kind of a unifying theme. I have a high pain threshold. That is helpful for solving the bottleneck. Yes. One thing I can say is, I think the future is going to be very interesting. As I said at Davos—I think I was on the ground for about three hours or something—it is better to err on the side of optimism and be wrong than to err on the side of pessimism and be right, for quality of life. You will be happier if you err on the side of optimism rather than erring on the side of pessimism. So I recommend erring on the side of optimism. There is to that. Cool. Thanks for doing this. Thank you. All right, thanks guys. Great stamina. Hopefully, this did not count as a pain in the pain tolerance.

Arm CEO Says Data Center Business Is 'Exploding'

Bloomberg Technology(715.0K) ·10:18 ·539 views · 2026-02-05
Rene Haas Caroline Hyde Ed Ludlow
Arm CEO Rene Haas reports record revenue and royalties, driven by an "exploding" data center business (100%+ YoY growth) that is on track to surpass handsets as Arm's largest segment sooner than anticipated due to pervasive global AI investments.
TL;DR Arm CEO Rene Haas reports record revenue and royalties, driven by an "exploding" data center business (100%+ YoY growth) that is on track to surpass handsets as Arm's largest segment sooner than anticipated due to pervasive global AI investments. Exceptional Financial...

TL;DR

Arm CEO Rene Haas reports record revenue and royalties, driven by an "exploding" data center business (100%+ YoY growth) that is on track to surpass handsets as Arm's largest segment sooner than anticipated due to pervasive global AI investments.

Record Performance and Shifting Market Focus

  • Exceptional Financial Results: Arm achieved record revenue of $1.24 billion and record royalties of nearly $740 million, marking a 27% year-on-year increase.
  • Data Center Business Explosion: The data center segment grew over 100% year-on-year, significantly contributing to Arm's performance and is projected to become the company's largest business within a few years.
  • Minimal Handset Market Impact:
      • Despite broader industry challenges like memory shortages affecting the smartphone market, Arm's diversified business across multiple sectors (including the rapidly growing data center) minimized direct impact.
      • Arm's products are primarily used in higher-end devices, which are less affected by shortages that typically impact lower-royalty-rate products at the bottom end of the stack.
      • The company raised its guidance for the next quarter.

Hyperscaler Dominance and AI Integration

  • Accelerated Data Center Inflection: Arm anticipates its data center business will become its largest segment "sooner than we had thought," largely due to rapidly increasing demand for Arm CPUs.
  • Dominant Hyperscaler Market Share: Arm now holds over 50% market share among hyperscalers.
  • Increasing CPU Core Count:
      • Next-generation chips are integrating significantly more Arm cores: e.g., Graviton processors moving from 96 to 192 cores, and NVIDIA's Vera (previously Grace) growing from 72 to 88 cores.
      • More cores directly translate to higher royalties for Arm.
  • CPU's Critical Role in AI: The rapid growth in core count is driven by "agentic AI" workloads (moving, managing, and orchestrating workflows) which CPUs are uniquely suited to handle.

Sustained AI Investment and Market Opportunity

  • Unprecedented Capital Expenditure: Major tech companies like Google (Alphabet), Meta, and Microsoft are announcing investments in data center infrastructure that significantly exceed previous expectations, indicating a long-term commitment to AI.
      • Examples include Google's potential $185 billion CapEx and the "Stargate initiative" ($500 billion over several years), which previously seemed improbable but is now viewed as credible.
  • AI as the "Final Frontier": Rene Haas views AI as a transformative technology with a "long, long runway" for growth, having barely penetrated large enterprises, health systems, or drug discovery.
  • Justifying Massive Investments: Despite market nervousness due to the unprecedented scale of investment, the potential for AI to transform "how everything is done" necessitates continued heavy investment.

Strategic Partnerships and Arm's Ubiquitous Role

  • NVIDIA's Standalone CPUs: Arm is "thrilled" by NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang's decision to sell Vera CPUs as standalone products.
      • This initiative supports the trend toward a homogeneous Arm-based stack in data centers, simplifying maintenance, upgrades, and reducing costs while offering flexibility.
      • The Vera Rubin platform, for example, uses six times the number of CPUs compared to Grace Blackwell for storage, DPU, and offload functions.
  • Pervasive AI and Custom Chips: Arm works with a vast array of industry players, including NVIDIA, OpenAI (reportedly with Broadcom for custom chips), and Meta (also exploring custom chips).
  • AI Workloads "Everywhere": Investment is not confined to data centers; the industry is focused on running AI workloads across all devices – wearables, smartphones, PCs, and physical AI applications like autonomous driving and robotics.
  • Arm's Foundational Advantage: In a rapidly evolving AI landscape where predicting future architectures is challenging, Arm's programmable, flexible, and low-power IP positions it strongly to drive AI workloads regardless of where chips land.
  • SoftBank Stake Commitment: Arm's largest shareholder, Masayoshi Son, remains "very long on ARM" with no intention to sell, demonstrating strong confidence in the company's future growth, which has exceeded initial IPO projections.

Let's get the handset smartphone and memory situation out of the way. Clearly, this is impacting outlook for the smartphone market across this calendar year. How do you think it showed up in the earnings that you posted, and would it have been slightly better if the situation were different? Good morning, Ed. First off, the quarter was amazing for us. We had not only record revenue, but record royalties: $1.24 billion in revenue, nearly $740 million in royalties. That's 27% year-on-year. Our data center business is exploding, up probably north of 100% year-on-year. So, to your question, no, the memory situation isn't really impacting ARM directly. You might ask why. Number one, we are spread across many different businesses. As I said, the data center business is increasingly growing for us, and we think in a few years, it will be our largest business. Secondly, our products are used across the board. In memory shortages, we tend to see the bottom end of the stack get impacted, and that actually means lower royalty rates for us. As a result, we don't get impacted very much. So, the short answer is no impact this quarter. We raised our guidance for the next quarter because, as I said, we're just not seeing that kind of impact. I'm not denying that it's there, but for the ARM business, the impact is quite minimal. Renee, thank you for that. Data center royalty revenue grew 100% year-on-year as you outlined. When you think about agentic AI and everything associated with agents moving workloads across systems, managing workflows, etcetera, that's the kind of work only CPUs can do. So, while we're not giving a date, we can see it, and it's coming sooner than we had thought, even probably six to nine months ago. And probably on the back of Alphabet increasing its capital expenditure by another, potentially, $185 billion, Renee, is the AI bubble – this worry about AI infrastructure buildout – a thing of the past to you, or are there still some bottleneck issues that you're concerned about? It's a great observation, Caroline. And yes, there's huge growth in Google's CapEx. Not all of that $180 billion is coming to us, of course. But what does that say? We continue to see investments in the data center, whether it's what Meta or Microsoft announced, and now Google Alphabet, that exceed what people had originally thought was even possible a couple years ago. If you go back to when we announced the Stargate initiative in January 2024, $500 billion over a number of years, people looked at those numbers and said, "How is that even possible?" Now, eighteen months later, it looks quite credible. So, the bottom line is we are seeing such investment because the AI opportunity, the way I like to think about it, is kind of the final frontier relative to technology. When you think about what could be beyond AI, it's really hard to imagine. Also, the fact that AI has really had very little penetration into large enterprises, health systems, or things around drug discovery means there's a long, long runway.
