So, it's natural to look at it and say, "Well, gravity's got to hit it sometime." But then, if you intellectually look at it and say, "My gosh, is AI going to be something that will change and transform how everything is done?" Of course. And then there's going to be investment to support that, and that's what we're seeing. Investment to support that. You mentioned Stargate, which immediately makes me think of Masayoshi Son. You were clear on the call yesterday that you've spoken to him and you don't think SoftBank is going to sell down its stake in ARM in any way. But how much is that an overhang in the longer term, and how much do you actually want to see more free float of your stock? I have one very large shareholder who I talk to quite frequently, who loves ARM and is very long on ARM. He has no intention to sell at all, and I shouldn't even say "anytime soon." I don't know when that would be, quite frankly. We do talk about it; he said he has no interest in selling. How that impacts the float, etcetera, is not something I candidly spend a whole lot of bandwidth thinking about. But he's very, very long on ARM because he sees the enormous opportunity that we have. If you think about what we've done since we went public in 2023, we told the world we'd be growing at 25% year-on-year for two, three years, and people didn't believe it was possible. Here we are now, a couple of years later, and we're beyond what we told investors during our roadshow, and things are even stronger than they were at that time. As a result, he sees it. He probably sees more of the data than anyone does, and that's why he's not selling. Renee, I want to go back to CPU. On January 26, Bloomberg spoke to NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang, and he spoke for the first time about selling a CPU as a standalone product to the market. That seems critically important because if you think about the pipeline for Blackwell and Rubin, those are GPUs coded in ARM-based architecture. And those building them don't want to have to cross-pollinate x86-based platforms with their ARM-based GPUs. It seemed to be really substantive. Could you explain how much of a boost that was for ARM and whether this idea that the Vera CPU goes into market as a standalone product changes the trajectory at all? Because at the same time, NVIDIA talks up how much content it owns proportionally in the overall system. Just take it and run with it, please. First off, I'm thrilled to see Jensen say that. They're a great partner, and Vera is a great product. As I mentioned earlier, Vera now has 88 CPU cores inside it, up from 72. So, why is that a really good thing for us going forward? Primarily, when you think about data centers and how they're evolving from general-purpose compute to AI compute—a mix of training and inference—and those inference workloads are agentic, that's right down the center of the plate for what the CPU not only is good at but can only do. This means that inside the data center, you're going to start to see movement towards a homogeneous type of structure where people would love to have the ARM stack running almost everything. It's just easier from a maintenance standpoint, easier from an upgrade standpoint, and easier from a cost standpoint, offering tremendous flexibility. You can build a tremendously efficient custom system based on ARM. The Vera Rubin platform, compared to Grace Blackwell, uses six times the number of CPUs when you look at the storage, the DPU, and the offload. That's a huge increase. Yes, selling loose Veras is a great thing. We're super happy to see that. Briefly, Renee, you have such a bird's-eye perspective because you're working with NVIDIA, but also with OpenAI. It was reported that OpenAI was potentially looking to work with your technology for its own custom chip with Broadcom. Meta is also looking at custom chips, of course. How do you see this evolving? I have a lucky job. I get to work with just about everybody in the industry across the planet, whether it's in the fab foundry area, a chip company, an OEM, or a software provider. We work and talk to everyone because ARM is just so pervasive. The common theme we see is continued investment in AI, and not just at the data center. It's about figuring out how to run those AI workloads everywhere: in wearables, making them more efficient in a smartphone or PC, or in physical AI, whether it's autonomous driving or robotics. So we're involved in all these conversations, and the common theme is heavy, heavy investment. One of the challenges we see in the chip world around this is that, as you know, it takes a couple of years to build a chip. The IP we develop that goes into chips takes maybe a couple of years before it's ready. Trying to predict what the architectures will look like four years after the chip has been designed, where the AI models are going, is very tricky. That lends itself very well to ARM, though, because we are programmable, flexible, and low-power. Meaning that wherever the chips drop, no pun intended, we should be in a very good position to be able to drive those workloads.

Silicon & AI | Jeetu Patel & Lip-Bu Tan, CEO, Intel

Cisco(417.0K) ·21:40 · 2026-02-05
Jeetu Patel Lip-Bu Tan
Lip-Bu Tan, CEO of Intel, outlines Intel's strategic turnaround, emphasizing its commitment to becoming a leading, general-purpose foundry and addressing critical AI infrastructure bottlenecks, while urging significant investment in US foundational r...
TL;DR Lip-Bu Tan, CEO of Intel, outlines Intel's strategic turnaround, emphasizing its commitment to becoming a leading, general-purpose foundry and addressing critical AI infrastructure bottlenecks, while urging significant investment in US foundational research and open-source...

TL;DR

Lip-Bu Tan, CEO of Intel, outlines Intel's strategic turnaround, emphasizing its commitment to becoming a leading, general-purpose foundry and addressing critical AI infrastructure bottlenecks, while urging significant investment in US foundational research and open-source development to counter escalating global competition.

Intel's Strategic Turnaround & Foundry Ambition

  • Renewed Leadership & Vision:
      • Lip-Bu Tan took on the CEO role (after two years on the board) recognizing Intel's importance as an "iconic company" and "national treasure" for the industry and the US.
      • He describes the journey as "marching off the map," reacting to real-time challenges.
  • Dual Business Model:
      • Intel operates as a complex company balancing two core areas: its foundry business and product business.
      • The intent is for the foundry to become a general-purpose service, serving external customers beyond Intel's own products.
  • Advanced Process Node Progress:
      • 18A Node: Yield improvement has reached 7-8% per month, considered "best practice," with external partners (PDF Solutions, KLA) assisting.
      • Customer Engagement: Several customers are now expressing interest in 18A, with 0.5 PDK available this month for test chips. Volume commitments are anticipated by the second half of the year.
      • 1.4nm Node (Intel 14A Equivalent): Laser-focused on this most advanced node, targeting risk production in 2028 and volume production in 2029.
  • Foundry Scaling & Trust:
      • Scaling requires a "business of grinding" mentality, earning customer trust by consistently meeting production demands, as their revenue and profit depend on it.
      • Intel aims to start with 5-10% of a customer's most important product and earn more business.
      • Strategic Importance: Emphasizes the critical need for US-based manufacturing foundries.

Addressing AI Infrastructure Bottlenecks

  • Memory Shortage:
      • Primary Constraint: Identified as the biggest challenge for AI, with "no relief" expected until 2028, largely due to high demand from products like NVIDIA's.
  • Compute Demand:
      • Rapid Growth: "Moore's Law" compute doubling is accelerating from 3-4 years to 3-4 months, with almost every CEO requesting more product.
      • Intel's Focus: Currently challenged to meet production and supply chain requirements.
      • CPU Utility: CPUs are highlighted as highly useful for general compute requirements in AI applications.
  • Thermal Management:
      • Cooling Evolution: Air cooling is no longer sufficient for high-performance processors (GPUs/CPUs).
      • Next-Gen Solutions: Liquid cooling, microfluidic cooling, and immersion cooling are becoming "very important" to prevent gigahertz throttling due to thermal issues.
  • Interconnect & Software Stack:
      • Interconnect Shift: Moving from copper to optical interconnects is critical for meeting speed and latency requirements in AI systems.
      • Full Stack Optimization: Addressing AI requires optimizing the entire stack—silicon, cooling, interconnect, and software—not just silicon.
      • Cluster Management: New software and startups are emerging to address complex cluster management issues for large GPU/CPU deployments, where tools like Kubernetes might lack specific diagnostic capabilities.

Geopolitical Landscape: Open Source & Innovation

  • Foundational Research Decline:
      • US Concern: A significant worry is the decline in fundamental research in US universities, with top professors being recruited to Asia and Europe.
      • Industry Limitations: Public companies have short-to-mid-term investment horizons, unable to fund the long-term, 10-20 year research needed.
  • Open Source Gap with China:
      • "Behind China": The US is "well behind China" in open-source development, with DeepSeek identified as a "wake-up call."
      • China's Capabilities: Despite limitations in advanced tools and GPUs, China is building strong, original capabilities, exemplified by Huawei's 100 top CPU architects. They compensate with infrastructure optimization and extensive engineering capacity.
  • Power & Regulatory Advantage:
      • Faster Approval: China's ability to quickly secure regulatory approvals for large power infrastructure projects gives them an advantage in deploying the massive power required for AI.
  • Open Source Funding Challenges:
      • Business Model: High training costs for open-source models make it difficult for US companies to sustain, unlike China's government-subsidized approach.
      • Call to Action: Encouraging open-source community rebuilding, funding top AI researchers, and establishing new institutes are crucial.

Future of Silicon & Enterprise AI Adoption

  • Intel's Diversified Product Strategy:
      • Beyond x86: Intel will build both CPUs and GPUs, not solely focused on x86, and embrace architectures like RISC-V.
      • Software-Driven Hardware: The focus is on "Software 2.0," where software defines and drives hardware optimization.
  • Advanced Materials & Packaging:
      • CMOS Limitations: CMOS technology is "running out of steam," necessitating exploration of new materials like glass, artificial diamond, and gallium nitride for improved performance and specialized applications.
      • Packaging Innovations: Advanced packaging, including system-on-wafer and new technologies, is critical for enabling AI drivers.
  • Advice for Enterprise AI Adoption:
      • Problem & Outcome Focus: CIOs and enterprises should start by defining the specific problems AI will solve and the desired outcomes.
      • Foundation First: Don't layer AI on legacy IT infrastructure; it requires foundational changes and a rethinking of existing systems.
      • Productivity & Measurable Impact: Enterprises must measure the productivity gains and revenue growth driven by AI investments to demonstrate value to their boards. Current global productivity growth is surprisingly low.
      • Augmentation Strategy: Adopt AI function by function to augment existing processes and roles.

"Intel is a national treasure... You are bringing it back again to life." — Jeetu Patel "My biggest challenge is focused on our production of supply chain. Make sure we can meet that requirement. Almost every CEO they call me up lipo. Can I have more?" — Lip-Bu Tan "We are quietly building on it. And so to me is they are just shortly behind us. You have a very unique record in my life. Do you know what that is? The speed at which I have developed a friendship with you. I don't think I've developed one with anyone else. It's not a function of me; I think it's a function of just how good you are at doing the kind of things that you do on a regular basis. Thank you for coming here. Well, thank you for inviting. Tell us a little bit about Intel. Intel is a national treasure. Intel has gone through some tough times. You are bringing it back to life, but there's so much that needs to get done. Walk us through the current state of Intel. Where do you think it is? Where do you think it should go? How should people think about it? First of all, I joined the board for two years, and then things started happening. Many good friends of mine from Intel, and it took me a while to decide to take this role. It wasn't easy; many of my friends told me not to do it. They said we have a great reputation in AI and that eventually the investment would be something I would love. But I took this on. I finally decided that this iconic company is very important for the industry and also for the United States. So I finally convinced my wife to let me do it one more time. Just to update, I've just passed ten months, coming up on eleven months. I kind of call it marching off the map, as some things somewhat surprised us. So you had to react in real-time and learn from it. In some way, I think it's good to have that update. We have a very complex company. We have a foundry that's strategically very important, and then there are the products. Clearly, you need to balance both. Do you believe your foundry business will become a general-purpose foundry that's outside of just your products? Yes, that's the intent. Our 18-nanometer process, when I took over, was quite poor. So I really got all my friends to help, from PDF Solutions to equipment suppliers, to ensure we had a 7-8% yield improvement per month. A 7-8% yield improvement per month would be best practice. It took me a while, but I'm now seeing 7% or 8% yield per month. It's very helpful that we opened up the kimono and let people come and help. For 18-nanometer, we just announced the Penta Link, and I can really count on my fab to produce that. Interestingly enough, a couple of customers are knocking on my door now saying, "Hey, it seems like your 18-nanometer is doing well. We want to be a part of that." We are delighted to see customers wanting to engage. We are laser-focused on the 14-nanometer, which is very important. This is the most advanced, and we're going to be in risk production by 2028 and volume production by 2029. Like any foundry, I've learned a lot. You need to have not only yield improvement but also variations so they're more predictable. You also need to have the final product and know what it looks like, including bit spin and all the details. Secondly, you need to have all the IP available. If you want multiple customers, you've got to have all the low-power IP to serve them. Something was missing in the past, so I really put things in place so we can have both and serve the customer. Good news is, a couple of customers are very excited. We will have the 0.5 PDK this month, and customers can use a test chip to work with us. So, the good news is a couple of customers are engaging heavily, and we are looking forward to serving them. Hopefully, in the second half of this year, I will see volume commitments from customers, detailing which products they want, and I can serve them. In the second half, you will see volume commitments on the foundry or on your core products? Customers need to let me know what products and what volume they need. Then I can proceed. One thing people will see, because first of all, I'm not going to announce any customer due to confidentiality; we need to support them. What we are going to do is when you see me starting to put money into substrates, which are a very important material. Secondly, some of the CapEx equipment I need to scale, meaning I have real customers I'm committed to. That's the kind of discipline I have. On the fab side, to get to mass scale, what does mass scale look like for you, and what's the timeframe you feel like mass scale could happen where you could be a formidable fab for third-party chips? It's very challenging to operate the two sides. One is about the product, driving innovation and the product roadmap. The other part is truly a service. The business culture had to change. In some ways, I tell my team this is the business of grinding. It's not like if you have good innovation and good technology, people will just come. You have to grind until you provide something they can call on you to make the production. Their whole revenue and profit depend on it. There's a lot of trust and responsibility, and we have to earn it. So, scaling is important. That's why I always ask customers to give me their biggest product, their most important product, and let me earn 5%, 10%, 20%, or 50% of it and earn their trust. Many of them I worked with when I was running Cadence, so they trust me. Trust is very important for AI, and even more important in foundry is customer trust. Now, if you think about the current constraints, do you think energy constraints—power, cooling, all of that—are more severe, or is it manufacturing capacity that limits growth first? In terms of AI, the biggest challenge for many of my customers is memory. There's no relief for memory, as far as I know. When I talk to the three key players, two of whom I speak to very frequently, they told me there's no relief until 2028. Why is that? Because this whole AI push is sucking up a lot of memory. As our mutual friend Jensen Huang would tell you, he clearly needs a lot of memory for his next-generation products. So, it's very important to have that memory; if anything slows down, it will be memory. Secondly, from the compute side, I was very happy to hear that customers are all crying for more products, and we haven't prepared production enough to meet their requirements. People need to realize that in many applications, CPU is actually more useful in terms of performance for all compute requirements. In fact, a friend of mine mentioned that Moore's Law used to double every three to four years, and now it's like three or four months. The increase in compute is so significant. Right now, my biggest challenge is focusing on our production supply chain to ensure we can meet that requirement. Almost every CEO calls me up, saying, "Can I have more? I'm your friend, your most important customer; I want more of that." So, it's encouraging for me to see that compute has become very important. Besides memory and compute, the other very important part is thermal management. We just talked about cooling technology. For some high-performance processors, either GPU or CPU, you sometimes have to throttle the gigahertz down due to thermal issues and power management. So, we need to find new ways of cooling, such as liquid cooling, microfluidic cooling, and immersion cooling. Air cooling is no longer sufficient. These new methods are becoming very important. Another aspect you mentioned that Cisco does very well is interconnect. It's becoming very critical. It used to be more about copper, like in our semiconductor and materials lab where I invest, but now it's moving to optical. Optical is becoming very critical; it's a new wave because speed and latency are very important. Another constraint is software. If you really want to address some of these Moore's Law challenges, you need to look at the whole full stack, not just the silicon. You need the cooling, the interconnect, and the software. I'm a strong believer in open source, and code compatibility is very important. Even more important now is the cluster of GPUs or CPUs. You become the cluster manager, handling the software. Sometimes you have problems and don't even know where to look. Now, there are a lot of new startups coming to me; this is an area we want to work on so we know exactly how to solve some of these cluster issues you're going to face. Kubernetes is great, but you can't find real issues there. So, being a venture capitalist and running as CEO of Intel gives me that kind of perspective on what new things are coming. Quantum computing is another big area around the corner. It's interesting to see that after addressing the urgent needs of AI, the next big wave involves physical layers, and then the next thing will be quantum. That's exciting. You talked about open source; tell us a little bit about your perspective on open source in the United States. How important is that? What needs to happen? Those are very good questions. I'm very passionate about education, involved with MIT, CMU, Berkeley, and others. One thing I worry a lot about is fundamental research. We're starting to not continue a lot of university research; the best professors are being poached by Asia and Europe. Fundamental research is an outcry to me. Public companies like Cisco and ourselves have short-term and mid-term requirements; you can't invest in projects ten or twenty years from now. The other part is open source. I'm a big fan of open source. Several knowledgeable people have told me that we are well behind China. That's something we have to pay attention to. DeepSeek is a wake-up call. Don't you think, to some degree, some of the models emerging in China are just pure distillations of the models that are here? So, at some point, if all they're doing is distilling models, they can't really get that much farther ahead, or do you think they'll actually figure out a way to start creating original models? That's a very good question. I initially thought they would fall behind because they don't have access to the most advanced GPUs from NVIDIA or the most advanced processors from many different players. But you'd be surprised; recently, when I tried to recruit some top talent in CPU architecture, I learned that Huawei has 100 top-notch CPU architects. I was shocked. When I asked them why they were going there, he said, "Even though we don't have access to the best tools like EDA tools from Cadence and Synopsys, we have a 'Pokemon way' to do it, and we can do it." I asked about the equipment they don't have, and he said, "Well, we are quietly building on it." So, to me, they are just shortly behind us, and if we are not careful, they could leapfrog ahead of us. What they've done is they've spent the effort on infrastructure optimization because they don't have the right chipsets. If they're at seven nanometer and we're at two nanometer, they're offsetting that with unlimited power and engineering capacity. That actually creates the caloric redirection towards ensuring they drive a certain level of infrastructure optimization. That's correct. One thing we are behind on is that the AI crowd needs a lot of power. If you look at us, regulatory approval just takes longer to get. In China, if they decide to have it, they can quickly get all the approvals and get it done. So, that's something we have to pay attention to. You're investing in companies. What do you see open source development changing materially in the US, or do you feel like it continues on the foundation model side where it's largely closed source and not open source models? That's a good question. Some companies call themselves "open source," but when they become successful, they become closed source, blocking it off. I think we have to continue encouraging open source; that's the best way to avoid repeating everyone's effort and to drive more successful improvement more quickly. But you almost need a business model where something else is able to go out and fund that. Because if you just go with open source, the training costs are so high that the economics don't work out. In China, it's different because the government subsidizes a lot of that. So, how do we handle that? What's the game theory on our side? What do we do? I know a couple of my friends are putting a lot of effort into rebuilding that open source community and even funding some of the best AI researchers to do that. They've even formed a new institute rather than just using universities to fund some of these programs, and I really encourage jumping on it because this is something badly needed. The same applies to semiconductors. I used to be the only person investing in ventures, and until this, everyone was knocking on my door asking, "What's the next big deal you're doing?" I'm glad to see all my brothers and sisters from the VC side starting to pour money into co-investing with me. We have to continue doing that. On the foundry side, it's very important to have a US manufacturing foundry. That's why I decided to come in. While it's a long-term business, we just have to really do that because the industry needs it, and the US needs it. Overall, it's not just the process but also advanced packaging that's becoming a bottleneck. That's something we have to continue to drive, like system-on-wafer packaging arrangements. That's why we are investing in some of that and new technology to drive packaging so we can help enable the AI revolution and truly take off. Is Intel going to build GPUs in the future? Yes. I just heard that the chief GPU architect is very good; I'm delighted he's joined me. It took some persuasion, and I told him that not just CPUs but GPUs are also very important for different application workloads. You have to really optimize. I'm not hung up on just x86; I also embrace RISC-V. You've got to be more flexible. The key thing is that it's more about the software layer; you have to drive from the software, meaning software 2.0 defines the software, and you drive the hardware. So, Intel is going to build CPUs, they're going to build GPUs, they're going to have their own foundry to build those CPUs and GPUs, and you're going to have a foundry that's going to be made at scale available to other manufacturers. We are exploring new materials. For example, glass is a very good insulator, so we are looking to use glass. Artificial diamond is another good material. You have to go through the different chemical tables and find out what new materials, like gallium nitride, are very good for RF and switch applications. It's interesting. So, we have to look at different new materials. We have to consider that CMOS is really running out of steam, so you need to look at some new materials. That's why it's very important for me to continue to have that curiosity—to start looking at these new things, thinking short-term, mid-term, and longer-term, and how you're going to make an impact on the industry. You have enterprise audiences here: CIOs, Chief Information Security Officers. There are probably 10 million people who will be watching this. What advice would you have for people on how they should be thinking about AI and how they should be thinking about scaled infrastructure build-out? What would you say to them? That's a very good question. We all get excited and also feel a lot of pressure to adopt AI within the enterprise. It's very important to think about what problem you're trying to solve and what outcome you want to look for. For example, at Intel, we have a very legacy IT infrastructure. I just recruited the best US CIO I could find and brought her on board. I've told her it's a good time to look at the foundation. What changes do we need to make? You can't just build on top of the old; the legacy isn't going to work. You have to really unwind that and embrace some of the new AI, function by function, to truly drive augmentations. More importantly, what are the processes that you can drive to success? A friend of mine, an MIT professor and MIT CEO advisory board member, just mentioned that if you look at all the AI studies, basically, the productivity of the global economy is growing at a very small percentage. So, we still have to adopt AI more broadly so we can truly see productivity go up. With AI agents and robots, productivity should be much higher than it's been. Compared to back in the 1800s, I was shocked to see the productivity curve he showed me. I thought that would be much higher. So, there's something we have to think about: What is the end outcome you want to have, and then go back to the foundation? How do we drive that? What is our self-accountability that enables us to truly drive productivity for the enterprise that you can measure and present to your board, saying, "By doing this, by investing in this new technology, I actually improved my productivity and revenue growth"? Intel is a national treasure, but so are you. Thank you for being here. Thank you for inviting.

"Everything has changed" - Gita Gopinath on the global economy in 2026

World Economic Forum(1.1M) ·28:05 ·11.1K views · 2026-02-05
Gita Gopinath
Despite stable global growth numbers in 2025, Harvard economist Gita Gopinath warns that the global economy has fundamentally shifted, moving away from a predictable order due to escalating tariffs, a breakdown of trust between major alliances, and an AI boom whose financial valuations carry significant systemic risk.
TL;DR Despite stable global growth numbers in 2025, Harvard economist Gita Gopinath warns that the global economy has fundamentally shifted, moving away from a predictable order due to escalating tariffs, a breakdown of trust between major alliances, and an AI boom whose...

TL;DR

Despite stable global growth numbers in 2025, Harvard economist Gita Gopinath warns that the global economy has fundamentally shifted, moving away from a predictable order due to escalating tariffs, a breakdown of trust between major alliances, and an AI boom whose financial valuations carry significant systemic risk. This profound uncertainty makes the economy far more fragile than headline figures suggest.

The Disconnect: Stable Growth Amidst Fundamental Change

  • Gita Gopinath's Core Message: The global economy has undergone fundamental changes, even though 2025 growth (3.2%) remained stable, masking underlying fragility.
  • Initial Tariff Impact: The US imposed some of its highest tariff rates in a century (actual paid rates around 14%, statutory 22-23%, up from 3%), yet anticipated severe economic consequences did not materialize.
  • Reasons for Mitigated Tariff Impact:
      • Paused Tariffs: Initial harsh tariffs were paused for negotiation, preventing an immediate shock.
      • Exemptions & Lag: Numerous exemptions for specific products and countries, alongside a grace period for goods already in transit, lowered the effective tariff burden.
  • Economic Offsets: Two key factors prevented a downturn:
      • AI Boom: Massive investment in Artificial Intelligence fueled a stock market surge, boosting wealth and consumption, particularly at higher income levels. Countries supplying AI inputs (e.g., Taiwan, South Korea) saw significant growth.
      • Fiscal Stimulus: Strong government spending in China, Germany (moving away from debt breaks), and the US (in 2026) provided crucial economic support.

Rupturing the Global Order & Rising Uncertainty

  • End of Predictable Trade: The world has moved away from a rules-based, predictable global trade order, introducing a pervasive level of uncertainty affecting businesses, especially smaller ones.
  • US-Europe Alliance Breakdown: A critical and unforeseen development is the "complete breakdown of trust" between the US and Europe, rupturing an alliance that was a cornerstone of the global economic order for 80 years.
      • Europe is now actively pursuing "strategic autonomy," seeking reduced reliance on the US for security and economic partnership, and forging new trade alliances (e.g., Mercosur).
  • Slow-Burn Effects: Similar to Brexit, the full economic consequences of these fundamental changes (e.g., on investment) are expected to unfold gradually over several years, rather than immediately.
  • Accelerated Geopolitical Risks: The current geopolitical landscape is "profound for at least our generation," marking a "once-in-a-century event" due to the breakdown of the global order, a significant departure from the post-Cold War peace dividend.

Key Risks to Global Stability

  • AI Bubble & Financial Fragility: The AI sector, a major growth driver, exhibits stretched valuations and significant potential for a correction.
      • Dot-Com Bust Comparison: A potential "dot-com-like bust" in AI could wipe out $35 trillion in wealth, far exceeding the original dot-com crash and representing a much larger share of current global GDP.
      • Profitability Concerns: Gopinath questions whether all AI companies can achieve the profits necessary to justify their current high valuations in a hyper-competitive market.
      • Financial Crisis Risk: Despite recent resilience, current easy financial conditions could be building towards a future financial crisis, potentially triggered by unexpected inflation forcing the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates, tightening financial conditions.
  • Debt Overhang: Almost all countries face record-high levels of government debt without clear paths for reduction, representing a major risk currently overshadowed by geopolitical tensions.
  • Central Bank Independence: The operational independence of central banks is crucial for maintaining low inflation and interest rates. Political pressure on institutions like the Federal Reserve poses a significant risk to this established principle.
  • Geopolitical Escalation: Threats of retaliation from the European Union (e.g., over US statements on Greenland), coupled with ongoing tensions in regions like Iran and Venezuela, risk amplifying trade wars and broader global instability.

Future Growth Drivers & Policy Outlook

  • AI's Productivity Promise: AI is expected to boost economic growth through productivity gains, though the exact timeline (a few years or longer) and magnitude (estimates range from 0.1% to 1.5% annual productivity growth) remain uncertain.
      • Job Market Concerns: Rapid AI transformation could lead to "jobless growth," a shrinking middle class, and potential social unrest if job displacement outpaces job creation.
  • Energy Transition: Investment in green energy (solar, wind, hydro) is a significant growth driver, especially for emerging and developing countries seeking energy independence, job creation, and solutions to local pollution.
  • China's Economic Challenges:
      • Projected Slowdown: China's growth is projected to slow from 5% (2025) to 4.5% (2026) and potentially 3.5% in the medium term.
      • Key Headwinds: Weak domestic consumption, an unresolved property sector crisis, an aging and shrinking population, and weak productivity growth are major challenges.
      • Required Shift: China must transition from an export-driven model to one focused on domestic demand, requiring stronger social safety nets and greater reliance on private enterprise.
  • Resurgence of Industrial Policy: Industrial policy is making a comeback globally, driven by national security concerns (e.g., chips, rare earths, defense) and reactions to existing policies in countries like China.
      • Risks: If executed poorly, industrial policy can lead to resource misallocation, stunted growth, increased national debt, and higher interest rates. Guardrails like targeted, temporary implementation are crucial.
  • 2026 Outlook: Gopinath expresses significant concern that 2026 has started with further rupturing of US-EU relations, indicating the world is "not correcting course" but rather "going off course." She hopes for de-escalation by global leaders.

Notable Quotes

"Don't be fooled. Everything has changed for the global economy. It hasn't shown up in the growth numbers for a bunch of reasons... But be aware that the world is in a very different place." – Gita Gopinath "The global economy is more fragile than headline numbers suggest." – Gita Gopinath "What's long lasting is the complete breakdown of trust between the US and the Europe. I mean that alliance was a critical part of the global economic order and the fact that that is being ruptured is very consequential." – Gita Gopinath "If you had a dot like bust, it would wipe out $35 trillion of wealth. That is many multiples of what happened during the com. It's a much bigger share of current world GDP than it was during the com. So it can be much more consequential." – Gita Gopinath
Everything has changed for the global economy. It hasn't shown up in the growth numbers for a bunch of reasons that I'm happy to get into. But be aware that the world is in a very different place. Welcome to Radio Davos, the podcast from the World Economic Forum that looks at the biggest challenges and how we might solve them. On this episode, we're looking at the state of the global economy and what to look out for in 2026. Find Radio Davos wherever you get podcasts: Spotify, Apple, YouTube, and all our podcasts are at w.chmpodcast from the World Economic Forum. This is Radio Davos. We're recording this video podcast at the annual meeting 2026 in Davos and are joined by one of the world's most distinguished economists. She's the former first deputy managing director and chief economist at the International Monetary Fund and she's now the Gregory and Anna Coffee Professor of Economics at Harvard University, Gita Gopinath. Gita, welcome to Radio Davos. It's a pleasure to join you, Robin. It's so great to have you here. You published an article in the Financial Times a few days ago, a few days before the Davos meeting started, and I'm going to quote you from it: "2025 was a year when everything changed yet somehow nothing did." What did you mean? 2025 was a year when we saw the US impose one of the highest tariff rates they'd had in over a century. They brought it down from extremely high levels, but still, the tariff rate was around 14%, much higher than at the start of the year where it was around 3%. There was also a lot of policy chaos, a lot of confusion about where the rates would settle. You would think that would have been very consequential for the global economy. But if you look at global growth, global growth for last year was 3.2%, pretty much exactly what was projected for growth in 2025, the year before when none of this was on the horizon. It's like nothing happened. The reason I wrote that piece was to basically make the point that, no, please be careful. Don't be fooled. Everything has changed for the global economy. It hasn't shown up in the growth numbers for a bunch of reasons that I'm happy to get into. But be aware that the world is in a very different place. Let's talk about the two things you mentioned then. Why didn't it have the impact that I think a lot of economists were expecting to happen? And then we'll look at what these fundamental changes are that you say. The first question then is, why did it not bring GDP growth down? There was a time last year when economists thought this could be highly consequential and even put the US economy in a recession. That was right after Liberation Day and the April 2nd tariffs. In that three-day window, markets reacted very strongly; it looked like something was going to break. But then the Trump administration basically put a pause on those tariffs and said they were opening up time for negotiation with countries. So, one important reason why things turned out better than expected was because the actual tariff rates were much lower than what was originally announced. If you look at the true tariffs being paid by firms as they're importing into the US, that's even lower than the statutory rate, which is around 22-23%. The actual tariff rate in terms of what they pay is 14%. Why is that? Why is there a discrepancy? There are a bunch of reasons for it. First, there have been a lot of exemptions provided to certain products and countries. Tariffs have been announced but then withdrawn, so the actual tariff rate is very different. Also, if your goods were on the sea when the tariff was imposed, you don't get slapped with the tariff. So, there were a lot of exemptions, and that played an important role. The economy did better than expected also because there were two important offsets to the world economy. One was AI. That was huge. There was a large amount of investment in AI. Stock markets boomed because of that, which was good for wealth and helped hold up consumption at the top end and encourage consumption at the top end of incomes. Countries that were supplying inputs into the AI boom also did really well. Exporters like Taiwan and South Korea saw their growth get a big burst because of AI. The second is fiscal policy, especially in China, where there was a lot of fiscal stimulus. Germany announced moving away from the debt break rule, and the effect of that is going to play out over this year and next year. The US economy also has a strong fiscal stimulus this particular year in 2026. All of that is providing support to an economy that's otherwise being pushed back by tariffs. So what has fundamentally changed then? I think you're saying there is still a risk to the downside for economic growth, but are there real fundamental changes that have happened last year that are going to hit us this year? The fundamental change that has happened is we've moved away from the rules-based, predictable global trade order. There is a level of uncertainty that wasn't there before. That uncertainty is affecting businesses, especially small businesses. It takes time for that effect to play out. What I'm concerned about is that last year, at least, there was no overall retaliation from all of the US's trading partners. China was the one country that retaliated; Europe did not. But we're starting this year with threats of retaliation from the European Union because of the US's statements on Greenland. That can amplify. This world, where there's so much uncertainty, makes it very hard to make investments and to do business. I like to think of what happened after Brexit. Right after Brexit, there were two years when investment continued to go up, and everybody thought this couldn't really be such a big deal. But then ultimately, investment slowed, and 10 years later, you look back and say this was really a negative effect on the UK economy. I'm not saying that's the exact effect that would be on the US because the US is a much larger economy than the UK. But still, all of these changes, not just in terms of international policy but also domestic policy, concerns about central bank independence, those have slower effects, but they do show up over time. It is an extraordinary time in terms of the speed of news breaking, where economists are going to change potentially their outlook for various economic indicators. Since you wrote that article in the Financial Times, Donald Trump announced more tariffs on Europe because of the Greenland thing. That happened between you writing that and coming here to Davos. We're recording this for anyone watching or listening on the day before Donald Trump gives his special address here in Davos, so who knows what might have happened in the interim. Anecdotally, you've been at the forefront of economic analysis for many years. Have you seen a time like this before? Is this a different scene? I was chief economist at the IMF when we had the pandemic, and that was something we hadn't seen in a century. So, that was a major shift. We couldn't be in the same room together; we had to be working remotely, so that was dramatic. Compared to that, this is somewhat less dramatic, but still, it is a once-in-a-century event with this breakdown of the global order, and we may just be at the beginning of all this happening. To your previous question about what is long-lasting, I think what's long-lasting is the complete breakdown of trust between the US and Europe. That alliance was a critical part of the global economic order, and the fact that it is being ruptured is very consequential. Maybe by the end of this Davos week and next week when this goes out, we'll know if things are being smoothed out in that relationship or if things are getting tough and there's more of a trade war. We just don't know right now. We don't know. But I do believe we are never going back to the time when Europe said they could rely completely on partnership with the US for their security and economic partnership. This has changed fundamentally. I think Europe is looking for strategic autonomy. They are figuring out how to rely less on payments dominated by US companies and, more generally, how to rely on their own internal economy and security. So, there's no going back on that regardless of how this week goes. Let's talk about economic growth. In your article, you said, and I quote, "The global economy is more fragile than headline numbers suggest." What are the main risks? You've already described the potential delayed impact of increased tariffs. Are there others? The sector that did incredibly well last year was artificial intelligence. That was a big boost to the global economy. I think that's a sector where there is still a lot of fragility. When I wrote the article, I wrote an article last year in The Economist around October where I said that if you had a dot-com-like bust, it would wipe out $35 trillion of wealth. That is many multiples of what happened during the dot-com era. It's a much bigger share of current world GDP than it was then, so it can be much more consequential. How does it compare to the 2008-2009 recession? That was a different crisis. In 2008-2009, you had a big financial crisis, but that affected banks; it was much deeper and came through the housing sector. It was different. This comparison is more like the dot-com era because it's all about tech and what could happen if you have a tech boom that ultimately doesn't deliver on its promise, especially in terms of profitability. So, I still think we're in a space where valuations are stretched. It is difficult for me to see how, in this hyper-competitive environment, all these companies could make the kinds of profits that justify their level of valuations we're seeing now. So, we could see corrections. That's not a statement about the technology; I think the technology is going to be great. But whether all these companies are going to make the size of profits that justify the valuations, I still have questions on that. So, I think that is a very important risk to keep in mind because financial conditions are actually quite easy everywhere in the world, though that keeps changing on a daily basis depending on news coming in. But that is a shoe that did not drop. We did not have a financial crisis despite five years of pandemic, Russia invading Ukraine, and interest rates going up very sharply. Could we be building up to a financial crisis? I am concerned about that. I bet people ask you every day: if there's a bubble, when is it going to burst? How do you answer that? There is no formula for predicting exactly when a bubble will burst; it could take some time. Usually, there's a trigger. One of the triggers, which is what happened last time right before the dot-com bust, was the Federal Reserve raised interest rates because they were worried about inflation. Could we have a situation this time where the Fed needs to raise interest rates because of tariffs, additional tariffs being put on that get passed through into consumer prices, because of the otherwise general high levels of demand in the US that we're seeing? So if there is surprise inflation and the Fed has to raise interest rates, which is not something markets are expecting, then you could see a sudden tightening in financial conditions, and that could prick a bubble. Let's talk about the Fed then. There is some pressure on it from the political side, from President Trump. How important is it? Is it an orthodox view that central banks should be independent of the political cycle so that they can set long-term policies that can contain inflation? What do you see? Is that a risk that independence might be eroded in the US and potentially elsewhere? I'd say there are two kinds of policies on which economists agree. One is that trade helps countries and people; yes, it may have some flaws and needs to be carefully managed, but in general, it is a positive. The second is central bank independence, which means that if you want to have low inflation in your country, which then will keep interest rates low, you need to have an independent central bank. To be clear, when we say independent central bank, that does not mean the central bank is not accountable to anybody. This is about operational independence, meaning that given the mandate that has come from the political class, from Congress in the case of the US, the actual execution of that mandate stays within an independent central bank. Let's go back to these risks to growth. You mentioned the potential of a tech bubble, or something like the dot-com bubble a couple of decades ago. There are geopolitical risks, aren't there? We've already mentioned Greenland, Iran, Venezuela, all places that America is potentially involved in. There are always geopolitical risks, but do you think we're at a particularly risky moment now? The geopolitical risks we're seeing right now are profound for at least our generation, if not for at least two generations. We had 80 years of a peace dividend, especially after 1990 and the end of the Cold War. Countries were coming together, with closer integration, peace, and growth. It was not perfect; it was absolutely the case that jobs were lost in some countries because of too much open trade. That was a risk that I think we all have to deal with now. But that said, what we're seeing now, especially with this shift in the relationship between the US and Europe, is something I don't think anybody predicted last year in Davos. There was this sense that we could see some decoupling between the West and China, but the fact that there would be a breaking up of the alliance of the West is not something we predicted. Does that have global implications, or is it just Europe needing to sort itself out? Are China, Russia, and the Global South looking at that relationship between the USA and Europe? It has huge global implications. If you look at Europe itself, Europe is now going and making trade relations with other countries, like the deal on work they signed recently. They've been working on it for many years, and though there's still been pushback from member states, they pushed it through. I think it's a statement that Europe believes it needs to have other friends and build those other relationships. Similarly, in their dealings with China, the global order is being shifted around who you work with; new alliances are being formed, however temporary they may be, but those shifts are happening. This is a very consequential relation between the US and Europe, and ruptures of the kind we're seeing will have big implications for everyone. Another risk that economists always flag up is debt, national debt, government debt being a big risk. Is that just being overshadowed? It's still there, right? If we didn't have this large scale of geopolitical tension and tariffs, that's the risk we would be talking about, which is debt. The fact that almost all countries in the world are sitting on record high levels of debt without a clear path on how that will be brought down is indeed a very important risk we are facing in addition to everything else. So, where can growth come from? You pointed out over the last year massive growth stimulated by investment in AI, which had knock-on effects around the world. You're worried, to whatever extent, that this might be a bubble. Are there other areas where we could achieve economic growth? I do think AI will help with economic growth. There are going to be productivity effects, whether it takes a couple of years or longer, we have to see. But I think that is an important source of growth because it's a general-purpose technology; it can transform all fields we're looking at. Of course, what we do need to keep our eye on is what's happening with jobs. Are we going to be creating growth that is jobless? That is a risk we need to worry about, and I am concerned that especially if this transformation happens very rapidly, there's a real risk of that happening. You could end up with a shrinking of the middle class, leading to civil conflict, people protesting. People will not stay quiet in the face of lots of people losing their jobs. How long do you think that will take? At this moment, given what we know, I think it's quite uncertain, the effect on productivity. We still have a fairly wide range of estimates, anywhere between adding 0.1% to productivity growth on a yearly basis to 1.5% of productivity growth. So, there is a big range there. Similarly, regarding the impact on jobs, there is a concern that young people coming on the job market are having a harder time finding jobs because of AI's ability to replace them. But at the same time, there are also young people who are going much more quickly into entrepreneurship because the technology helps them start new enterprises. So, we're still in the phase of figuring this out. The energy price and the oil price are very important when it comes to how the economy functions. Where do you see oil prices and energy prices in general going? Energy prices, oil especially, are going to be in the low 60s. That's the most likely outcome. The reason why Venezuela didn't have any big effect is because even though it has a large amount of supply, getting that out of the ground is going to take time. There's been such damage to their infrastructure that this is not going to happen overnight. Iran is about 1% of the oil market, so it is consequential, but it is not a huge effect on oil markets as such, and there's a lot of supply coming from other parts of the world. What about the energy transition? That was often touted, not least here in Davos, as a driver of growth: investment in new types of energy or different types of energy, green energy, energy storage. We're hearing less about that in the headlines right now. Is there still an energy transition going on, and is it somewhere that people should be investing in and looking for growth? There is an energy transition going on, and for several countries, especially emerging and developing countries that otherwise would import a lot of their energy, that is a source of growth for them. It's not just about sustainability but also about job creation and having to rely less on imported fuel. This is going to be an important source of growth for them. They're investing in solar, wind, hydro, all those different forms of sustainable energy. That's a direction in which that will continue. China is doing a lot; I think China is continuing to be on the forefront of the green transition. India is also paying attention, doing the investments that are needed. Pollution is a big challenge in India right now, so even if you are not so worried about sustainability from a global perspective, the fact that you have to get rid of the high levels of pollution itself will move you in the green direction, and weather events are highly consequential for the world, and that will remain the case. You mentioned China. I wonder if you could give us an overview of where you see China's economy going, because it's a very different type of economy to the places we've mostly been talking about until now, North America and Europe. China has relied quite a bit on export-led growth in these last two years because if you look at domestic consumption, that has been fairly weak, and they haven't solved that problem. In China, growth for 2025 is around 5%; it's projected to go down to 4.5%. They still have a problem with their property sector. There was overinvestment in that particular sector, and we still haven't seen reallocation of resources away from property to other markets efficiently. That is still a challenge. It's an aging population; the population shrank for the fourth year in a row—that's another headwind. Productivity growth in China has not been very strong. For all those reasons, if you look out into the medium term, we should expect China's growth to slow to around 3.5% or so. China cannot rely on export-driven growth for multiple reasons. One, it's just too large an economy to be able to grow just on the export side. Secondly, other countries are not going to take in such large amounts of goods; they will slap tariffs very quickly, and they would be justified to do that, even by WTO rules, because of anti-dumping reasons. So, China does need to move towards a growth strategy that is more domestic-demand driven. That's going to require stronger social safety nets for households in China so that people don't feel they have to save that much. And they have to invest in productivity, moving away from state-owned capital towards much more private enterprise. They've made a start in that. Do you see that continuing? They had made a big shift in that right after the great financial crisis, but then after the pandemic, they seem to have drifted sideways again, back into export-led growth. The most recent 15th plan they came up with still talks about investing in new sectors—frontier sectors like AI and frontier energy sectors, and so on—and not enough on the need to have high levels of consumption. So, I'm not sure they've made the switch. I think maybe silently, quietly, they recognize that they cannot have overproduction in every sector. That is a problem because you're going to end up worrying about prices going too low, which is a problem that China has and no other country does. So, they need to do more in terms of reorienting the economy inwards, relying more on consumption and local demand-driven growth as opposed to external growth. I don't see a big dramatic shift in the policies being made, so this is going to take time. I suspect they'll only move slowly. Industrial policy. This used to be seen as a thing of the past, certainly in the West, with governments getting involved in economies that many economists would say, 'let the market sort this out.' It's come back, hasn't it? Where do you see industrial policy going from here? Industrial policy has been around for a while, and I would say China did a lot of it for many years. It's not just in the form of providing a direct subsidy or a tax break to companies, but also in terms of cheap credit and cheap land that generated very large amounts of production capacity in many different sectors, including automobiles, electric vehicles, and also solar, and so on in the case of China. Now, other countries are also reacting partly to what happened in China, but also now there is a big concern, especially after Russia's invasion of Ukraine, about national security concerns. So, there is this need to encourage certain kinds of sectors at home, be it chips, rare earths, energy, or defense, to ensure that some level of national security is still maintained. So, I expect industrial policy is going to be here for a while. It is still important, though, to have guardrails because you could end up in a situation where governments are spending a whole lot of money which they just cannot afford given their level of debt. So, if you're going to do it, do it in a targeted manner on a temporary basis; that's going to be very important. What are the risks of it, though, if it's done badly? If it's done badly, firstly, you're not going to get any of the benefits of the industrial policy because you end up misallocating resources, and you don't see that in growth, and you don't see enough jobs being created. That would be one consequence. The second, of course, is if you end up spending a whole lot and the economy doesn't grow, then your debt as a ratio of GDP is going to go up even more, and how are you going to pay your bills? So, interest rates will go up, and that will just be bad for all other parts of the economy that are not benefiting from industrial policy. So we're sitting here in Davos, looking out to the rest of 2026. I'm going to ask you what you expect to happen in 2026. I'll just quote again from your article in the Financial Times: "The reality is that 2025 was a year when everything changed. The question now is whether 2026 will be the year we correct course." Do you think it will be? Unfortunately, 2026 has not started off that well with the US push for Greenland and the rupturing of relations further between the US and the European Union. This gives me great concern. So, we're certainly not correcting course; we seem to be going off course. But I want to remain hopeful. All the important leaders are here in Davos. They have an opportunity to come together, and I believe they will. So, my hope is that they will find a way of de-escalating because that is super important for every human being on this planet. By the time this goes out, they will have met. I'm sure there will be ongoing conversations. Gita Gopinath, thanks so much for joining us on Radio Davos. Thank you, Robin.
